Introduction to the Sixth Maqsad: The Simple Body is Indivisible
Al-Jurjani introduces the sixth section presenting the philosophers' doctrine on indivisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the central claim of the philosophers that simple bodies are not divisible and the context in which this claim is presented.

Core Idea
Philosophers hold that simple natural bodies cannot be physically divided.

Al-Jurjani now begins the sixth section of the first observatory, which examines the philosophers' view on the indivisibility of simple bodies. This lesson establishes the central claim that will be argued over the coming pages. The passage presents the heading and introduces the philosophers' argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Note the structure: a heading followed by a parenthetical explanation.
The passage is the opening of a new section.
2
Identify the central claim
Find the statement that expresses the philosophers' doctrine. The key phrase is 'الجسم البسيط لا يتجزأ' meaning 'the simple body is not divisible.'
3
Understand the context
Realize that this is the start of a longer argument. The phrase 'they said: since it has been established...' indicates that the philosophers will present a proof from earlier premises.
The full argument will be given in the following lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد السادس : الجسم البسيط لا يتجزأ ] ( في تحرير مذهب الحكماء ) في الأجسام البسيطة الطباع ( قالوا : لما تقرر ) .
The sixth aim: The simple body is not divisible. (In setting forth the doctrine of the philosophers) concerning simple bodies in nature, (they said: Since it has been established...).
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of the sixth section (al-maqsad al-sadis) of the first observatory. The heading states the topic: 'The simple body is not divisible.' The commentator then introduces the philosophical doctrine: the philosophers (al-hukama') hold that simple bodies (al-ajsam al-basita al-tiba') are not divisible. The phrase 'they said: since it has been established...' indicates that the philosophers provide a proof based on previous premises. However, the text cuts off, so the full argument is not present in this window. This lesson serves as the heading and opening of the section.

Key Terms
الجسم البسيط
لا يتجزأ
مذهب الحكماء
الأجسام البسيطة الطباع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers deny all forms of division, including theoretical divisibility.
Correction: The philosophers allow for theoretical divisibility but deny actual physical division in simple bodies.

Checkpoint
What is the claim of the philosophers in this section?
A) The simple body is divisible
B) The simple body is not divisible
C) All bodies are divisible
D) The simple body is a point

Key Takeaway
The sixth maqsad begins with the affirmation that simple bodies are indivisible, a key position of the philosophers.

Proving the Existence of Primary Hyle
Al-Iji presents the proof for hyle and form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical proof that a body is composed of a persisting substrate (primary hyle) distinct from its form or connection.

Core Idea
The body's ability to alternate between connection and separation proves it has a lasting underlying matter.

This passage presents the core argument for the existence of primary hyle, the substrate that persists through changes in the body's connectedness. Al-Iji uses the example of water poured from one jar into two to show that something must remain to receive both states. Understanding this proof is essential to grasp the Aristotelian notion of matter and form.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Follow the logical steps from the example to the conclusion.
Notice how the author uses the example of water to illustrate the concept.
2
Understand the key move
Identify why the substrate cannot be the connection itself. The argument uses the principle that a thing cannot be a substrate for itself or its opposite.
This is the crucial inference to establish hyle as distinct from form.
3
Grasp the analogy with quantities
The summary draws an analogy: just as changing quantities imply a persisting form, changing connections imply a persisting hyle. Reflect on how this reinforces the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد السابع : الجسم مركب من الهيولى والصورة ( دليلهم على إثبات الهيولى والصورة وكون الجسم مركباً منهما، قالوا: فالجسم البسيط (متصل) واحد في حد ذاته كما عرفت ( وهو قابل للانفصال ) الانفكاكي، كما إذا صب ماء الجرة في إنائين، فثمة اتصال) أي جوهر ممتد في الجهات متصل في نفسه ( نسميه الصورة الجسمية وندعي أنه) أي ذلك الجوهر المتصل ليس بتمام حقيقة الجسم بل ثمة أمر آخر يقوم به الاتصال أي الجوهر المتصل على معنى أنه يختص به اختصاصاً ناعتاً له فيكون حالاً فيه. وبيانه أن الجسم المتصل إذا طرأ عليه الانفصال زال اتصاله وصار منفصلاً وحينئذ نقول : فإن ثمة أمراً قابلاً للاتصال تارة والانفصال أخرى وذلك القابل لهما ليس نفس الاتصال ضرورة أن القابل الثابت للشيئين غير كل واحد من الشيئين المتزايلين، فالقابل للاتصال والانفصال يغاير كلا منهما أو نقول : قابل الاتصال والانفصال باق مع الانفصال والاتصال لا يبقى مع الانفصال فهو غيره أي قابل الاتصال والانفصال غير الاتصال، وكيف لا والشيء لا يكون قابلاً لنفسه ولا لما ينافيه، فهذا الأمر الذي هو قابل للانفصال ومغاير للاتصال هو الذي نسميه بالهيولى الأولى التي تحل فيها الصورة الجسمية، فإنه كان قبل طريان الانفصال متصفاً بالاتصال الواحد حيث كان متصلاً واحداً، وبعده متصفاً بانفصال بل باتصالين حادثين عنده حيث كان حينئذ متصلين. وتلخيصه أنهم كما أثبتوا بتوارد المقادير المختلفة على الجسم مع بقاء صورة جوهرية اتصالية قابلة للكميات المتواردة كون الكم المتغير غير الاتصال الباقي بحاله أثبتوا أيضاً بتوارد اتصالات مختلفة بالشخص على أمر باق. اعلم أن الهيولى على الإطلاق هي محل الصورة الجوهرية وهي أربعة أقسام: الهيولى الأولى: وهو جوهر غير جسم محل المتصل بذاته. والهيولى الثانية: هو جسم قام به صورة كالأجسام بالنسبة إلى صورها النوعية. والهيولى الثالثة: وهي الأجسام مع صورها النوعية التي صارت محلاً لصورة أخرى كالخشب لصورة السرير والطين لصورة الكوز. والهيولى الرابعة: وهي أن يكون الجسم مع الصورتين محلاً لصورة أخرى كالأعضاء لصورة البدن وأجزاء البيت لصورته. فالهيولى الأولى جزء الجسم هو جزء والثانية نفس الجسم والأخيران جزءاً لهما.
The seventh goal: The body is composed of hyle and form. (Their proof for establishing hyle and form and that the body is composed of them: They said: The simple body is a single connected thing in itself, as you know, and it is capable of actual separation, as when the water of a jar is poured into two vessels. So there is a connection, i.e., a substance extended in directions, connected in itself, which we call the bodily form, and we claim that this connected substance is not the entire reality of the body, but rather there is another thing by which the connection occurs, i.e., the connected substance in the sense that it is characterized by it as an attribute, so it is a state in it. Its explanation is that if separation occurs to the connected body, its connection ceases and it becomes disconnected. Then we say: There is something that is capable of connection sometimes and separation at other times, and that which is capable of both is not the connection itself, necessarily, because the persistent substrate of the two things is other than each of the two things that alternate. So the substrate of connection and separation is different from each of them. Or we say: The substrate of connection and separation persists with separation, while connection does not persist with separation, so it is other than it; i.e., the substrate of connection and separation is other than connection. How could it not be? A thing cannot be a substrate for itself nor for what contradicts it. So this thing, which is capable of separation and different from connection, is what we call the primary hyle, in which the bodily form inheres. For before the occurrence of separation it was characterized by a single connection, being one connected thing, and after it, it is characterized by separation, rather by two connections occurring in it, being then two connected things. Its summary is that just as they established, by the succession of different quantities on the body while a substantial connected form persists that is capable of the succeeding quantities, that the changing quantity is other than the connection that remains in its state, so they also established, by the succession of different connections in individual identity on a persisting thing, that... Know that hyle in an absolute sense is the substrate of substantial form, and it is of four kinds: The first hyle: it is a substance, not a body, which is the substrate of that which is connected by itself. The second hyle: it is a body in which a form subsists, like bodies in relation to their specific forms. The third hyle: it is bodies together with their specific forms that have become a substrate for another form, like wood for the form of a bed and clay for the form of a jug. The fourth hyle: it is when the body with the two forms becomes a substrate for another form, like body parts for the form of the body and parts of a house for its form. So the first hyle is a part of the body, the second is the body itself, and the last two are not parts of them.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage provides the proof for the composition of the body from hyle (matter) and form (sura). It begins by stating the claim: the simple body is one connected thing in itself but is capable of actual separation. The example is water poured from a jar into two vessels – the body goes from being one connected substance to two separate connected substances. The argument then proceeds: when separation occurs, the original connection is lost and a new disconnection or new connections appear. Therefore, there must be something that persists through this change and is capable of both connection and separation. This substrate cannot be the connection itself, because the connection disappears when separation occurs. The substrate must be something else, and that is what the philosophers call 'primary hyle' (al-hayula al-ula), upon which the bodily form (al-sura al-jismiyya) inheres. The author then summarizes the proof by analogy: just as the succession of different quantities on a body with a persisting substantial form shows that the quantity is different from the form, so too the succession of different connections on a persisting thing shows that the hyle is different from the connection. The passage then defines hyle absolutely as the substrate of substantial form and lists four kinds: (1) primary hyle – a non-bodily substance that is the substrate of the self-connected; (2) secondary hyle – a body with a form (like a natural body); (3) tertiary hyle – bodies with specific forms that become substrates for another form (e.g., wood for a bed); (4) quaternary hyle – a body with two forms that becomes a substrate for yet another form (e.g., body parts for the whole body). The first hyle is a part of the body, the second is the body itself, and the last two are not parts of them. This classification shows the different levels at which 'hyle' is used.

Key Terms
هيولى
صورة
جسم
اتصال
انفصال
هيولى أولى
جوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the body is purely simple and has no underlying matter; it is just a single connected substance.
Correction: The proof shows that the body can change from connection to separation, so there must be a persisting substrate that is not the connection itself. This substrate is primary hyle.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument for the existence of primary hyle?
A) The body is capable of both connection and separation, so there must be a persisting substrate other than the connection.
B) The body is composite by observation, so it must have parts.
C) The Qur'an states that everything is made from matter.
D) Hyle is defined as the substrate of accidents.

Key Takeaway
The body's ability to switch between connection and separation proves the existence of a distinct, persistent substrate called primary hyle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that your body is more than what you see; there is a hidden, persisting principle. In what areas of your life do you mistake temporary attributes (like health, status, relationships) for your lasting, God-given reality? What would change if you truly lived aware of the hyle-like core that remains when those attributes shift?
?
Connection and separation are accidents that come and go. What constant 'you' survives the daily 'separations'—losses, changes, and trials? How can you anchor your identity in that constant, rather than in the fleeting forms?
?
The proof relies on observing change to deduce a persisting substrate. When you look at your own spiritual state—moments of closeness to Allah followed by distance—what is the underlying constant that allows you to return? How does this lesson reveal that your connection to Allah is not inherent to your nature but a gift that requires conscious preservation?

The Four Types of Hyle
Al-Iji defines hyle in general and lists its four categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four types of hyle as classified by Al-Iji and their roles in composition.

Core Idea
Hyle is not a single thing; it has four levels from pure potentiality to complex composites.

After establishing the necessity of primary hyle, Al-Iji clarifies the meaning of 'hyle' by listing its four forms. This classification shows how the same term applies at different levels of composition, from the non-bodily substrate to the body with multiple forms. Understanding these levels helps to avoid confusion about what 'matter' means in different contexts.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification
Read the Arabic passage listing the four kinds of hyle. Note how each is defined and what example is given.
The classification begins with 'اعلم أن الهيولى على الإطلاق...'
2
Understand each type
For each type, identify its definition: first hyle is non-bodily substance, second is body with form, third is body with form as substrate for another form, fourth is body with two forms as substrate for another.
Pay attention to the examples: wood for a bed, clay for a jug, body parts for the whole body.
3
Analyze the concluding remark
The author notes which types are parts of the body: first hyle is a part, second is the body itself, and the last two are not parts. Reflect on why this distinction matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد السابع : الجسم مركب من الهيولى والصورة ( دليلهم على إثبات الهيولى والصورة وكون الجسم مركباً منهما، قالوا: فالجسم البسيط (متصل) واحد في حد ذاته كما عرفت ( وهو قابل للانفصال ) الانفكاكي، كما إذا صب ماء الجرة في إنائين، فثمة اتصال) أي جوهر ممتد في الجهات متصل في نفسه ( نسميه الصورة الجسمية وندعي أنه) أي ذلك الجوهر المتصل ليس بتمام حقيقة الجسم بل ثمة أمر آخر يقوم به الاتصال أي الجوهر المتصل على معنى أنه يختص به اختصاصاً ناعتاً له فيكون حالاً فيه. وبيانه أن الجسم المتصل إذا طرأ عليه الانفصال زال اتصاله وصار منفصلاً وحينئذ نقول : فإن ثمة أمراً قابلاً للاتصال تارة والانفصال أخرى وذلك القابل لهما ليس نفس الاتصال ضرورة أن القابل الثابت للشيئين غير كل واحد من الشيئين المتزايلين، فالقابل للاتصال والانفصال يغاير كلا منهما أو نقول : قابل الاتصال والانفصال باق مع الانفصال والاتصال لا يبقى مع الانفصال فهو غيره أي قابل الاتصال والانفصال غير الاتصال، وكيف لا والشيء لا يكون قابلاً لنفسه ولا لما ينافيه، فهذا الأمر الذي هو قابل للانفصال ومغاير للاتصال هو الذي نسميه بالهيولى الأولى التي تحل فيها الصورة الجسمية، فإنه كان قبل طريان الانفصال متصفاً بالاتصال الواحد حيث كان متصلاً واحداً، وبعده متصفاً بانفصال بل باتصالين حادثين عنده حيث كان حينئذ متصلين. وتلخيصه أنهم كما أثبتوا بتوارد المقادير المختلفة على الجسم مع بقاء صورة جوهرية اتصالية قابلة للكميات المتواردة كون الكم المتغير غير الاتصال الباقي بحاله أثبتوا أيضاً بتوارد اتصالات مختلفة بالشخص على أمر باق. اعلم أن الهيولى على الإطلاق هي محل الصورة الجوهرية وهي أربعة أقسام: الهيولى الأولى: وهو جوهر غير جسم محل المتصل بذاته. والهيولى الثانية: هو جسم قام به صورة كالأجسام بالنسبة إلى صورها النوعية. والهيولى الثالثة: وهي الأجسام مع صورها النوعية التي صارت محلاً لصورة أخرى كالخشب لصورة السرير والطين لصورة الكوز. والهيولى الرابعة: وهي أن يكون الجسم مع الصورتين محلاً لصورة أخرى كالأعضاء لصورة البدن وأجزاء البيت لصورته. فالهيولى الأولى جزء الجسم هو جزء والثانية نفس الجسم والأخيران جزءاً لهما.
The seventh goal: The body is composed of hyle and form. (Their proof for establishing hyle and form and that the body is composed of them: They said: The simple body is a single connected thing in itself, as you know, and it is capable of actual separation, as when the water of a jar is poured into two vessels. So there is a connection, i.e., a substance extended in directions, connected in itself, which we call the bodily form, and we claim that this connected substance is not the entire reality of the body, but rather there is another thing by which the connection occurs, i.e., the connected substance in the sense that it is characterized by it as an attribute, so it is a state in it. Its explanation is that if separation occurs to the connected body, its connection ceases and it becomes disconnected. Then we say: There is something that is capable of connection sometimes and separation at other times, and that which is capable of both is not the connection itself, necessarily, because the persistent substrate of the two things is other than each of the two things that alternate. So the substrate of connection and separation is different from each of them. Or we say: The substrate of connection and separation persists with separation, while connection does not persist with separation, so it is other than it; i.e., the substrate of connection and separation is other than connection. How could it not be? A thing cannot be a substrate for itself nor for what contradicts it. So this thing, which is capable of separation and different from connection, is what we call the primary hyle, in which the bodily form inheres. For before the occurrence of separation it was characterized by a single connection, being one connected thing, and after it, it is characterized by separation, rather by two connections occurring in it, being then two connected things. Its summary is that just as they established, by the succession of different quantities on the body while a substantial connected form persists that is capable of the succeeding quantities, that the changing quantity is other than the connection that remains in its state, so they also established, by the succession of different connections in individual identity on a persisting thing, that... Know that hyle in an absolute sense is the substrate of substantial form, and it is of four kinds: The first hyle: it is a substance, not a body, which is the substrate of that which is connected by itself. The second hyle: it is a body in which a form subsists, like bodies in relation to their specific forms. The third hyle: it is bodies together with their specific forms that have become a substrate for another form, like wood for the form of a bed and clay for the form of a jug. The fourth hyle: it is when the body with the two forms becomes a substrate for another form, like body parts for the form of the body and parts of a house for its form. So the first hyle is a part of the body, the second is the body itself, and the last two are not parts of them.

Commentary
This passage provides the proof for the composition of the body from hyle (matter) and form (sura). It begins by stating the claim: the simple body is one connected thing in itself but is capable of actual separation. The example is water poured from a jar into two vessels – the body goes from being one connected substance to two separate connected substances. The argument then proceeds: when separation occurs, the original connection is lost and a new disconnection or new connections appear. Therefore, there must be something that persists through this change and is capable of both connection and separation. This substrate cannot be the connection itself, because the connection disappears when separation occurs. The substrate must be something else, and that is what the philosophers call 'primary hyle' (al-hayula al-ula), upon which the bodily form (al-sura al-jismiyya) inheres. The author then summarizes the proof by analogy: just as the succession of different quantities on a body with a persisting substantial form shows that the quantity is different from the form, so too the succession of different connections on a persisting thing shows that the hyle is different from the connection. The passage then defines hyle absolutely as the substrate of substantial form and lists four kinds: (1) primary hyle – a non-bodily substance that is the substrate of the self-connected; (2) secondary hyle – a body with a form (like a natural body); (3) tertiary hyle – bodies with specific forms that become substrates for another form (e.g., wood for a bed); (4) quaternary hyle – a body with two forms that becomes a substrate for yet another form (e.g., body parts for the whole body). The first hyle is a part of the body, the second is the body itself, and the last two are not parts of them. This classification shows the different levels at which 'hyle' is used.

Checkpoint
Which type of hyle is considered the body itself?
A) First hyle
B) Second hyle

Key Takeaway
Hyle is classified into four types: primary matter, natural bodies, bodies as matter for further forms, and bodies with two forms as matter for yet another form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The first hyle is described as 'not a body' yet it is the foundation of everything physical. What non-physical foundation is your life built upon? Are you investing in something that will last beyond the physical, or are you only building with 'second hyle'—the body and its forms?
?
The passage shows that even a formed body (second hyle) can become matter for a higher form (third and fourth hyle). In your own life, what is the 'higher form' you are allowing your daily activities and habits to serve as matter for? Or have you settled for being merely a 'second hyle'—a natural body with no further purpose?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
5 min
Proof and Objection: Ibn Sina's Hyle Argument
Ibn Sina's proof of hyle with dialectical objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core of Ibn Sina's proof of hyle based on similarity of parts and the dialectical objection that undermines its demonstrative force.

Core Idea
Without essential similarity of parts, the proof of hyle collapses from demonstrative to dialectical.

Ibn Sina's argument for hyle (prime matter) is a cornerstone of Aristotelian physics, but it hinges on a critical premise: that the elementary parts of bodies are identical in essence. This lesson presents the proof and then a sharp objection that challenges that very premise, forcing us to reconsider whether the argument is truly demonstrative or merely dialectical.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the logical flow: a conditional statement, then a conclusion, then a premise, then an objection.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Unpack the proof's logic
Identify the conditional: ‘if it were necessary for the essence, then the species would be limited to one individual’. Understand why this is impossible, leading to the conclusion that the attribute is separable, and thus hyle is proven.
The proof relies on the parts being identical in essence.
3
Grasp the objection
The objection denies the premise of essential similarity: parts could differ in essence. Even if water appears uniform to the senses, that doesn't guarantee metaphysical similarity. This undermines the proof.
The objection targets the foundation of the argument.
4
See the consequence: dialectical vs demonstrative
If the proof depends on the opponent's concession that parts are similar, it is merely dialectical (jadalī), not demonstrative (burhānī). Understand this distinction.
This shows the importance of premises in philosophical argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يكون لازماً لماهيته وإلا انحصر نوعه في شخصه وإذا لم يكن لازماً فيمكن مفارقته وعند فرض زواله يكون قابلاً للانفصال والاتصال بالفعل ويحصل المطلوب الذي هو إثبات الهيولى ومبناه أي مبنى ما ذكره ابن سينا كون الأجزاء التي هي تلك الأجسام الصغار متوافقة في الماهية كما أشرنا إليه وهو ممنوع لجواز أن تكون متخالفة في الماهية بحيث لا يوجد فيها جزءان متوافقان في النوع واستبعاد تركب الماء المتشابه في الحس من أجزاء متخالفة الحقائق بأسرها مما لا يجدي في أمثال هذه المباحث وإن بني الدليل على تسليم الخصم كان جدلياً لا برهانياً.
It would not be necessary for its essence, otherwise its species would be confined to its individual. And when it is not necessary, then its separation is possible, and upon assuming its removal, it becomes receptive to disconnection and connection actually, and the desired goal is achieved, which is the proof of hyle. And its foundation—that is, the foundation of what Ibn Sina mentioned—is that the parts which are those small bodies are identical in essence, as we pointed out. But this is denied because it is possible that they differ in essence such that there are no two parts identical in species, and the improbability of water, which appears similar to the senses, being composed of parts with entirely different realities is of no avail in such investigations. And if the proof is built on the opponent's concession, it is dialectical, not demonstrative.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage presents the core of Ibn Sina's proof for hyle and then raises a fundamental objection. It begins by stating a conditional: if a certain attribute (here, the ability to receive connection and disconnection) were necessary for the essence, then the species would be limited to a single individual—an impossibility. Therefore, it is not necessary, so it can be separated, and upon removal, the body becomes actually capable of disconnection and connection, proving the existence of a substrate (hyle). The foundation of this proof is that all parts (the small bodies) are identical in essence. The objection immediately challenges this premise: it is possible that the parts differ in essence such that no two parts are the same in species. The objection dismisses the intuitive similarity of water as irrelevant in such technical discourses. It then notes that if the proof relies on the opponent's admission that the parts are similar, it becomes merely dialectical (jadalī) rather than demonstrative (burhānī).

Key Terms
الهيولى (hyle)
التماثل في الماهية (similarity in essence)
جدلي (dialectical)
برهاني (demonstrative)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Ibn Sina's proof is sound regardless of whether parts are similar in essence.
Correction: The proof's demonstrative force depends on the premise that parts are identical in essence. Without it, the argument is only dialectical, as the objection shows.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection to Ibn Sina's proof of hyle as presented here?
A) The proof relies on an impossibility.
B) The parts may differ in essence, making the proof dialectical.
C) The proof does not account for the role of form.
D) The proof assumes that connection and disconnection are accidents.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's proof of hyle is only as strong as the premise that all parts are identical in essence; without that, it is merely dialectical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief about Allah or His creation based on something that merely appeared consistent—like the uniformity of water—without examining whether it is truly necessary in essence? How can you move from dialectical certainty to demonstrative certainty in your own faith?
?
When you argue for Islamic beliefs, do you rely on premises that are merely conceded by others (dialectical) or on irrefutable proofs? What would change in your da'wah if you insisted on demonstrative proofs?
?
This passage shows that even a great mind like Ibn Sina can build a proof on an unverified premise. Are there any unexamined premises in your own theology that might collapse if challenged? Will you investigate them or ignore them?

Objection that Connection and Disconnection are Accidents and the Reply
An objection and reply that reframes the debate about composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how an objection against the proof of hyle is turned into an advantage by showing that even the opponent's denial implies a receptive substrate.

Core Idea
Denying connection as a part still admits a substrate for connection.

This passage presents a key objection to the hyle (matter) argument: if connection and disconnection are mere accidents, how can they be parts of the body? The reply cleverly shows that denying connection as a part only confirms the existence of a substrate. This lesson unpacks the logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall argument flow.
2
Identify the objector's claim
The objector says connection and disconnection are accidents, not parts. Note how they define them as unity and multiplicity.
3
Understand the reply's move
The reply points out that the objector's denial of connection as a part tacitly admits something else receptive to it.
4
See how the dispute shifts
The new question becomes: is the body just the substrate or the substrate plus connection?
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this turns the objection into support for the hyle theorist's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما يعترض على برهان الهيولى ويقال الاتصال هو الوحدة والانفصال هو الكثرة وهما عارضان للجسم خارجان عنه فعليكم ببيان كون الاتصال جزءاً من الجسم حتى يثبت تركيبه من الاتصال والأمر القابل له فأنا من وراء المنع أي نمنع كونه جزءاً منه وهذا الذي يقال فيه التزام لثبوت أمر غير الاتصال قابل له وللانفصال أيضاً ويصير النزاع حينئذ في كون الجسم ذلك القابل وحده أو هو مع هذا الاتصال.
And perhaps an objection may be raised against the proof of hyle, and it is said: connection is unity and disconnection is multiplicity, and both are accidents of the body, external to it. So you must explain how connection is a part of the body so that its composition from connection and the thing receptive to it is proven. For we are beyond denying—that is, we deny it being a part of it. And this which is said implies an admission of the existence of something other than connection that is receptive to it and also to disconnection. Then the dispute becomes, at that point, whether the body is that receptive thing alone or that thing together with this connection.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and an implied reply. The objector argues that connection (ittisāl) is nothing but unity, and disconnection (infisāl) is multiplicity—both are accidents (ʿāriḍān) that befall the body, not essential parts. Therefore, the proponent of hyle must demonstrate that connection is actually a part of the body, so that the body can be said to be composed of connection and a receptive substrate. The objector simply denies that connection is a part. The reply, framed as 'this which is said implies an admission', points out that the objector's own statement 'I deny it being a part' tacitly acknowledges that there is something else (namely, the substrate) that is receptive to both connection and disconnection. Consequently, the real dispute shifts to whether the body is just that receptive substrate or that substrate together with this connection. This turns the objection into an advantage for the hyle theorist.

Key Terms
الاتصال (connection)
الانفصال (disconnection)
عارض (accident)
القابل (receptive substrate)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that connection and disconnection are mere accidents with no substrate, and that denying them as parts ends the debate.
Correction: The reply shows that even the opponent's denial implies a substrate, shifting the dispute rather than ending it.

Checkpoint
What does the objector claim about connection and disconnection?
A) They are essential parts of the body.
B) They are accidents, not parts.
C) They are the same as hyle.
D) They are impossible.

Key Takeaway
The objector's denial of connection as a part inadvertently confirms the need for a receptive substrate, shifting the debate in favor of hyle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reject a truth about Allah, do you ever notice that your rejection itself relies on assumptions that only make sense within that truth? How does this passage expose the subtle dishonesty in your own resistance?
?
In what area of your faith have you been denying God's complete control, yet your daily actions implicitly acknowledge His power? What step will you take today to fully submit that area to Him?
?
The objector's denial inadvertently admitted the substrate. What truth about yourself or your Lord have you been denying, and how might your very denial be confirming what you try to escape?

Clarification that Connection Means Extended Substance
The passage explains that the connection in question is the substance extended in dimensions, not an accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the dispute over the composition of the body reduces to a verbal disagreement once the extended substance and matter are properly distinguished.

Core Idea
The extended substance is self-evident; only the matter needs proof.

This passage reframes a central debate in Islamic metaphysics about the composition of bodies. The author cuts through confusion by identifying what is known necessarily and what requires demonstration. Once we distinguish the extended substance (the connective form) from the matter that receives it, the entire controversy dissolves into a mere verbal dispute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage silently, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own argument flow before you analyze it.
Notice the strong opening phrase 'there is no doubt'—the author presents this as settled.
2
Identify the two metaphysical entities
Find the two key terms: the extended substance (connective form) and the matter. Note which one is said to be known necessarily and which requires proof.
The extended substance is the first thing perceived of a body's substantiality.
3
Understand the resolution to verbal dispute
Once the existence of matter is admitted, and we accept that one is receptive and the other received, the question 'what is body?' becomes a matter of definition, not reality.
This shows the dispute is over words, not metaphysical facts.
4
Reflect on the implications for kalam
Consider how this clarification simplifies the debate about the physical world in Islamic theology. It shows that core metaphysical realities can be non-controversial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا شك أن الصورة الاتصالية أي الجوهر الممتد في الجهات الذي تبين بنفي الجزء اتصاله في نفسه أول ما يدرك من جوهرية الجسم أي حقيقته بل هو الجسم في بادئ الرأي المعلوم وجوده بالضرورة والذي يحتاج إلى الإثبات بالدليل هو المادة المتصفة بذلك الجوهر المتصل فإذا سلم ثبوتها وأن أحدهما قابل والآخر مقبول فيصير النزاع في أن الجسم ماذا نزاعاً لفظياً لا فائدة فيه.
And there is no doubt that the connective form—that is, the substance extended in the dimensions, whose connection in itself is clarified by the denial of the part—is the first thing perceived of the substantiality of the body, i.e., its reality. Rather, it is the body in the first view, known to exist necessarily. And what needs to be proven by evidence is the matter qualified by that extended substance. So when its existence is admitted, and that one is receptive and the other is received, then the dispute about what the body is becomes a verbal dispute with no benefit.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage clarifies the true meaning of 'connection' (ittisāl) in the debate about the composition of the body. The author asserts that there is no doubt that the connective form (al-ṣūra al-ittiṣāliyya) is precisely the substance extended in the dimensions (al-jawhar al-mumtadd fī al-jihāt). This substance's internal connection is established by denying that it has a part (i.e., it is indivisible as a substance). This extended substance is the first thing we perceive when we grasp the substantiality of the body—indeed, it is the body in the initial impression, and its existence is known necessarily. What actually requires proof is the matter (mādda) that is qualified by this extended substance. Once the existence of that matter is conceded, and it is understood that one (the matter) is the receptive substrate and the other (the extended substance) is the received form, then the entire dispute about what the body is becomes merely verbal (lafẓī) and fruitless. The point is that both sides agree on the existence of something receptive and something received; the only question is whether we call the whole body the receptacle or the composite. This is a semantic disagreement.

Key Terms
الصورة الاتصالية (connective form)
الجوهر الممتد (extended substance)
المادة (matter)
نزاع لفظي (verbal dispute)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the dispute about the composition of the body is a real metaphysical disagreement about whether the body is composed of matter and form.
Correction: The passage shows that once the existence of matter is conceded, the disagreement becomes verbal—both sides agree on the realities but label the whole differently.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is known necessarily and does not require proof?
A) The matter (mādda)
B) The extended substance (al-jawhar al-mumtadd)
C) The composition of body from hyle and form
D) The existence of parts

Key Takeaway
The connection in the body is the self-evident extended substance, and the matter it inheres in is the only thing requiring proof; the rest is semantics.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you argue about religious or theological matters, how often do you stop to ask whether the disagreement is about real substance or merely about labels? What would change in your conversations if you applied this lesson's insight to your own debates?
?
You have just learned that the extended substance is self-evident and only the matter needs proof. What is something in your own faith or worldview that you accept as self-evident but have never actually examined? Will you examine it now, or remain content with what 'everyone knows'?
?
The author says that once the existence of the matter is admitted, the dispute becomes verbal. Is there a belief or practice you hold onto simply because you have invested in defending a label, rather than because the underlying reality matters to Allah? What would honesty cost you?

The Fifth Proof for Substantial Forms
Iji presents the fifth proof for substantial forms based on differing properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth proof for substantial forms (الصورة النوعية) and how it demonstrates that differences in bodily properties require an intrinsic substantial principle beyond common matter.

Core Idea
Bodies differ in properties, so they must have distinct substantial forms.

Iji presents a sophisticated argument that goes beyond mere observation: he shows that the diversity of physical properties in bodies cannot be explained by what is common (like matter or corporeality) but must come from a specific substantial form inherent in each body. This proof is foundational for understanding how the physical world is structured and how it points to deeper metaphysical realities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the parallel structure: 'just as hyle... so it is never devoid of another form.'
2
Identify the key observation
Iji points out that bodies differ in their necessary properties like splitting, conglomeration, and shaping. Some do so easily, some with difficulty, some not at all.
This observation is the foundation of the proof.
3
Understand the elimination of common causes
Iji eliminates three possible common causes: common corporeality (الجسمية المشتركة), hyle (الهيولى), and the separate intellect (المفارق). Learn why each cannot account for the differences.
The key principle: 'different things cannot be caused by a single common thing.'
4
Examine the two possibilities and the conclusion
The specific attribute must be necessary (لازم). If it is constitutive (مقوم), it is a substance – the desired substantial form. If not, infinite regress follows, which is impossible.
This step establishes the existence of substantial forms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خامسها : ( كما أن الهيولى لا تخلو عن الصورة الجسمية كذلك لا تخلو عن صورة أخرى بل لكل جسم من الأجسام ( صورة نوعية ) بحسبها يتنوع الجسم أنواعاً كثيرة من البسائط والمركبات وذلك ) لأنها ) أي الأجسام (مختلفة في اللوازم كقبول الانقسام الانفكاكي وقبول الالتئام والتشكل التابع لهما (بسهولة) كما في العنصريات الرطبة مثل الماء والهواء ) أو عسر ) كما في العنصريات اليابسة مثل الحجر والحديد ( أو عدمه ) أي عدم قبول ذلك الانقسام والالتئام والتشكل كما في الفلكيات. وليس ذلك الاختلاف في تلك اللوازم (للجسمية المشتركة) بين الأجسام لأن الأمور المختلفة لا يجوز أن تكون معللة بأمر مشترك ولا للهيولى لأنها قابلة فلا تكون فاعلة وأيضاً هيولى العناصر مشتركة فلا تكون مبدأ لأمور مختلفة، ولا للمفارق لأن نسبته إلى الأجسام كلها على السوية ( بل) لا بد أن يكون ذلك لأمر مختص) أي ثابت لبعض من الأجسام دون بعض ويجب أن يكون ذلك ) الأمر المختص لازماً ليمكن استناد ما هو لازم إليه ) فإن كان ذلك الأمر المختص اللازم ( مقوماً للجسم فهو المطلوب ) إذ لا بد حينئذ من أن يكون جوهراً، فقد ثبت في الأجسام جواهر مختصة هي مباد لآثارها ولوازمها المختلفة ولا معنى للصورة النوعية إلا ذلك ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم يكن مقوماً للجسم بل كان خارجاً لازماً (عاد الكلام فيه ) لاحتياجه حينئذ إلى أمر آخر مختص يستند هو إليه، ( ويتسلسل
Fifth: Just as hyle is never devoid of corporeal form, so it is never devoid of another form; rather, every body has a specific substantial form, according to which bodies are diversified into many types, both simple and composite. This is because bodies differ in their necessary properties, such as accepting splitting separation, and accepting conglomeration and shaping consequent upon them, (easily) as in moist elemental things like water and air, or (with difficulty) as in dry elemental things like stone and iron, or (not at all) as in celestial things. And that difference in those necessary properties is not due to the common corporeality (shared by all bodies), because different things cannot be caused by a single common thing, nor due to hyle, because it is receptive and not active, and also the hyle of elements is common and cannot be the source of different things, nor due to the separate intellect, because its relation to all bodies is equal. Rather, it must be due to a specific attribute (i.e., something belonging to some bodies and not others), and that specific attribute must be necessary so that what is necessary can be attributed to it. If that necessary specific attribute is constitutive of the body, then that is what is sought; for then it must be a substance, and it has been established that bodies have specific substances that are the principles of their different effects and necessary properties, and substantial form means nothing other than that. But if it is not constitutive of the body (i.e., it is an external necessary attribute), then the discussion applies to it, because it would then require another specific attribute to which it is attributed, leading to infinite regress.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof for the existence of substantial forms (الصورة النوعية) in bodies. It begins by stating that, just as hyle (prime matter) is never without corporeal form, it is also never without another form—a specific substantial form for each body. This is argued based on observation: bodies differ in their necessary properties, such as their ability to be split, conjoined, or shaped. Some do so easily (like water and air), some with difficulty (like stone and iron), and some not at all (like celestial spheres). These differences cannot be caused by what is common to all bodies (like corporeality itself) because a single common cause would produce uniform effects. Nor can they be caused by hyle, because hyle is passive and receptive, not active; moreover, the hyle of all elements is the same, so it cannot be the source of diverse properties. Nor can they be caused by the separate intellect, which relates equally to all bodies. Therefore, there must be a specific attribute peculiar to each body that is the cause of its specific properties. This attribute must be necessary (لازم) for the body, meaning it is inseparable. If it is constitutive (مقوم) of the body, then it must be a substance (جوهر) — and that is precisely what is meant by substantial form. If it were merely an accidental necessary attribute, then it would itself require another specific cause, leading to an infinite regress (تسلسل), which is impossible. Thus, the argument establishes that each body has an intrinsic substantial form that accounts for its distinctive essential properties.

Key Terms
هيولى
صورة نوعية
جسمية مشتركة
لازم
مقوم
جوهر
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Differences in bodily properties are due to accidental attributes rather than a substantial intrinsic principle.
Correction: The argument demonstrates that these differences require an intrinsic substantial form, not merely accidents, because accidental causes would themselves need explanation, leading to infinite regress.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth proof, why cannot the differences in bodily properties (such as ease or difficulty of division) be caused by the common corporeality (الجسمية المشتركة) shared by all bodies?
A) Because corporeality is not a cause at all.
B) Because different effects cannot be produced by a single, common cause.
C) Because corporeality is an accident.
D) Because bodies do not actually differ.

Key Takeaway
Bodies differ in properties because each has a unique substantial form that constitutes its essence and is the source of its specific necessary properties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every body has a unique substantial form that makes it what it is, what does that imply about the purpose God has for each created thing? How does that change the way you look at the water you drink or the ground you walk on?
?
The argument shows that differences are not accidental but essential. In your own life, do you treat the differences between people (abilities, circumstances) as merely accidental, or do you see them as part of a deeper divine wisdom? Will you commit to seeing others as having their own essential form and purpose?
?
Iji argues that if the specific attribute were not constitutive, we would need an infinite chain of explanations. Are you satisfied with superficial explanations for your own existence, or do you seek the ultimate constitutive cause that stops the regress? Where do you place your trust for your own identity?

The Succession of Forms Argument and the Reply from Essence
Al-Jurjani presents the objection to substantial forms and a reply based on intuitive essence-difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the possibility of infinite succession of forms implies the same for accidents, and the counter-reply that essential differences are known intuitively.

Core Idea
If forms can succeed infinitely, why can't accidents?

This passage presents a key debate in Islamic atomism and hylomorphism. The proponents of substantial forms argue that specific forms account for differences in matter. Their opponents turn the argument around: if infinite succession of forms is allowed, then accidents could also succeed infinitely, making substantial forms unnecessary. A reply is offered based on intuitive knowledge of essences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once for overall sense, then read the English translation. Note the dialectical structure: proponents' claim, objection, reply.
The passage begins mid-argument; the context is about proving hyle with specific forms.
2
Identify the objection
Find the sentence that begins with 'Since the succession of forms...' and understand why the opponent sees it as a problem for the proponents.
The key is that if forms can succeed infinitely, there is no argument against accidents doing the same.
3
Analyze the reply
Read the final sentence about intuitive knowledge. Note that the reply claims we know fire and water differ essentially, not accidentally.
The reply does not prove that the difference is substantial; it merely asserts it as intuitive.
4
Reflect on the debate
Consider whether the reply successfully refutes the objection. What assumptions does it make?
This debate touches on the nature of essences and the limits of rational argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الثامن : إثبات الهيولى في الجسم بصورها النوعية ليس للجسمية المشتركة، ولا للهيولى ولا للمفارق لما مر بعينه، فلا استنادها إلى صور أخرى ،مختصة وقد أجابوا عن ذلك بأن هيوليات الأفلاك متخالفة بالماهية وكل واحدة منها لا تقبل إلا صورة معينة وأما اختصاص العناصر بصورها، فلأن المادة قبل هذه الصورة كانت متصفة بصورة أخرى لأجلها استعدت لقبول الصورة اللاحقة، وهكذا إلى ما لا يتناهى (و) حينئذ ( نقول لهم : ( لما لم يمتنع تعاقب صور بلا نهاية (فلم) أي فلاي شيء ( يمتنع تعاقب أعراض بلا نهاية ) بل هذا أيضاً جائز فلا حاجة إلى إثبات الصورة النوعية في العناصر لذلك ولا في الأفلاك لأن موادها لا تقبل إلا ما هو عارض لها (وأجاب بعضهم عن ذلك بأنا نعلم بديهة أن حقيقة النار مخالفة لحقيقة الماء فلا بد من اختلافهما بأمر جوهري مختص
The First Observatory - The Eighth Objective: Proving the Hyle (al-hayula) in the body with its specific forms (suwar al-naw'iyya) is not for the common corporeality, nor for the hyle, nor for the separate, because of what has passed exactly. So it is not attributed to other specific forms. And they answered that the hyle of the celestial spheres are different in essence (māhiyya), and each one of them accepts only a specific form. As for the specific forms of the elements, it is because the matter before this form was qualified by another form for the sake of which it became prepared to accept the subsequent form, and so on to infinity. Then we say to them: Since the succession of forms without end is not impossible, then for what reason is the succession of accidents without end impossible? Rather, this is also permissible. So there is no need to prove the specific form (al-sūra al-naw'iyya) in the elements for that reason, nor in the celestial spheres, because their materials accept only what is accidental to them. And some answered that by saying: We know intuitively that the reality of fire is different from the reality of water, so they must differ by a specific substantial thing (amr jawharī mukhtaṣṣ).
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents an argument of the proponents of substantial forms and the objection of their opponents. The author (al-Jurjani) begins by stating the context: the discussion is about proving the existence of hyle (prime matter) in the body with its specific forms. The proponents argue that the common corporeality, hyle, and separate forms cannot account for the specific forms, so there must be specific forms. They respond to an objection about celestial spheres by saying their hyle differ in essence. Then the opponents object: if the succession of forms is possible eternally, then why can't accidents succeed likewise? This would eliminate the need for substantial forms. In reply, some proponents claim that we intuitively know that fire and water have different realities, implying a substantial difference. The author does not endorse this reply but presents it as part of the debate.

صورة نوعية
تعاقب الصور
عرض
جوهر
بديهة
حقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The intuitive knowledge that fire and water are different is about their accidental properties.
Correction: The reply states that we know intuitively their realities (essences) are different, implying a substantial, not accidental, difference.

Checkpoint
What is the opponents' objection based on the possibility of infinite succession?
A) It proves that hyle is eternal.
B) It shows that specific forms are necessary.
C) It implies that accidents can also succeed infinitely, making specific forms unnecessary.
D) It refutes the existence of celestial spheres.

Key Takeaway
The objection from infinite succession challenges the necessity of substantial forms, but some proponents argue that essential differences are known intuitively.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever rely on intuitive knowledge ('badīha') to settle a debate, or do you demand proof? Can you think of a belief you hold that you consider self-evident but might be questioned by others? How does this lesson challenge you to examine the grounds of your certainty?
?
The opponents argue that if infinite succession is allowed for forms, it should also be allowed for accidents, undermining the need for forms. In your own life, do you apply the same standard of reasoning consistently to similar cases, or do you sometimes make exceptions? Reflect on a time when inconsistency in your reasoning allowed you to avoid changing a deeply held view.

The Proof from Water's Return and the First Refutation
Al-Jurjani presents a proof for substantial forms and gives the first refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument for substantial forms from water returning to cold and why it fails because a principle of quality does not have to be a constitutive form.

Core Idea
Water's return does not prove a substantial form; a quality principle need not be a constituent.

Al-Jurjani presents a common argument for substantial forms: water heated and left returns to cold, implying a permanent principle. He then refutes it by arguing that even if such a principle exists, it does not have to be a constituent of the body like a substantial form. This lesson unpacks that argument and its first refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and initial response
Read the Arabic and translation of the proof and the beginning of the refutation. Note how the proof assumes a permanent principle of quality.
The proof is based on observable behavior of water.
2
Identify the two parts
Distinguish the proof (water returns to cold) from the first counterargument (the principle need not be a constitutive form).
3
Reflect on the counterargument
Ask yourself: why does the existence of a principle of quality not imply it is a substantial form? The refutation says it could be accidental or due to divine action.
This connects to the broader debate on the composition of bodies.
4
Consider the implication
Understand that this refutation undermines the proof for substantial forms without denying the possibility of a principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما يستدل على إثبات الصورة النوعية بأن الماء إذا سخن ثم ترك يعود بالطبع بارداً فثمة أمر هو مبدأ للكيفية باق يرد الماء إلى الكيفية الزائلة بعد زوال القاسر . ( قلنا : ( إِن سلم أن في الجسم أمرا هو مبدأ للكيفية فلا يجديكم ( ومن أين يلزم كونه من مقومات الجسم حتى يكون صورة نوعية ؟ على أنا لا نسلم ذلك، (و) نقول : (لم قلتم : إنه ) أي عود الماء إلى البرودة ( ليس بفعل الفاعل المختار) على طريقة جري العادة وهذا الفرع الخامس أعني ثبوت الصورة النوعية (مع ضعفه لعدم صحة أدلته ( أصل ) كبير له فروع كثيرة ) من المباحث الفلكية ) والعنصرية ( فتحققه ولا تنس كيلا تحتاج إلى التنبيه على ضعف ما يتفرع عليه من تلك المباحث . قال الإمام الرازي: لما فرغنا من بيان ذاتيات الجسم ومقوماته فلنذكر أحكامه ثم شرع في إثبات الحيز الطبيعي إلا أن المصنف جعله من تفاريع الهيولى،
And perhaps one may infer to prove the specific form (al-sūra al-naw'iyya) by the fact that water when heated and then left returns by nature to cold, so there is something that is a principle of the quality (mabda' li-l-kayfiyya) remaining which returns the water to the vanishing quality after the coercer ceases. We say: Even if we grant that in the body there is something that is a principle of the quality, it does not benefit you; and from where does it necessitate that it is among the constituents of the body so that it is a specific form? Moreover, we do not grant that. And we say: Why do you say that it, i.e., the return of water to coldness, is not the act of the free agent (al-fā'il al-mukhtār) according to the way of the habitual course of events? And this fifth branch, namely the proof of the specific form (al-sūra al-naw'iyya), despite its weakness for lack of soundness of its proofs, is a great root having many branches, from astronomical and elemental investigations. So realize it and do not forget, so that you do not need to draw attention to the weakness of what branches out from that of those investigations. Imam al-Razi said: When we finished the exposition of the essentials of the body and its constituents, let us mention its properties. Then he proceeded to prove the natural place (al-ḥayz al-ṭabī'ī), except that the author made it a derivative of the hyle.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage contains a second argument for substantial forms and its refutation, followed by an editorial note. The argument: water heated and then left to itself returns to cold, indicating a permanent principle that brings back the lost quality. The refutation has two parts: first, even if such a principle exists, it does not have to be a constituent (muqawwim) of the body like a substantial form; second, the return could be directly caused by God acting by His habitual course. The editorial note comments that this fifth branch (the proof of substantial form) is weak, but it is a major source for many astronomical and elemental issues. The note ends with a quotation from al-Razi indicating that after discussing the essentials of the body, the next topic is natural place, which the author (al-Iji) treated as derivative from hyle.

Key Terms
صورة نوعية
مبدأ الكيفية
مقومات الجسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any enduring principle of change in a body must be a substantial form.
Correction: The principle could be an accidental quality or direct divine action; it does not have to be a constitutive substantial form.

Checkpoint
What is the first refutation's main point?
A) Water does not actually return to cold.
B) The principle of quality does not have to be a constitutive form.
C) The principle of quality is impossible.
D) The proof is valid.

Key Takeaway
The proof from water's return fails because a principle of quality need not be a substantial form; it could be something else.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This refutation shows that a seemingly obvious inference (water's return to cold proves a substantial form) can be wrong. When in your own life have you confidently attributed a cause to something that may actually be directly from God? Will you pause next time to consider that Allah may be acting without an intermediate 'nature'?
?
The argument assumes that what is 'natural' is reliable, but the Ash'ari reminds you that all 'natural' events are sustained by Allah. Does your daily routine reflect a conviction that every moment, even the warming of water, is a direct act of God? How can you cultivate that awareness?
?
If a principle of quality does not have to be a constituent of the body, then your own qualities—your talents, moods, health—may not be 'essential' to you. How would you live differently if you truly believed that every quality you have is a gift from Allah that He could remove or replace at any moment?

The Refutation by Divine Habit and the Editorial Note
Al-Jurjani gives the second refutation and an editorial note on the branch's importance and weakness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the appeal to divine habitual action refutes the substantial form argument, and why this branch is considered weak yet foundational for many astronomical and elemental discussions.

Core Idea
Water's return may be due to God's habit, not an immanent form; this branch is weak but a root of many branches.

Al-Jurjani continues the refutation by offering an alternative explanation: the return of water could be directly caused by the free agent (God) acting according to His customary way. He then remarks that this fifth branch (the proof of substantial form) is weak but a great root for many issues in astronomy and elements. This lesson explores that second refutation and the editorial comment.

Guided Path
1
Read the second refutation
Read the passage where Al-Jurjani questions why the return cannot be the act of the free agent by way of habit. Understand the Ash'ari concept of 'jari al-ada'.
This is a key Ash'ari response to natural causality.
2
Examine the editorial note
Read the comment that this branch, though weak, is a root for many branches in astronomy and elemental science.
The author warns to remember this to avoid repeating mistakes.
3
Connect to al-Razi's transition
Read the quote from al-Razi that after discussing the essentials of the body, the next topic is natural place, but the author (al-Iji) links it to hyle.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how the two refutations together undermine the proof for substantial forms, and why the editor still considers the topic important.

Definitions of Simple Body and Their Four Considerations
Defines simple body with two definitions and four considerations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two definitions of a simple body in Islamic atomism and the four considerations arising from applying each definition to truth or sense.

Core Idea
A simple body is defined by its parts and composition — two definitions yield four considerations.

The author begins by dividing bodies into simple and compound, then provides two definitions of the simple body. The first definition requires that every part equals the whole in name and definition, but this works only if the part is quantitative. The second definition focuses on composition from different natures. Each definition can be considered according to truth or sense, leading to four distinct categories. This passage establishes the conceptual framework for classifying physical bodies.

Guided Path
1
Understand the division of body
Body is divided into simple and compound. The simple body is defined in two ways. Read the first definition and note the condition involving the part.
The first definition fails if the body is composed of hyle and form unless the part is quantitative.
2
Examine the condition for the first definition
Imam al-Razi explains that the definition holds only if the body is not composed of hyle and form. Otherwise, the part must be qualified as quantitative. The author clarifies that 'part' means quantitative part.
Otherwise, hyle and form would be incorrectly included as parts.
3
Learn the second definition and the four considerations
The second definition: simple body is not composed of bodies of different natures. Each definition can be considered by truth or sense, giving four considerations in total.
The first two considerations are partially explained: truth includes elements but excludes spheres and organic parts; sense includes elements and organs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجسم ينقسم إلى بسيط ومركب ) ويظهر لك وجه الانحصار فيهما من بيان مفهوميهما (و) الجسم ( البسيط له رسمان ) مشهوران (الأول : ما جزؤه ) أي كل جزء منه ( مساو لكله في الاسم والحد كالماء مثلاً، قال الإمام الرازي : هذا إنما يستقيم إذا قلنا بأن الجسم غير مركب من الهيولى والصورة، بل هو جوهر متصل قائم بذاته لا بمادة ، وأما إذا قيل إنه مركب منهما فإنه لا يستقيم، لأن جزءه المادي وحده أو الصوري وحده لا يساويه في الاسم والحد بل لا بد حينئذ من أن يقيد الجزء بكونه جسمياً أي مقدارياً، وإلى ذلك أشار المصنف بقوله : ( والمراد ) بالجزء المذكور في رسم البسيط ( هو الجزء المقداري وإلا ورد الهيولي والصورة ) فإنهما جزءان من الجسم البسيط ولا يساويانه فيما ذكر فلا ينطبق هذا الرسم على شيء من الأجسام البسيطة وإذا أريد الجزء المقداري كان منطبقاً عليها سواء تركبت منهما أو لا . ( الثاني : ( من رسمي الجسم البسيط ( ما لا يتركب من أجسام مختلفة الطبائع وكل منهما أي من هذين الرسمين ( قد يعتبر بحسب الحقيقة أو الحس فهذه أربعة اعتبارات) في رسم البسيط . الأول : ما جزؤه المقداري بحسب الحقيقة مساو لكله في الاسم والحد فيندرج فيه العناصر الأربعة، لأن كل جزء مقداري يفرض فيها يساوي كله في اسمه وحده دون الفلك، إذ ليس أجزاؤه المقدارية المفروضة فيه كذلك ودون الأعضاء المتشابهة الحيوانية كالعظم واللحم مثلا، إذ فيها أجزاء العناصر ولا تشاركها في أسمائها وحدودها الثاني : ما يكون جزؤه المقداري بحسب الحس مساوياً له فيما ذكر، فيتناول مع العناصر الأعضاء
Body is divided into simple and compound. The face of their exclusivity will appear from clarifying their concepts. The simple body has two well-known definitions: The first: that whose part — i.e., every part of it — is equal to the whole in name and definition, like water for example. Imam al-Razi said: This is valid only if we say that body is not composed of hyle and form, but is a continuous substance subsisting by itself without matter. But if it is said that it is composed of them, then it is not valid, because its material part alone or its formal part alone does not equal it in name and definition. Rather, then the part must be qualified as being bodily, i.e., quantitative. And to that the author pointed by saying: 'And what is meant by the part mentioned in the definition of the simple is the quantitative part; otherwise, hyle and form would be included,' for they are two parts of the simple body and do not equal it in what was mentioned, so this definition does not apply to any of the simple bodies. But if the quantitative part is intended, it applies to them whether they are composed of them or not. The second: of the two definitions of the simple body: that which is not composed of bodies of different natures. And each of these two definitions may be considered according to truth or sense; thus these are four considerations concerning the definition of the simple. The first: that whose quantitative part, according to truth, is equal to the whole in name and definition; so it includes the four elements, because every quantitative part assumed in them equals the whole in its name and definition, but not the sphere, since its assumed quantitative parts are not like that, nor the similar animal parts like bone and meat for example, for they contain parts of the elements and do not share with them in their names and definitions. The second: that whose quantitative part, according to sense, is equal to it in what was mentioned; so it includes, along with the elements, the organs.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage defines the simple body and provides its two definitions. First, the body is divided into simple (بسيط) and compound (مركب). The simple body has two definitions: (1) that whose every part equals the whole in name and definition. This works only if the body is not composed of hyle and form; otherwise the material or formal part alone does not equal the whole. The author clarifies that 'part' means quantitative part, otherwise hyle and form would be included. (2) That which is not composed of bodies of different natures. Each definition can be considered according to truth (الحقيقة) or sense (الحس), giving four considerations. The first consideration (truth) includes the four elements but excludes spheres and organic parts. The second consideration (sense) includes elements and organs. The passage ends with the beginning of the second consideration.

Key Terms
بسيط
مركب
رسم
جزء مقداري
هيولي
صورة
جسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first definition of simple body applies universally without needing to qualify the part as quantitative.
Correction: Imam al-Razi explains that if the body is composed of hyle and form, the material or formal part alone does not equal the whole, so the part must be understood as quantitative, not hyle or form.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Razi, when does the first definition of simple body fail?
A) When the body is a continuous substance.
B) When the body is composed of hyle and form.
C) When the part is quantitative.
D) When the body is a sphere.

Key Takeaway
A simple body is defined either by the equality of its quantitative part to the whole or by not being composed of different natures, with each definition applicable to truth or sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a definition of a religious concept without checking its conditions, as al-Razi did with the first definition? What might you need to re-examine with greater precision today?
?
The lesson shows that even the elements, which seem simple, have complexity when considered differently. Does your faith feel simple or complex? Are you willing to explore the depths of Aqeedah even if it challenges your comfort?
?
In your understanding of Tawheed, do you distinguish between how Allah is described in truth versus how you perceive Him? How can you guard against confusing the two based on this lesson’s emphasis on the two considerations?

The Division of Body and Two Definitions of Simple
Division of body and two definitions of simple.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the body is divided into simple and composite, and the two definitions of a simple body, including the resolution of an objection by Imam al-Razi.

Core Idea
A simple body is defined either by its parts' equality or its lack of compositional heterogeneity.

Before diving into the properties of physical bodies, we need a clear classification. This passage from al-Iji's Mawāqif introduces the fundamental division of body into simple and composite, and provides two definitions of the simple. Understanding these definitions is crucial for later discussions on mixture and change.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how al-Iji structures the division and the definitions.
Pay attention to al-Razi's objection and its resolution.
2
Identify the division and the two definitions
The passage states that body is divided into simple and composite. Then it gives two definitions of simple. Locate each definition and see how they differ.
The first definition concerns parts; the second concerns composition from different natures.
3
Analyze the first definition and al-Razi's objection
The first definition says a simple body is that whose every part is equal to the whole in name and definition. Al-Razi objects if body is composed of hyle and form. See how the author resolves this by specifying 'quantitative part'.
This shows the importance of precise wording in definitions.
4
Understand the second definition
The second definition is simpler: a simple body is not composed of bodies of different natures. Consider how this complements the first definition.
This definition focuses on homogeneity of substance.
5
Reflect on the purpose of two definitions
Ask yourself why two definitions are needed. Are they two ways of saying the same thing, or do they capture different aspects of simplicity?
The commentary explains they are both valid but emphasize different features.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - الفصل الثاني : مقدمة في تركيبة الجسم
[ الفصل الثاني ]
من فصلي المرصد الأول ( في أقسامه ) أي أقسام الجسم الطبيعي الذي تبين في الفصل الأول حقيقته وأجزاؤه ) وأحكام كل قسم منها ) أي من تلك الأقسام ( وفيه ) أي في هذا الفصل الثاني ( مقدمة وأقسام) خمسة .
[ المقدمة : في تركيبة الجسم ]
الجسم ينقسم إلى بسيط ومركب ) ويظهر لك وجه الانحصار فيهما من بيان مفهوميهما (و) الجسم ( البسيط له رسمان ) مشهوران (الأول : ما جزؤه ) أي كل جزء منه ( مساو لكله في الاسم والحد كالماء مثلاً، قال الإمام الرازي : هذا إنما يستقيم إذا قلنا بأن الجسم غير مركب من الهيولى والصورة، بل هو جوهر متصل قائم بذاته لا بمادة ، وأما إذا قيل إنه مركب منهما فإنه لا يستقيم، لأن جزءه المادي وحده أو الصوري وحده لا يساويه في الاسم والحد بل لا بد حينئذ من أن يقيد الجزء بكونه جسمياً أي مقدارياً، وإلى ذلك أشار المصنف بقوله : ( والمراد ) بالجزء المذكور في رسم البسيط ( هو الجزء المقداري وإلا ورد الهيولي والصورة ) فإنهما جزءان من الجسم البسيط ولا يساويانه فيما ذكر فلا ينطبق هذا الرسم على شيء من الأجسام البسيطة وإذا أريد الجزء المقداري كان منطبقاً عليها سواء تركبت منهما أو لا . ( الثاني : ( من رسمي الجسم البسيط ( ما لا يتركب من أجسام مختلفة الطبائع .
The First Observatory - Chapter Two: Introduction to the Composition of Body. [Chapter Two] from the two sections of the First Observatory (on its divisions, i.e., the divisions of the natural body whose reality and parts were clarified in the first chapter) and the rulings of each division from those divisions. And in it, i.e., in this second chapter, there is an introduction and five sections. [Introduction: On the Composition of Body] The body is divided into simple and composite. The reason for their exclusivity appears from the explanation of their concepts. The simple body has two famous definitions. First: that whose part, i.e., every part of it, is equal to the whole in name and definition, like water for example. Imam al-Razi said: This is valid only if we say that the body is not composed of hyle and form, but is a self-subsisting continuous substance not in a substrate. But if it is said that it is composed of them, then it is not valid, because its material part alone or its formal part alone is not equal to it in name and definition; rather then the part must be qualified as bodily, i.e., quantitative. The author alluded to this by his saying: 'What is meant by the part mentioned in the definition of the simple is the quantitative part; otherwise hyle and form would be included.' For they are two parts of the simple body and are not equal to it in what was mentioned, so this definition does not apply to any simple body. If the quantitative part is intended, it applies to them whether they are composed of them or not. Second: of the two definitions of the simple body: that which is not composed of bodies of different natures.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage defines the division of body into simple and composite, and gives two definitions of the simple body. It begins by stating the division: 'the body is divided into simple and composite', and notes that the exclusivity of these two categories is understood from their concepts. The first definition of the simple is: that every quantitative part of it is equal to the whole in name and definition, citing water as an example. Imam al-Razi is then quoted raising a problem: if the body is composed of hyle and form, then the material or formal part alone is not equal to the whole, so the definition fails unless the part is qualified as 'quantitative'. The commentator explains that the author (al-Iji) pointed this out by specifying that the intended part is the quantitative part, thus excluding hyle and form. The second definition is: the simple is that which is not composed of bodies of different natures. The passage ends with this definition, setting the stage for the four considerations that follow. The commentary (qāla) clarifies the author's wording regarding the exclusivity and the scope of the definitions.

Key Terms
بسيط
مركب
جزء مقداري
هيولى
صورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The two definitions of simple body are contradictory or redundant.
Correction: They are complementary: the first addresses parts and definition, the second addresses composition. A simple body satisfies both.

Checkpoint
According to the first definition of a simple body, what condition must its quantitative part satisfy?
A) It must be equal to the whole in name and definition.
B) It must not be composed of different natures.
C) It must be sensible.
D) It must be a compound of hyle and form.

Key Takeaway
The body is divided into simple and composite; a simple body is defined either by part-whole equality (quantitative parts) or by uniformity of nature (not composed of different bodies).

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
7 min
The Four Considerations of the Simple Body
Enumerates four considerations of simple body with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the two definitions of simple body, combined with the modes of reality and sense, generate four distinct considerations.

Core Idea
Two definitions × two modes = four classifications for simple bodies.

This passage from al-Mawaqif introduces a precise classification of simple bodies. By considering each of the two definitions under the aspects of reality (ḥaqīqa) and sense (ḥiss), four categories emerge, each including different types of bodies. The author then illustrates these categories with examples such as the four elements, the celestial sphere, and animal organs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions of simple body
Read the beginning of the passage to find the two definitions (rasmān). Note that each can be considered by reality or sense.
The two definitions are the ones given earlier in the text; here they are applied.
2
List the four considerations
The passage enumerates four considerations. Write them down: first (reality, first definition), second (sense, first definition), third (reality, second definition), fourth (sense, second definition).
Pay attention to the examples: elements, celestial sphere, similar organs.
3
Analyze the relationships
The passage explains that the first consideration is the most specific, the fourth is the most general, and the second and third have partial overlap. Understand why based on the membership criteria.
Use the summary given to see how each consideration excludes certain bodies.
4
Summarize the classification
Restate the four considerations in your own words, noting which bodies are included in each. This will solidify your understanding.
The commentary clarifies that these are applications of the definitions, not separate categories.
