وكل منهما أي من هذين الرسمين ( قد يعتبر بحسب الحقيقة أو الحس فهذه أربعة اعتبارات) في رسم البسيط .
قوله : ( ويظهر لك إلخ) فلذا تعرض المصنف لتعريفها وترك دليل الانحصار .
قوله : ( أي كل جزء منه الذي بعض أجزائه مساو لكله دون البعض داخل في المركب
قوله : (قال الإمام إلخ ) لم يظهر لي فائدة نقل كلام الإمام .
الأول : ما جزؤه المقداري بحسب الحقيقة مساو لكله في الاسم والحد فيندرج فيه العناصر الأربعة، لأن كل جزء مقداري يفرض فيها يساوي كله في اسمه وحده دون الفلك، إذ ليس أجزاؤه المقدارية المفروضة فيه كذلك ودون الأعضاء المتشابهة الحيوانية كالعظم واللحم مثلا، إذ فيها أجزاء العناصر ولا تشاركها في أسمائها وحدودها الثاني : ما يكون جزؤه المقداري بحسب الحس مساوياً له فيما ذكر، فيتناول مع العناصر الأعضاء المتشابهة، فإن كل جزء محسوس منها يساويها في الاسم والحد دون الفلك. الثالث : ما لا يتركب بحسب الحقيقة من أجسام مختلفة الطبائع فيشمل العناصر والفلك دون شيء من أعضاء الحيوان . الرابع: ما لا يتركب بحسب الحس من أجسام مختلفة الطبائع فيتناول الكل، فهو أعم الاعتبارات وأولها أخصها وبين الثاني والثالث عموم من وجه وتلخيصه أن ما لا يتركب من أجسام محسوسة مختلفة الطبائع إما أن لا يتركب من أجسام مختلفة ويتركب منها لكنها غير محسوسة. وعلى الأول : إما أن لا يكون اسمه موضوعاً له بشرط كونه موصوفاً بصفة مخصوصة كالماء والأرض والهواء والنار، فيشاركه أجزاؤه في اسمه وحده، وإما أن يكون مشروطاً به فلا يطلق اسمه على أجزائه، كالفلك إذ قد اعتبر في اسمه شكل معين. وعلى الثاني أيضاً، إما أن لا يعتبر في الاسم صفة كاللحم والعظم فيطلق اسمه على جزئه، أو يعتبر فلا يطلق كالشريان والوريد، إذ قد اعتبر فيهما التجويف والهيئة المخصوصة. فالاعتبار الرابع هذه الأربعة بأسرها. والأول يتناول واحداً منها ولا يخفى عليك حال الآخرين وإلى ما فصلناه لك أشار مجملا بقوله : ( فاعتبر ذلك ) أي الذي ذكرناه من اعتبار كل واحد من رسمي البسيط بحسب الحقيقة أو الحس ) في الأعضاء المتشابهة ) الحيوانية ( كاللحم والعظم) ونظائرهما ( وفي الفلك يظهر لك الفرق ) بين الاعتبارات الأربعة كما عرفت (و) الجسم ( المركب بخلافه ) فهو على الرسم الأول ما لا يكون جزؤه المقداري بحسب الحقيقة مساوياً له في الاسم والحد فيخرج عنه من البسائط المذكورة العناصر دون الفلك والأعضاء المتشابهة . وإن اعتبر الجزء المقداري بحسب الحس خرجت تلك الأعضاء أيضاً، وعلى الرسم الثاني هو ما يتركب بحسب الحقيقة

(
قوله : ( وأولها أخصها لاختصاصه بالعناصر . قوله : ( وبين الثاني والثالث عموم إلخ ) لصدقهما على العناصر وصدق الثاني على الأعضاء المتشابهة بدون الثالث وصدق الثالث بدون الثاني في الفلك . قوله : (كالشريان) وهي العرق النابت من القلب المتحرك بحركته، والوريد العرق النابت من الكبد الغير المتحرك .
قوله : ( كالشريان والوريد إلخ الشريان هو عرق نابض مجوف ثابت من القلب، والوريد هو عرق مجوف مضاعف غليظ في العنق، وهو اثنان : ففي كل عنق وريدان كل منها اثنان . قوله : ( دون الفلك لا يكون جميع أجزائه المقدارية بحسب الحقيقة مساوياً في الاسم والحد ، فحينئذ لا يضر أن يكون بعض أجزائه كذلك مثل مجموع المتممين الحاوي والمحوي، فإنه جزء من فلكه وإنه مساو له في ذلك بحسب الحقيقة كما لا يخفى .
And each of them, i.e., of these two definitions, may be considered with respect to reality or sense; so these are four considerations in the definition of the simple. His saying: 'and it appears to you etc.' So the author engaged in defining them and left the proof of exclusivity. His saying: 'i.e., every part of it whose some parts are equal to the whole but not others is included in the composite.' His saying: 'Imam etc.' I do not see the benefit of quoting the Imam's words. First: That whose quantitative part with respect to reality is equal to the whole in name and definition; so it includes the four elements, because every quantitative part supposed in them is equal to the whole in its name and definition, unlike the celestial sphere, for its supposed quantitative parts are not so, and unlike the similar animal organs like bone and flesh, for they contain parts of the elements and do not share their names and definitions. Second: That whose quantitative part with respect to sense is equal to it in what was mentioned; so it includes along with the elements the similar organs, for every sensible part of them is equal to them in name and definition, unlike the celestial sphere. Third: That which is not composed with respect to reality of bodies of different natures; so it includes the elements and the celestial sphere, and excludes any animal organs. Fourth: That which is not composed with respect to sense of bodies of different natures; so it includes everything, so it is the most general of the considerations, and the first is the most specific, and between the second and third there is generality and specificity from one aspect. Its summary: that which is not composed of sensible bodies of different natures either is not composed of different bodies, or is composed of them but they are insensible. On the first: either its name is not posited for it conditionally upon its being described with a particular attribute, like water, earth, air, and fire, so its parts share its name and definition; or it is conditional, so its name is not applied to its parts, like the celestial sphere, for a specific form is considered in its name. On the second also: either no attribute is considered in the name like flesh and bone, so its name is applied to its part; or it is considered, so it is not applied, like the artery and vein, for cavity and specific form are considered in them. So the fourth consideration includes these all. The first takes one of them, and the condition of the others is not hidden from you. And to what we detailed for you he alluded summarily by his saying: 'So consider that' i.e., what we mentioned of considering each of the two definitions of the simple according to reality or sense in the similar animal organs like flesh and bone and their analogues, and in the celestial sphere the difference between the four considerations appears, as you have known. And the composite body is contrary to it; according to the first definition, it is that whose quantitative part with respect to reality is not equal to it in name and definition, so from the mentioned simples, the elements are excluded, but not the celestial sphere and the similar organs. If the quantitative part is considered with respect to sense, those organs are also excluded. And according to the second definition, it is that which is composed with respect to reality. (Commentary: His saying: 'and the first is the most specific because it is specific to the elements.' His saying: 'and between the second and third there is generality etc.' because both are true of the elements, and the second is true of the similar organs without the third, and the third is true without the second in the celestial sphere. His saying: 'like the artery' which is the vein that springs from the heart moving with its movement, and the vein is the vein that springs from the liver without movement. His saying: 'like the artery and vein etc.' The artery is a pulsating hollow vein fixed from the heart, and the vein is a hollow thick double vein in the neck, and it is two: in each neck there are two veins, each of them two. His saying: 'except the celestial sphere' not all of its quantitative parts with respect to reality are equal in name and definition, so then it does not harm that some of its parts are so, like the totality of the completing 'container and contained', for it is part of its sphere and it is equal to it in that with respect to reality, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the four considerations (i'tibarāt) that result from applying the two definitions of the simple body under two modes: reality (ḥaqīqa) and sense (ḥiss). It also gives the definition of the composite (murakkab). The author begins by stating that each of the two definitions can be considered by reality or sense, yielding four considerations. The commentary then clarifies the author's earlier wording. The first consideration, based on the first definition by reality, includes only the four elements (water, earth, air, fire) because their quantitative parts share their name and definition; the celestial sphere and similar animal organs (like bone and flesh) are excluded. The second consideration, based on the first definition by sense, adds similar organs because their sensible parts are equal to the whole, still excluding the celestial sphere. The third consideration, based on the second definition by reality, includes elements and the celestial sphere (since they are not composed of bodies of different natures with respect to reality), but excludes animal organs which are composed of different natures. The fourth consideration, based on the second definition by sense, includes everything because nothing composed of sensible bodies of different natures is excluded at the sensible level. The relationship: the first consideration is the most specific (only elements), the fourth is the most general (everything), and the second and third have partial overlap. A summary is given: the subject under consideration may either not be composed of different bodies, or be composed but insensibly; in both cases further subdivisions affect name application. The author alludes to this in his condensed statement. Finally, the composite body is defined in contrast to the simple: according to the first definition, it is that whose quantitative part is not equal to the whole (by reality, excludes elements; by sense, also excludes similar organs); according to the second definition, it is that which is composed with respect to reality or sense. The commentary clarifies technical terms (artery, vein, celestial sphere parts) and the relationships between the considerations.

Key Terms
اعتبار
حقيقة
حس
بسيط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The four considerations are separate, mutually exclusive categories of simple bodies.
Correction: They are different ways of applying the two definitions under two modes; they overlap and are not exclusive. For example, elements appear in all four considerations.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is included in the first consideration (by reality of first definition)?
A) The celestial sphere
B) A bone
C) Water
D) An animal organ

Key Takeaway
The four considerations classify simple bodies based on two definitions applied under reality or sense, each yielding a different set of included bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world around you, do you tend to judge things by their sensible appearance or by their true reality? How might this distinction affect your understanding of Allah's creation?
?
Consider your own soul: is it simple or composite in its essence? How does the way you perceive your own nature (by sense or by reality) shape your relationship with your Creator?
?
The fourth consideration includes all bodies as simple at the level of sense. In your life, are there areas where you accept things as simple or unproblematic based only on superficial perception, while ignoring a deeper complexity that requires accountability?

What Is Kalam?

6 min
The Definition of the Composite Body
Defines composite body by negation of simple criteria.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the composite body is defined in contrast to the simple body using the same two definitions and modes.

Core Idea
The composite body is defined negatively: it fails the simple body criteria under each consideration.

After detailing the four considerations for simple bodies, the author now defines the composite body. The composite is that which does not satisfy the condition of the simple under a given definition and mode. This lesson examines how the definition of composite depends on which consideration of simple is used, and how it excludes certain bodies.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two definitions of simple body
Review the two definitions: (1) quantitative part equal to whole in name and definition, (2) not composed of bodies of different natures. The composite is defined as the opposite.
The composite is 'bikhilafihi' (contrary to it).
2
Understand the composite based on the first definition
According to the first definition, the composite is that whose quantitative part is not equal to the whole. Then it depends on mode: by reality, elements are excluded (they are simple), but celestial sphere and similar organs are composite? Actually read carefully: 'So from the mentioned simples, the elements are excluded, but not the celestial sphere and the similar organs.' This is tricky: it means under the first definition by reality, the composite includes celestial sphere and similar organs because they are not simple. By sense, the composite excludes similar organs (they become simple under sense?). The passage needs careful interpretation.
The commentary explains that the definition of composite is the negation of the simple condition.
3
Understand the composite based on the second definition
According to the second definition, the composite is that which is composed of bodies of different natures. By reality, animal organs are composite; by sense, nothing is composite (all bodies appear simple at sensible level) but the author says 'according to the second definition, it is that which is composed with respect to reality' - meaning the composite is defined only by reality? The passage says: 'And according to the second definition, it is that which is composed with respect to reality.' So the composite under the second definition is only by reality; sense mode yields no composite because everything is sensible simple.
Note that the composite definition mirrors the simple but in negation.

قوله : ( كالشريان والوريد إلخ الشريان هو عرق نابض مجوف ثابت من القلب، والوريد هو عرق مجوف مضاعف غليظ في العنق، وهو اثنان : ففي كل عنق وريدان كل منها اثنان . قوله : ( دون الفلك لا يكون جميع أجزائه المقدارية بحسب الحقيقة مساوياً في الاسم والحد ، فحينئذ لا يضر أن يكون بعض أجزائه كذلك مثل مجموع المتممين الحاوي والمحوي، فإنه جزء من فلكه وإنه مساو له في ذلك بحسب الحقيقة كما لا يخفى .
And each of them, i.e., of these two definitions, may be considered with respect to reality or sense; so these are four considerations in the definition of the simple. His saying: 'and it appears to you etc.' So the author engaged in defining them and left the proof of exclusivity. His saying: 'i.e., every part of it whose some parts are equal to the whole but not others is included in the composite.' His saying: 'Imam etc.' I do not see the benefit of quoting the Imam's words. First: That whose quantitative part with respect to reality is equal to the whole in name and definition; so it includes the four elements, because every quantitative part supposed in them is equal to the whole in its name and definition, unlike the celestial sphere, for its supposed quantitative parts are not so, and unlike the similar animal organs like bone and flesh, for they contain parts of the elements and do not share their names and definitions. Second: That whose quantitative part with respect to sense is equal to it in what was mentioned; so it includes along with the elements the similar organs, for every sensible part of them is equal to them in name and definition, unlike the celestial sphere. Third: That which is not composed with respect to reality of bodies of different natures; so it includes the elements and the celestial sphere, and excludes any animal organs. Fourth: That which is not composed with respect to sense of bodies of different natures; so it includes everything, so it is the most general of the considerations, and the first is the most specific, and between the second and third there is generality and specificity from one aspect. Its summary: that which is not composed of sensible bodies of different natures either is not composed of different bodies, or is composed of them but they are insensible. On the first: either its name is not posited for it conditionally upon its being described with a particular attribute, like water, earth, air, and fire, so its parts share its name and definition; or it is conditional, so its name is not applied to its parts, like the celestial sphere, for a specific form is considered in its name. On the second also: either no attribute is considered in the name like flesh and bone, so its name is applied to its part; or it is considered, so it is not applied, like the artery and vein, for cavity and specific form are considered in them. So the fourth consideration includes these all. The first takes one of them, and the condition of the others is not hidden from you. And to what we detailed for you he alluded summarily by his saying: 'So consider that' i.e., what we mentioned of considering each of the two definitions of the simple according to reality or sense in the similar animal organs like flesh and bone and their analogues, and in the celestial sphere the difference between the four considerations appears, as you have known. And the composite body is contrary to it; according to the first definition, it is that whose quantitative part with respect to reality is not equal to it in name and definition, so from the mentioned simples, the elements are excluded, but not the celestial sphere and the similar organs. If the quantitative part is considered with respect to sense, those organs are also excluded. And according to the second definition, it is that which is composed with respect to reality. (Commentary: His saying: 'and the first is the most specific because it is specific to the elements.' His saying: 'and between the second and third there is generality etc.' because both are true of the elements, and the second is true of the similar organs without the third, and the third is true without the second in the celestial sphere. His saying: 'like the artery' which is the vein that springs from the heart moving with its movement, and the vein is the vein that springs from the liver without movement. His saying: 'like the artery and vein etc.' The artery is a pulsating hollow vein fixed from the heart, and the vein is a hollow thick double vein in the neck, and it is two: in each neck there are two veins, each of them two. His saying: 'except the celestial sphere' not all of its quantitative parts with respect to reality are equal in name and definition, so then it does not harm that some of its parts are so, like the totality of the completing 'container and contained', for it is part of its sphere and it is equal to it in that with respect to reality, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the four considerations (i'tibarāt) that result from applying the two definitions of the simple body under two modes: reality (ḥaqīqa) and sense (ḥiss). It also gives the definition of the composite (murakkab). The author begins by stating that each of the two definitions can be considered by reality or sense, yielding four considerations. The commentary then clarifies the author's earlier wording. The first consideration, based on the first definition by reality, includes only the four elements (water, earth, air, fire) because their quantitative parts share their name and definition; the celestial sphere and similar animal organs (like bone and flesh) are excluded. The second consideration, based on the first definition by sense, adds similar organs because their sensible parts are equal to the whole, still excluding the celestial sphere. The third consideration, based on the second definition by reality, includes elements and the celestial sphere (since they are not composed of bodies of different natures with respect to reality), but excludes animal organs which are composed of different natures. The fourth consideration, based on the second definition by sense, includes everything because nothing composed of sensible bodies of different natures is excluded at the sensible level. The relationship: the first consideration is the most specific (only elements), the fourth is the most general (everything), and the second and third have partial overlap. A summary is given: the subject under consideration may either not be composed of different bodies, or be composed but insensibly; in both cases further subdivisions affect name application. The author alludes to this in his condensed statement. Finally, the composite body is defined in contrast to the simple: according to the first definition, it is that whose quantitative part is not equal to the whole (by reality, excludes elements; by sense, also excludes similar organs); according to the second definition, it is that which is composed with respect to reality or sense. The commentary clarifies technical terms (artery, vein, celestial sphere parts) and the relationships between the considerations.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The composite body is simply the opposite of the simple body in all respects.
Correction: The definition of composite depends on which definition and mode of the simple is used; it is not a uniform negation.

Checkpoint
According to the first definition considered by sense, which body is composite?
A) The four elements
B) The celestial sphere
C) A bone
D) An animal organ

Key Takeaway
The composite body is defined as the negation of the simple body's criteria under each of the four considerations, resulting in different inclusions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The celestial sphere is composite by reality but simple by sense. Is there something in your life that you judge based on appearances (sense) rather than its true nature (reality)? Commit to investigating one such matter this week with the intention of uncovering its haqiqa.
?
The definition of the composite emerges from negation: it is not simple. Are you defined by what you are not more than by what you are? Consider one label you reject about yourself and ask: what positive reality does that denial conceal? What would it mean to live from that reality instead?

Classification of Bodies into Simple and Composite
Enumeration of five body divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fivefold classification of bodies into simple (with three subtypes) and composite (with two subtypes) as explained by the author.

Core Idea
The fivefold classification maps all bodies: three simple, two composite.

The author now introduces a classification of bodies based on their composition. Understanding this taxonomy is key to later discussions on the nature of the universe. The passage is short but dense, enumerating five distinct categories derived from two main branches.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without translating. Notice the key terms بسيط and مركب.
2
Study the literal translation
Read the English translation carefully. Identify the main division into simple and composite.
3
Follow the author's subdivisions
Trace how simple divides into celestial (further divided into spheres and planets) and elemental (one division). Then composite divides into those with and without temperament.
Count the divisions: 3 simple + 2 composite = 5 total.
4
Check your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question and list the five divisions in your own words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الجسم البسيط أي الذي لا تتركب حقيقته من أجسام مختلفة الطبائع ينقسم إلى فلكي وعنصري فالفلكي الأفلاك والكواكب فهو قسمان والعنصري العناصر الأربعة وهذا قسم واحد والمركب ينقسم إلى ما له مزاج وإلى ما لا مزاج له فهذه خمسة أقسام ثلاثة للبسيط واثنان للمركب
Then the simple body, i.e., whose reality is not composed of different natures, divides into celestial and elemental. So the celestial includes the spheres and planets, so it is two divisions. The elemental includes the four elements, and this is one division. The composite divides into that which has a temperament and that which has no temperament. So these are five divisions: three for the simple and two for the composite.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage defines the classification of bodies. The author first defines a simple body as one whose essence is not composed of different natures. He then divides it into celestial (the spheres and planets) and elemental (the four elements). However, he further divides the celestial into two sub-divisions (spheres and planets), making a total of three simple divisions: spheres, planets, and elements. Composite bodies are divided into those with a temperament (like living beings) and those without a temperament (like artificial objects), making two composite divisions. Thus total divisions are five.

The Nine Celestial Spheres and Their Religious Identifications
Enumeration of nine spheres with religious identifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers' nine universal spheres correspond to the Throne, Footstool, and planetary spheres, and how the total count reaches twenty-four with epicycles and eccentrics.

Core Idea
The cosmos has nine spheres; the outermost is the Throne, the next the Footstool.

This passage from the philosophical tradition lists the nine celestial spheres as established by observation. The author aligns each sphere with a term from Islamic scripture, linking cosmology to religious language. Understanding this list helps you see how pre-modern thinkers integrated philosophy and faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice the structure: a claim, then an enumeration.
2
Identify the nine universal spheres
List the spheres in order from outermost to innermost. Note which are identified with the Throne and Footstool.
The outermost is the 'sphere of spheres' (atlas), then the sphere of fixed stars, then the seven planets.
3
Understand the count of twenty-four
The nine universal spheres plus six epicycles and eight eccentrics total twenty-four spheres. This shows the complexity of the Ptolemaic model.
4
Reflect on the religious identifications
Consider why the Throne (ʿarsh) is identified with the outermost sphere and the Footstool (kursī) with the sphere of fixed stars. This connects cosmology to Quranic terms.
This identification is a key point of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن الحكماء زعموا أن الأفلاك الكلية الثابتة بالرصد تسعة تشتمل هذه التسعة على أربعة وعشرين فلكا أي هي مع ما في ضمنها من الأفلاك الجزئية هذا العدد. فتسعة من الأفلاك كما سيتلى عليك كلية وستة تداوير وثمانية خارجة. أما التسعة الكلية فهي فلك الأفلاك وهو المسمى أيضا عندهم بالفلك الأطلس لأنه غير مكوكب على رأيهم والمسمى بالعرش المجيد في لسان الشرع وتحته فلك الثوابت وهو الكرسي ثم فلك زحل ثم فلك المشتري ثم فلك المريخ ثم فلك الشمس ثم فلك الزهرة ثم فلك عطارد ثم فلك السماء الدنيا لأنه أقرب إلينا من سائر الأفلاك.
The sages claimed that the universal spheres established by observation are nine. These nine contain twenty-four spheres, meaning along with the partial spheres within them this number. So nine of the spheres, as will be recited to you, are universal, six are epicycles, and eight are eccentrics. As for the nine universal spheres: they are the sphere of spheres, also called by them the atlas sphere because in their view it is starless, and called the Glorious Throne in religious language; beneath it is the sphere of fixed stars, which is the Footstool; then the sphere of Saturn, then Jupiter, then Mars, then the Sun, then Venus, then Mercury, then the sphere of the lowest heaven because it is nearest to us among the spheres.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage enumerates the nine celestial spheres. The author begins by stating the claim of the sages that there are nine universal spheres established by observation. These nine, together with their partial spheres (epicycles and eccentrics), total 24 spheres. He then lists the nine universal spheres in descending order: the outermost is the sphere of spheres (al-falak al-aṭlas), identified with the Throne (al-ʿarsh); then the sphere of fixed stars, identified with the Footstool (al-kursī); then the seven spheres of the planets: Saturn, Jupiter, Mars, Sun, Venus, Mercury, and the Moon (the lowest heaven). Each identification aligns philosophical cosmology with Islamic religious terms.

Correction: This passage explicitly identifies the Throne with the outermost sphere (the atlas sphere), showing that philosophers equated it with the highest physical sphere.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the night sky, do you see only stars and planets, or do you perceive the Throne and Footstool as described? How would your awe of Allah increase if you internalized that the outermost sphere is His Throne? This week, spend a moment under the open sky and consciously recite 'آية الكرسي' reflecting on its cosmic reality.
?
The sages used observation to map the spheres, yet they identified them with revealed names. Are you equally diligent in using both reason and revelation to understand Allah's creation? In what area of your life have you neglected to see the signs of Allah? Commit to studying one sign of Allah in nature this week and write down how it points to Him.
?
If the lowest heaven is the moon's sphere, which is closest to you, and above it are seven more spheres up to the Throne, what does that tell you about your position in the universe? Are you living as if you are under the direct rule of the One on the Throne? Before you sleep tonight, ask yourself: 'Have I acted today as if Allah's Throne encompasses all?' and make a concrete intention to change one behavior.

Definition and Proof of Direction (Jiha)
Al-Jurjani defines 'direction' as the terminus of sensory indication and the goal of motion, and proves its existence via two syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical definition of direction (jiha) as the terminus of sensory indication and the goal of motion, and why it must exist as an abstraction from bodies.

Core Idea
Direction is not a non-being; it is a real concept abstracted from bodies.

This lesson introduces the concept of direction (jiha) as understood by Islamic philosophers. Al-Jurjani defines it in two complementary ways—as the terminus of sensory indication and as the goal of motion—and then argues that direction must exist because pure non-being cannot be indicated or moved toward. Understanding this foundation is essential for later discussions about the body that determines directions.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of direction
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice the two definitions: 'terminus of sensory indication' and 'goal of the spatial mover.'
The definitions are given in one sentence.
2
Understand the two properties
Identify the two properties: being an object of sensory indication and being a goal of rectilinear motion. These are the basis for the proof.
3
Follow the proof of existence
The passage argues that direction must exist because pure non-being cannot be a terminus of indication or a goal of motion. Reflect on why that is impossible.
Pure non-being has no reality to be indicated or aimed at.
4
See how direction exists as an abstraction
The commentary clarifies that direction exists not independently but as an abstraction from a body, e.g., 'down' is abstracted from the center of the earth. This resolves the objection about the direction of down.
This corrects the misconception that direction is a self-subsistent entity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - الفصل الثاني : القسم الأول : المقصد الثاني : في اتجاهات الجسم
[المقصد الثاني : في اتجاهات الجسم]
(في المحدد أي في إثبات جسم يحدد الجهات ويعين وضعها، وفي بيان أحكامه ( قالوا : ( أي الحكماء ( الجهة منتهى الإشارة الحسية ( ومقصد المتحرك ) الأيني ) بالحصول فيه ) أي بالقرب منه والحصول عنده، وذلك أن العقلاء يشيرون إشارة حسية إلى الجهات ويقولون : تحرك كذا في جهة كذا فقد تعلق الإشارة الحسية بالجهة، وصارت أيضاً مقصداً للحركة المستقيمة ( فهي موجودة لامتناع أن يكون العدم المحض كذلك ) أي متعلق الإشارة الحسية ومقصد المتحرك بالوصول إليه، أو
قوله : ( في المحدود من الحد بمعنى التمييز، أي مميزاً لجهات .
قوله: ( ويعين وضعها أي ما يتعين به قبولها للإشارة فاندفع ما قيل: من أنه إن أريد بمحدد الجهات فاعلها فلا نسلم كونه ذا وضع وإن أريد به قابلها فمحدود العلو والسفل، ليس واحداً ضرورة أن المركز قائم بالأرض .
أنه
قوله : ( منتهى الإشارة هاتان خاصتان للجهة يستدل بكل واحد منهما على وجود هاتين الجهتين، ففسروا بتفسيرين : مبنى الأول على العلو محدب الفلك الأعظم، ومبنى الثاني على مقعر فلك القمر على ما وهم .
قوله : ( المتحرك الأيني قيد اتفاقي بيان للواقع لا احترازي.
قوله : ( أي بالقرب منه والحصول عنده إذ معنى الحصول الحصول فيه عنده قرباً ووصولاً
إذ لا يمكن الحصول في الجهة . قوله : (تحرك كذا في جهة (كذا أي تحرك في سمت يتأدى إليها كذا في المقاصد قوله : ( فقد تعلق إلخ ) نشر على ترتيب اللف .
قوله : (فهي موجودة نتيجة للقياسين المستفادين مما سبق على هيئة الأول أي الجهة منتهى الإشارة وكل ما هو منتهى الإشارة موجود والجهة مقصد الحركة وكل ما هو مقصد المتحرك موجود المراد في الخارج إما في نفسها أو في غيرها ومعنى وجودها كون الغير في الخارج بحيث تنتزع تلك الجهة منه فلا يرد أن جهة السفل، أعني المركز ليست بموجودة في
الخارج.
قوله : ( العدم المحض) أي ما ليس له وجود في نفسه ولا في شيء ينتزع منه بل هو مجرد
اعتبار توهم من الوهم .
قوله : (أي بالقرب منه وجه تفسير قوله : بالحصول فيه بقوله : أي بالقرب منه ظاهر إذ لا يتصور الحصول في الجهات بل المتصور هو القرب منها، كما في النقطة المركزية التي هي مركز
العالم .
The first observation - Chapter two - Section one - Second purpose: On the directions of a body. [Second purpose: On the directions of a body.] (On the determiner, i.e., on establishing a body that determines the directions and specifies their position, and on explaining its properties) They said, i.e., the philosophers: 'Direction is the terminus of sensory indication' and 'the goal of the spatial mover by obtaining in it' i.e., by being near it and obtaining at it. And that is because rational people indicate sensorily towards directions and say: 'So-and-so moved in such a direction.' Thus sensory indication attaches to the direction, and it also becomes a goal for rectilinear motion. So it is existent, because it is impossible for pure non-being to be such, i.e., the object of sensory indication and the goal of the mover in reaching it. Or (his statement: (in the determiner from 'determination' meaning distinction, i.e., distinguishing directions. His statement: (and specifies their position, i.e., that by which they become receptive to indication. So the objection that if by determiner of directions is meant its agent, we do not grant that it has a position, and if by it is meant its receiver, then the determiner of up and down is not one, necessarily because the center is established in the earth. That he said: (the terminus of indication) These are two properties of direction by each of which one infers the existence of these two directions, and they interpreted them in two ways: the first is based on the convex of the highest sphere, and the second on the concave of the sphere of the moon, as they fancied. His statement: (the spatial mover is an incidental qualification, an explanation of reality, not restrictive. His statement: (i.e., by nearness to it and obtaining at it because the meaning of 'obtaining' is obtaining in it nearness and arrival, since obtaining in the direction is impossible. His statement: ('moved in such a direction' i.e., moved in a direction leading to it, such in purposes. His statement: (thus it attaches etc.) is a disjunction according to the order of the wording. His statement: (so it is existent) is the result of two syllogisms derived from what preceded in the form of the first: i.e., direction is the terminus of indication, and everything that is the terminus of indication is existent; and direction is the goal of motion, and everything that is the goal of the mover is existent. The intended [existence] is in the external world either in itself or in something else, and the meaning of its existence is that the other is in the external world such that that direction is abstracted from it. So the objection that the direction of down, i.e., the center, is not existent in the external world does not apply. His statement: (pure non-being) i.e., what has no existence in itself nor in anything from which it is abstracted, but is a mere imaginative consideration of the estimative faculty. His statement: (i.e., by nearness to it) is the explanation of his statement: 'by obtaining in it' by his statement: 'i.e., by nearness to it', which is clear because obtaining in directions is inconceivable; rather, what is conceivable is nearness to them, as in the central point which is the center of the world.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage defines and proves the existence of 'direction' (جهة). It begins by stating the purpose: to establish a body that determines directions and specifies their positions. Then it quotes the philosophers: 'Direction is the terminus of sensory indication' and 'the goal of the spatial mover by obtaining in it' i.e., by being near it. Rational people indicate directions sensorially, e.g., 'he moved in such a direction,' so direction becomes an object of sensory indication and a goal for rectilinear motion. The passage then argues that direction must exist because pure non-being (عدم محض) cannot be an object of sensory indication or a goal of motion. The commentary (قوله) explains key points: 'determiner' (محدد) means that which distinguishes directions; the objection that a determiner might be an agent (not having position) or a receiver (being multiple) is dismissed. Two properties of direction are given: being terminus of indication and being goal of motion; each proves existence. Philosophers interpreted these as the convex of the highest sphere (for up) and the concave of the moon's sphere (for down). The commentator clarifies that 'spatial mover' is an incidental qualification, not restrictive. Obtaining in a direction is impossible; we only obtain nearness, like the central point. The existence of direction is proved by two syllogisms: direction is terminus of indication → everything that is terminus of indication exists; direction is goal of motion → everything that is goal of motion exists. Existence here means either in itself or as an abstraction from something external. Thus the direction 'down' (center) exists as an abstraction from the earth, not as a separate entity. Pure non-being is merely an imaginary consideration.

Key Terms
جهة (direction)
منتهى الإشارة (terminus of indication)
مقصد المتحرك (goal of the mover)
عدم محض (pure non-being)
محدد (determiner)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Direction is a self-subsistent entity existing independently.
Correction: Direction exists not in itself but as an abstraction from a body that determines it, e.g., 'down' is abstracted from the earth's center.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why must direction (جهة) be existent?
A) Because it is a physical body that occupies space.
B) Because pure non-being cannot be a terminus of sensory indication or a goal of motion.
C) Because it is directly perceived by the senses as a separate entity.
D) Because all philosophers agree on its existence without proof.

Key Takeaway
Direction is a real concept abstracted from bodies, not a non-being; its existence is proven by its being the object of sensory indication and the goal of motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your daily prayer has a direction—the qibla. After learning that direction is a real concept abstracted from bodies, do you feel your focus during prayer is on the physical direction itself or on the One it points to? How can this lesson sharpen your intention?
?
If direction were pure non-being, it could not be pointed to. Your life is full of directions—goals, ambitions, moral paths. Are you treating these directions as abstract realities that shape your actions, or as empty illusions? What would change if you saw them as real?
?
The proof of direction relies on it being an object of sensory indication. When you point to a place, what are you really indicating? Examine your heart: do you sometimes reduce spiritual realities to mere gestures? How can you ensure your pointing—your worship—is anchored in real meaning?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
5 min
The Sun's Inclined Orbit
Al-Iji proves the sun's orbit inclination from observed north-south oscillation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Iji uses the sun's north-south oscillation to prove that its orbit is inclined relative to the celestial equator.

Core Idea
The sun's north-south movement proves its orbit is not in the equator's plane.

Al-Iji begins by describing a simple observation: the sun moves north of the zenith, reaches a limit, returns, then moves south similarly. From this cyclic oscillation, he draws a geometrical necessity: the sun's orbit must be inclined (mā'il) relative to the celestial equator. This passage establishes the foundational astronomical fact used later to define the zodiac.

Guided Path
1
Visualize the sun's motion
Read the description of the sun moving northward, returning to zenith, then southward. Imagine this from a point on Earth.
The 'limit' refers to the maximum declination (the tropics).
2
Identify the logical deduction
Notice the phrase 'فعلم أن مدار الشمس مائل عن معدل النهار' – this is the key conclusion. Ask: why does this oscillation imply inclination?
If the orbit were in the equator's plane, the sun would always be on the equator; no north/south swing.
3
Connect to the celestial equator
The celestial equator (معدل النهار) is the reference. The sun's deviation north and south shows its orbit is not in that plane.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - الفصل الثاني : القسم الأول : المقصد الثاني : في اتجاهات الجسم هناك تارة إلى الشمال متباعدة عن سمت الرأس في تلك المواضع ( قليلاً قليلاً إلى غاية ما ثم ترجع من تلك الغاية ( متقاربة إليه قليلا قليلا حتى تسامت ثم تميل إلى الجنوب كذلك أي متباعدة عن سمت الرأس إلى غاية ما مساوية للغاية الأولى ثم ترجع منها متقاربة إليه قليلاً قليلاً حتى تسامته ( هكذا حالها ( دائماً) إذ تميل تارة أخرى إلى الشمال إلى تلك الغاية ثم ترجع وتميل إلى الجنوب وتعود أبدا إلى مثل الحالة الأولى ( فعلم) من ذلك ( أن مدار الشمس مائل عن معدل النهار ليس) واقعاً ( في سطحه وإلا لم يمل عن المعدل شمالاً وجنوباً ) والشمس إذا قارنت كوكباً ما ) من الكواكب ( الثابتة خلفته إلى المغرب، فعلم من هذا ) أن لها حركة ) خاصة من المغرب ( إلى المشرق أسرع من حركة الثوابت ) يعني حركتها الخاصة كما ستعرفها ( بها تدرك ) الشمس (الثوابت التي تكون في جهة المشرق منها تم تتجاوزها مخلفة إياها إلى المغرب وتفرض دائرة موازية لمدارها في الفلك الأعظم قاطعة لجميع ما تحتها ) من الأفلاك وغيرها ( كأنها ) أي كان تلك الدائرة الموازية القاطعة (مدار الشمس) التي يتحرك عليها مركزها ( انبسطت ) إلى سطح الفلك الأعلى وانقبضت إلى ما تحتها (وتسمى ( الدائرة المذكورة ) منطقة البروج ) لمرورها بأوساط البروج ) وفلك البروج ) إطلاقا لاسم الفلك على الدائرة ) ومنطقة الحركة الثانية ) لأن منطقة الفلك الثامن المتحرك بالحركة الثانية في سطح هذه الدائرة ( وإنها ) أي الدائرة الموازية ( تقطع معدل النهار بنصفين على نقطتين متقابلتين لأنهما دائرتان عظيمتان وهناك يكون دورة الفلك دولابياً. ولا يكون في الفلك كوكب ولا نقطة إلا وهو يطلع ويغرب سوى قطبي العالم وما يقرب منهما من الكواكب والنقط وسيجيء تمام الكلام إن شاء الله تعالى .
The First Observatory - The Second Chapter - The First Section - The Second Intention: On the Directions of the Body. There [the sun] sometimes [inclines] to the north, moving away from the zenith in those places little by little to a certain limit, then returns from that limit approaching it little by little until it is directly overhead, then it inclines to the south similarly, i.e., moving away from the zenith to a certain limit equal to the first limit, then returns from it approaching it little by little until it is directly overhead. This is its state always, for it inclines another time to the north to that limit, then returns and inclines to the south and returns forever to like the first state. So it is known from that that the orbit of the sun is inclined from the celestial equator, not lying in its surface, otherwise it would not incline from the equator north and south. And when the sun aligns with a fixed star, it leaves it behind to the west, so it is known from this that it has a proper motion from the west to the east faster than the motion of the fixed stars – meaning its proper motion as you will know. By it the sun overtakes the fixed stars that are to the east of it, then passes them leaving them behind to the west. And we suppose a circle parallel to its orbit in the highest sphere, cutting through everything below it from the spheres and others, as if that parallel cutting circle were the sun's orbit on which its center moves, expanded to the surface of the highest sphere and contracted to what is below it. And that mentioned circle is called the zodiac, because it passes through the middles of the zodiacal constellations; and the sphere of the zodiac, by applying the name of sphere to the circle; and the region of the second motion, because the region of the eighth sphere moving with the second motion is in the surface of this circle. And it – i.e., the parallel circle – cuts the celestial equator in halves at two opposite points because they are two great circles. There the revolution of the sphere is like a wheel. And there is no star or point in the sphere except that it rises and sets, except the two poles of the world and what is near them of stars and points, and the complete discussion will come, God willing.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. It begins by describing the sun's apparent motion relative to an observer's zenith: the sun moves northward away from the zenith to a limit, then returns to the zenith, then moves southward similarly, repeating this cycle. From this observed oscillation, al-Iji deduces that the sun's orbit (مدار الشمس) is inclined (مائل) relative to the celestial equator (معدل النهار), for if it were in the same plane, the sun would not deviate north and south. Next, he notes that when the sun reaches a fixed star, it leaves it behind toward the west, implying that the sun has a proper motion from west to east faster than the fixed stars. He then introduces an imagined circle parallel to the sun's orbit in the highest sphere, cutting all lower spheres, as if the sun's orbit were expanded to that sphere. This circle is called 'منطقة البروج' (the zodiac) because it passes through the middles of the zodiacal constellations, and also 'فلك البروج' (sphere of the zodiac) by extension, and 'منطقة الحركة الثانية' (region of the second motion) because the eighth sphere's motion lies in its surface. Finally, he states that this circle cuts the celestial equator at two opposite points (the equinoxes) because both are great circles, and notes that all stars rise and set except the celestial poles and nearby points.

Key Terms
مدار الشمس
معدل النهار
سمت الرأس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sun's orbit is in the same plane as the celestial equator.
Correction: The observed north-south oscillation proves it is inclined.

Checkpoint
What observation does Al-Iji use to conclude that the sun's orbit is inclined?
A) The sun leaves fixed stars behind.
B) The sun moves north and south of the zenith.
C) The ecliptic is a great circle.
D) The sun rises and sets at different points.

Key Takeaway
The sun's annual north-south oscillation proves its orbit is inclined to the celestial equator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the sun's path across the sky each day and its changing height throughout the year, do you see it as a mere astronomical fact or as a sign from your Creator calling you to reflect? What would change in your daily awareness if you deliberately connected each sunrise to the One who set it in its precise, inclined orbit?
?
Al-Iji deduced the sun's inclination from careful observation and reasoning. When was the last time you observed something in nature—not casually, but with the intent to recognize the wisdom and power behind it? What prevents you from doing this more often?
?
The sun's movement is so regular it can be described as 'always' returning to the same limits. How does the unerring constancy of the sun's motion challenge your own consistency in worship? Are you as reliable in your return to obedience as the sun is in its return to its limits?

The Sun's Proper Motion
Al-Iji proves the sun's proper motion from its overtaking fixed stars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the sun overtaking fixed stars reveals its proper motion from west to east.

Core Idea
The sun catches up to and passes fixed stars, showing it moves eastward faster.

Al-Iji makes another observation: when the sun aligns with a fixed star, it leaves that star behind to the west. This indicates that the sun has its own motion relative to the fixed stars. He then describes the direction: from west to east, faster than the fixed stars' motion. This proper motion is crucial for understanding the ecliptic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the observation
The sun and a fixed star are together; later the sun is west of the star (i.e., star is left behind). This means the sun moves eastward relative to stars.
Fixed stars move slowly westward due to precession; sun moves faster eastward.
2
Identify the conclusion
The phrase 'فعلم من هذا أن لها حركة خاصة من المغرب إلى المشرق' states the conclusion. Note the direction: west to east.
3
Compare speeds
Al-Iji says the sun's motion is faster than that of the fixed stars. This explains why the sun overtakes them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Definition of the Ecliptic
Al-Iji defines the ecliptic and its names and properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition and names of the ecliptic (zodiac) as the circle parallel to the sun's orbit.

Core Idea
The ecliptic is an imagined circle that expands the sun's orbit to the highest sphere.

Al-Iji now introduces a conceptual circle: a circle parallel to the sun's orbit in the highest sphere, cutting through all lower spheres. This circle is given multiple names, including 'zodiac' and 'region of the second motion'. He explains why it is called so and notes its geometric relation to the celestial equator.

Guided Path
1
Visualize the conceptual circle
Imagine a circle parallel to the sun's orbit but expanded to the highest sphere. This circle cuts all spheres below.
This is a mental construct, not a physical circle.
2
Learn the names
The circle is 'منطقة البروج' because it passes through the middles of zodiacal constellations, and also 'فلك البروج' and 'منطقة الحركة الثانية'.
The eighth sphere's second motion lies in its surface.
3
Understand the intersection
The ecliptic and celestial equator are both great circles and intersect at two opposite points (equinoxes).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Correction: It is a conceptual circle, assumed to be parallel to the sun's orbit and used as a reference.

Checkpoint
Why is the circle called 'منطقة البروج'?
A) Because it is the region of the fixed stars.
B) Because it passes through the middles of the zodiacal constellations.
C) Because it is the equator.
D) Because it is the sun's actual orbit.

Key Takeaway
The ecliptic is an imagined circle parallel to the sun's orbit, named the zodiac and region of the second motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The ecliptic is an imagined tool to understand the sun's path, not a physical reality. What models or frameworks do you use in your deen that might be misconstrued as the ultimate reality? How can you remind yourself that they are tools, not ends?
?
Al-Iji describes a second motion so subtle it takes centuries to notice—yet it is part of Allah's constant order. Are you attentive to the slow, ongoing signs of Allah's power in your own life? Commit to noticing one such sign this week and acting on it.
?
You have just read about circles and spheres used to describe the heavens. Do you ever pause to consider that the universe itself is a sign of Allah's knowledge and power? What one astronomical fact from this lesson can you use in your daily reflections to deepen your awe of Him?

Morning and Evening Distance: Definition and Exception
Commentary explains definition and restricts to Venus and Mercury.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of morning and evening distance and why it applies only to Venus and Mercury.

Core Idea
Morning and evening distance is a measure that only makes sense for the inferior planets.

This passage clarifies a technical term used in Ptolemaic astronomy: the 'morning and evening distance' of a planet from the sun. The commentator first gives a precise definition, then critiques the author's attempt to apply it to all planets, arguing it is only meaningful for Venus and Mercury. Let's read the Arabic and translation carefully, then unpack the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the full Arabic passage and its literal English translation. This will give you the complete argument before we analyze it.
2
Understand the definition of morning and evening distance
The first sentence defines the term: the eastern half of the epicycle diameter from the sun's center is called 'evening distance', and the western half is 'morning distance', named after the time the planet appears.
This definition is purely geometrical.
3
Follow the commentator's critique of the author
The commentator says that for most planets, considering the morning/evening distance from the sun is 'futile' (لغو) because the author's goal is to prove carriers (eccentrics) via differences in epicycle radii, and this distance is unrelated to that proof.
4
See the exception for Venus and Mercury
The commentator then explains that for Venus and Mercury, the situation is special: their epicycle centers are always near the sun, so their maximum morning/evening distance equals the epicycle radius. Thus, the definition applies correctly to them.
This resolves the apparent inconsistency and refutes the author's overextension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بعدها الصباحي والمسائي النصف المشرقي من مركز الشمس من قطر التدوير بعداً مسائياً لظهور الكواكب إذا كان عليه مساء، والبعد المغربي منه بعد يسمى صباحياً لظهور الكوكب إذا كان عليه صباحاً.
قوله : ( كأنه أراد به إلخ لأن المصنف في صدد إثبات الحوامل وهي إنما تثبت باختلاف أنصاف تداويرها بحسب الرؤية فلا بد أن تكون التداوير في حوامل خوارج المركز ولا مدخل في هذا المطلوب لاختلاف البعد الصباحي والمسائي من الشمس فاعتبار اختلاف البعد الصباحي والمسائي بالقياس إلى الشمس لغو .
قوله : (بحسب نصف قطريهما لكون مركز تدويرهما مقارناً للشمس دائماً فلا يبعدان عن الشمس قدامها وخلفها إلا بقدر ما يقتضيه نصف قطر تدويرهما هذا بحسب الجلي من النظر وأما بحسب الدقيق فيجب أن لا يكون مقارناً للشمس بحسب الحقيقة بل مقارنته قد تكون بالتقريب . ولذا يختلف غاية البعد الصباحي والمسائي مع كونه مركز التدوير في موضع معين كذا في شرح التذكرة المحضري فحينئذ عبارة المتن صحيحة بلا استثناء.
قوله : ( كأنه أراد به أي بالبعد الصباحي والمسائي إلى قوله : والمسطور في كتب القوم هذا إشارة إلى الرد على المصنف رحمه الله حيث جعل البعد الصباحي والمسائي متناولاً للخمسة المتحيرة مع أنه لا يكون إلا في السفلين أعني الزهرة والعطارد على ما سنذكره إن شاء الله تعالى. وقوله : إنما هي بحسب نصف قطر تدويرهما أي لا بحسب مركز تدويرهما حتى يلغو قوله عن الشمس ما فيهما أيضاً وذلك لأن مركز تدويرهما ملازم للشمس فلا يلغو ماذكره فيهما.
His saying: 'Its morning and evening distance: the eastern half from the center of the sun of the diameter of the epicycle is an evening distance due to the appearance of the planets when it is upon it in the evening, and the western distance from it is a distance called morning distance due to the appearance of the planet when it is upon it in the morning.' His saying: 'As if he meant by it, etc.' Because the author is in the process of proving the carriers, and they are only established by the difference of the radii of their epicycles according to sight, so it is necessary that the epicycles be in eccentric carriers, and there is no entry in this desired matter for the difference of the morning and evening distance from the sun, so considering the difference of the morning and evening distance in relation to the sun is futile. His saying: 'According to the radius of both of them' because the center of their epicycle is always adjacent to the sun, so they are not distant from the sun in front of it or behind it except by the amount required by the radius of their epicycle; this is according to the apparent view, but according to the precise view, it must not be adjacent to the sun in reality, rather its adjacency may be approximate. Hence the extreme morning and evening distance varies with the center of the epicycle being in a specific place, as in Sharh al-Tadhkira al-Mahdari; thus the statement of the text is correct without exception. His saying: 'As if he meant by it' i.e. by the morning and evening distance up to his saying: 'And what is recorded in the books of the people' is an indication of a refutation of the author, may God have mercy on him, where he made the morning and evening distance inclusive of the five wandering planets, while it is only for the two inferiors, namely Venus and Mercury, as we will mention, God willing. And his saying: 'It is only according to the radius of their epicycle' i.e. not according to the center of their epicycle, so that his statement 'from the sun' in them also becomes futile, because the center of their epicycle is attached to the sun, so what he mentioned about them is not futile.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'morning and evening distance' and explains its specific application to Venus and Mercury. The commentary begins by clarifying the definition: the eastern half of the epicycle's diameter from the sun's center is the evening distance, and the western half is the morning distance, named after the time the planet appears. Then it addresses the author's intention, noting that the author is trying to prove the existence of carriers (eccentrics) through differences in visible sizes of epicycle radii. The commentator argues that considering the morning and evening distance from the sun is irrelevant to this proof, making the author's reference to it futile. However, for Venus and Mercury specifically, the situation is different because their epicycle centers are always near the sun, so their maximum morning/evening distance is exactly the epicycle radius. The commentator reconciles this by stating that the text's statement is correct without exception when understood precisely. Finally, the commentator refutes the author's overextension of the term to all five planets, insisting it applies only to the inferior planets.

Key Terms
البعد الصباحي والمسائي
التدوير
الحامل
خارج المركز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The morning and evening distance applies to all five wandering planets.
Correction: It only applies to Venus and Mercury because their epicycle centers are attached to the sun.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator consider the morning and evening distance 'futile' (لغو) for the superior planets?
A) Because their epicycle centers are far from the sun and the distance is not related to the epicycle radius.
B) Because the author only intended it for Venus and Mercury.
C) Because the morning and evening distance is defined only for the inferior planets due to their proximity to the sun.
D) Because the carriers of superior planets are not eccentric.

Key Takeaway
Morning and evening distance is defined only for Venus and Mercury because their epicycle centers are always near the sun, making the distance equal to the epicycle radius.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The commentator corrected the author’s overgeneralization about morning and evening distance. In your own pursuit of knowledge, where have you been tempted to apply a rule beyond its proper domain? Are you willing to humbly accept correction when you overstep?
?
Consider the precision required to track planetary motions as described in this lesson. How does this intricate order point to the Creator’s wisdom? Does your daily life reflect awe at such detailed signs, or do you take them for granted?
?
The commentator goes out of his way to reconcile the author’s statement after criticizing it. When you disagree with someone, do you seek to salvage what is correct in their position, or do you simply dismiss them? What would change if you adopted this charitable approach?

The Five Single-Element Theories
Enumerates five single-element theories with their justifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five alternative theories that posit a single element as the origin of all things.

Core Idea
Five different metals were considered the one primal element.

The passage begins by questioning why the elements must be four, and then presents five theories that each claim a single element is the origin of all generated things. Each theory is based on a different physical principle, such as simplicity, compliance, or intermediacy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and list the five theories
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, then identify each of the five theories. Note the elements they propose: fire, air, water, earth, ocean.
Each theory has a distinct reasoning.
2
Examine the reasoning for fire
Why is fire considered the single element? The passage mentions its intense simplicity, its ability to transform others, and condensation producing the rest.
3
Compare the other four theories
For each of air, water, earth, and ocean, note the principle given (e.g., wetness and compliance for air, intermediacy for ocean).
Look for patterns in the arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المصنف: (المناقضة) لما ذكروه أن يقال : (لم لا يجوز أن لا تكون أربعة ؟ بل الحق أحد الأقوال التي ذكرها الآن إذ قيل هي (واحدة) واختلفوا في تلك الواحدة (على خمسة أقوال : الأول : إنما هي النار لشدة بساطتها ) إذ لا جسم أصرف في طبيعته من النار ( إذ الحرارة ( المفرطة التي فيها ( مديرة للكائنات ولأنها تحيل الغير إلى طبعها وحصلت البواقي من النار (بالتكاثف) فهي نار متكاثفة على وجوه متفاوتة. ( الثاني : ) الهواء الرطوبته ومطاوعته للانفعالات ) ولا شك أن الأصل يجب أن يكون مطاوعاً للتغيرات ( ويحصل) من الهواء النار بالحرارة الملطفة فهي هواء لطفته الحرارة ) والباقيان بالبرودة (المكثفة فهما هواء متكاثف تكاثفاً متفاوتاً. ( الثالث : ( هي ( الماء إذ قبوله التخلخل ) بالحرارة ( والتكاثف ) بالبرودة ( محسوس ) فحصل من تخلخله الهواء والنار ومن تكاثفه الأرض. (الرابع ) تكاثفه الأرض . ( الرابع ) هي ( الأرض وحصلت البواقي بالتلطيف ( الواقع على مراتب مختلفة (الخامس : ) هي البحار لتوسطه بين . الأربعة ) في اللطافة والكثافة فبازدياد لطافته يصير هواء ونارا، وبازدياد كثافته ماء وأرضاً . ( وقيل : ( ليست واحدة بل لا بد من التعدد ) فيها لأن التركيب في يستدعي تعدد ما منه تركيبها ( فاثنان على ثلاثة أقوال الأول : ) هما ( النار لأنها في غاية الخفة والحرارة، والأرض لأنها في غاية الثقل ،والبرودة والهواء نار مفترة، والماء أرض متخلخلة بالمزج مع أجزاء نارية ( الثاني : ( هما (الماء والأرض لافتقار الكائنات إلى الرطب للانفعال وحصول الأشكال (و) إلى ( اليابس للحفظ ) على الأشكال الحاصلة ( الثالث : ( هما ( الأرض والهواء لمثل ذلك ) فإن الهواء رطب قابل للأشكال بسهولة والأرض يابسة حافظة لها فالماء هواء اشتد تكاثفه والنار هواء اشتد حرارته . ( وقيل : ( العناصر ( ثلاثة هي : الأرض والماء والهواء لما مر من افتقار الكائنات إلى رطب ويابس ) والنار للحرارة المديرة وقد وقع في كلام الآمدي الهواء بدل الماء ولذلك قال : فالماء هواء متكاثف وفي كلام بعضهم أن الثلاثة ما عدا النار ) وقيل : ( أصول المركبات ليست أربعة أو مادونها على ما مر بل هي ( أجسام) وفي كلام الآمدي جواهر (صلبة غير متجزئة لا نهاية لها ) . وقيل : أصول المركبات هي ( السطوح ) لأن التركيب إنما يكون بالتلاقي والتماس وأول ما يكون ذلك بين السطوح المستقيمة
The author said: The contradiction to what they mentioned is that it said: why is it not permissible that they are not four? Rather the truth is one of the sayings that he mentioned now, for it is said they are one, and they differed concerning that one in five sayings: The first: that they are fire because of its intense simplicity, for there is no body purer in nature than fire, because the excessive heat in it manages the generated things, and because it transforms others into its nature, and the rest arise from fire by condensation, so they are condensed fire in varying ways. The second: air, because of its wetness and its compliance to affections, and there is no doubt that the origin must be compliant to changes, and from air fire arises by rarefying heat, so it is air that heat has rarefied, and the remaining two by condensing cold, so they are air condensed in varying degrees. The third: water, because its acceptance of rarefaction by heat and condensation by cold is sensed, so from its rarefaction arise air and fire, and from its condensation arise earth. The fourth: earth, and the rest arise by rarefaction occurring in different degrees. The fifth: the ocean because it is intermediate between the four in subtlety and density, so with increase of its subtlety it becomes air and fire, and with increase of its density it becomes water and earth. And it is said: they are not one, but rather multiplicity is necessary in them because composition in [generated things] requires multiplicity of that from which their composition [arises]. So two, on three sayings: The first: they are fire, because it is in the utmost lightness and heat, and earth because it is in the utmost heaviness and cold, and air is rarefied fire, and water is earth rarefied by mixing with fiery parts. The second: they are water and earth because generated things need the wet for affection and obtaining shapes, and the dry for preserving the shapes obtained. The third: they are earth and air for similar reasons, for air is wet and receptive of shapes easily, and earth is dry and preserves them, so water is air with intense condensation, and fire is air with intense heat. And it is said: the elements are three: earth, water, and air, because of the need of generated things for wet and dry, and fire for managing heat; and in the speech of al-Amidi, air replaced water, and therefore he said: water is condensed air, and in the speech of some of them that the three are other than fire. And it is said: the principles of compounds are not four or less than that as mentioned, but rather they are bodies, and in the speech of al-Amidi: indivisible solid substances without end. And it is said: the principles of compounds are surfaces, because composition occurs only by meeting and contact, and the first thing in that is between straight surfaces.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage presents a series of alternative theories to the four-element doctrine. It first lists five theories that posit a single element: fire, air, water, earth, or the ocean. Then it lists three theories that posit two elements: fire and earth; water and earth; earth and air. Then a theory positing three elements (earth, water, air, with fire as a managing principle, sometimes with air replacing water). Then theories rejecting elemental composition altogether: one theory says the principles are indivisible solid bodies; another says they are surfaces. The passage serves to show that the four-element view is not the only possibility.

Key Terms
مناقضة
عنصر واحد
نار
هواء
ماء
أرض
بحر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These theories are arbitrary guesses.
Correction: Each theory is based on a physical principle (simplicity, compliance, observability, etc.) and systematically explains how other elements derive from the single one.

Checkpoint
According to the fire theory, how do the other elements arise?
A) By rarefaction
B) By condensation
C) By mixing
D) By transformation

Key Takeaway
There are multiple, reasoned single-element theories, each with a different physical rationale.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an area in your life where you hold firmly to a single explanation for something complex—like why certain events happen or why someone behaves a certain way. The philosophers in this passage each argued powerfully for one element. What might you be missing by not considering alternative explanations? Will you commit to exploring at least one other perspective this week?
?
The passage shows five different reasoned views about the fundamental nature of reality. What does this variety teach you about intellectual humility? Is there a belief about Allah, existence, or yourself that you hold without ever questioning, even though other sincere people have held different views? How can you guard against arrogance in your own understanding?

The Three Two-Element Theories
Enumerates three two-element theories with reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three theories that propose two fundamental elements from which all others are derived.

Core Idea
Two elements can combine or transform to produce the rest.

After rejecting the single-element view, some argued for two elements. The passage presents three theories: fire and earth; water and earth; earth and air. Each pairs opposite qualities to account for diversity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three theories
Read the Arabic and translation, then extract the three pairs: (fire, earth), (water, earth), (earth, air).
Notice the reasoning for each.
2
Examine the fire-earth theory
Fire is light and hot; earth is heavy and cold. Air and water are derived by mixture or rarefaction.
3
Compare the other two theories
Water-earth emphasizes wet and dry; earth-air also uses wet and dry. See how they explain the missing elements.
Observe the shift in roles.

Commentary
This passage presents a series of alternative theories to the four-element doctrine. It first lists five theories that posit a single element: fire, air, water, earth, or the ocean. Then it lists three theories that posit two elements: fire and earth; water and earth; earth and air. Then a theory positing three elements (earth, water, air, with fire as a managing principle, sometimes with air replacing water). Then theories rejecting elemental composition altogether: one theory says the principles are indivisible solid bodies; another says they are surfaces. The passage serves to show that the four-element view is not the only possibility.

Three-Element and Non-Elemental Theories
Presents a three-element theory and two non-elemental theories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand theories that propose three elements or reject elements altogether in favor of indivisible bodies or surfaces.

Core Idea
Beyond four elements, thinkers proposed three, or no elements at all.

The passage continues with a theory that posits three elements (earth, water, air) with fire as a managing principle, and then two radical theories: that the principles of compounds are indivisible solid bodies, or that they are surfaces. These show the range of ancient natural philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three-element theory
Find the section starting 'وقيل: ثلاثة هي الأرض والماء والهواء'. Note that fire is treated as a managing principle, not an element.
Also note al-Amidi's variation that replaces water with air.
2
Examine the indivisible bodies theory
Read the part about 'أجسام صلبة غير متجزئة لا نهاية لها'. This is an atomistic view.
3
Examine the surfaces theory
Read the final part about 'السطوح'. This claims composition occurs through surface contact.
Note the reasoning: composition requires meeting and contact, and the first contact is between surfaces.

The author said: The contradiction to what they mentioned is that it said: why is it not permissible that they are not four? Rather the truth is one of the sayings that he mentioned now, for it is said they are one, and they differed concerning that one in five sayings: The first: that they are fire because of its intense simplicity, for there is no body purer in nature than fire, because the excessive heat in it manages the generated things, and because it transforms others into its nature, and the rest arise from fire by condensation, so they are condensed fire in varying ways. The second: air, because of its wetness and its compliance to affections, and there is no doubt that the origin must be compliant to changes, and from air fire arises by rarefying heat, so it is air that heat has rarefied, and the remaining two by condensing cold, so they are air condensed in varying degrees. The third: water, because its acceptance of rarefaction by heat and condensation by cold is sensed, so from its rarefaction arise air and fire, and from its condensation arise earth. The fourth: earth, and the rest arise by rarefaction occurring in different degrees. The fifth: the ocean because it is intermediate between the four in subtlety and density, so with increase of its subtlety it becomes air and fire, and with increase of its density it becomes water and earth. And it is said: they are not one, but rather multiplicity is necessary in them because composition in [generated things] requires multiplicity of that from which their composition [arises]. So two, on three sayings: The first: they are fire, because it is in the utmost lightness and heat, and earth because it is in the utmost heaviness and cold, and air is rarefied fire, and water is earth rarefied by mixing with fiery parts. The second: they are water and earth because generated things need the wet for affection and obtaining shapes, and the dry for preserving the shapes obtained. The third: they are earth and air for similar reasons, for air is wet and receptive of shapes easily, and earth is dry and preserves them, so water is air with intense condensation, and fire is air with intense heat. And it is said: the elements are three: earth, water, and air, because of the need of generated things for wet and dry, and fire for managing heat; and in the speech of al-Amidi, air replaced water, and therefore he said: water is condensed air, and in the speech of some of them that the three are other than fire. And it is said: the principles of compounds are not four or less than that as mentioned, but rather they are bodies, and in the speech of al-Amidi: indivisible solid substances without end. And it is said: the principles of compounds are surfaces, because composition occurs only by meeting and contact, and the first thing in that is between straight surfaces.

Overview of All Alternative Element Theories
Comprehensive listing of alternative theories on the number and nature of elements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will have a comprehensive overview of all the alternative theories to the four-element doctrine presented in this passage.

Core Idea
The four-element view was challenged by many coherent alternatives.

This lesson consolidates everything from the previous lessons, serving as a summary and review. You will see the full range of theories: one element, two elements, three elements, atoms, and surfaces. Understanding this diversity shows the depth of pre-modern scientific reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Review the list of all theories

2
Identify the common pattern
Notice that each theory is based on a fundamental principle or observation (e.g., simplicity, compliance, need for wet/dry, contact).
3
Reflect on the purpose of this critique
The passage is a 'contradiction' to the claim that there are exactly four elements. It shows that many reasonable alternatives exist.
This is a dialectical move in kalam.
