Philosophers' Classification of Elements
Authors state the philosophers' classification of elements based on moist and dry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers classified the elements using the properties of moist and dry, and the reasoning behind each pairing.

Core Idea
Elements are distinguished by their capacity to receive and preserve forms.

The philosophers approached the physical world by analyzing the fundamental building blocks of matter. This passage presents three different classifications of the elements, each grounded in the interplay of moisture and dryness. As we read, pay attention to how each pair serves a specific function in generation and preservation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation to familiarize yourself with the content. Notice the three numbered opinions.
The passage starts mid-sentence; the first opinion is briefly stated.
2
Identify the three classifications
List the three pairs of elements mentioned. For each, note the rationale given based on moist and dry.
The pairs are: (1) earth + fire, (2) water + earth, (3) earth + air.
3
Reflect on the underlying principle
Consider why moist and dry are chosen as key properties. How do they relate to passivity (receiving forms) and preservation?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قول الحكماء في أقسام العناصر أرض متخلخلة بالمزج مع أجزاء نارية ( الثاني : ( هما (الماء والأرض لافتقار الكائنات إلى الرطب للانفعال وحصول الأشكال (و) إلى ( اليابس للحفظ ) على الأشكال الحاصلة ( الثالث : ( هما ( الأرض والهواء لمثل ذلك ) فإن الهواء رطب قابل للأشكال بسهولة والأرض يابسة حافظة لها فالماء هواء اشتد تكاثفه والنار هواء اشتد حرارته .
The saying of the philosophers concerning the divisions of the elements: Earth rarefied by mixing with fiery parts. (Second: (They are (Water and Earth due to the need of generated things for moist for passivity and acquisition of shapes, and for dry for preservation of the acquired shapes. (Third: (They are (Earth and Air for the same reason; for air is moist, receptive to shapes easily, and earth is dry, preserving them. So water is air intensified in condensation, and fire is air intensified in heat.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' classification of the elements. It begins by stating the subject: the divisions of the elements. The first element mentioned is earth mixed with fiery parts, which suggests an initial combination of earth and fire. The second opinion holds that the two main elements are water and earth, because contingent beings need moisture for passivity and form acquisition, and dryness for preserving forms. The third opinion considers earth and air as fundamental, because air is moist and easily receives forms, while earth is dry and preserves them. The passage concludes by identifying water as highly condensed air and fire as extremely hot air, implying a transformation between elements.

Key Terms
عناصر
رطب
يابس
انفعال
كائنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All ancient philosophers believed in exactly four elements: earth, water, air, fire.
Correction: The passage shows that philosophers debated which elements are fundamental, with some emphasizing different pairings based on properties.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, why are water and earth considered fundamental elements?
A) Because water is hot and earth is cold
B) Because generated things need moist for passivity and shapes, and dry for preservation
C) Because air is a mixture of water and earth
D) Because fire is derived from air

Key Takeaway
The philosophers classified elements based on the properties of moisture and dryness, highlighting different fundamental pairs for generation and preservation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson says generated things need moisture to receive forms and dryness to preserve them. In your own life, are you more open to receiving new spiritual insights or more focused on preserving what you already know? Which one are you neglecting, and what is one concrete step you will take today to address that imbalance?
?
The philosophers viewed air as the base element from which water and fire are derived. If you had to choose one 'element' that shapes your daily actions—being receptive (moist), preservative (dry), or transformative (air-like)—which dominates? How might that be holding you back from growth, and what change will you commit to this week?

Alternative Views on Elements and the Impossibility of Proving Four
Alternative theories of elements and the argument against proving four.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various alternative theories about the fundamental elements and why proving the standard four-element theory requires refuting all alternatives—a task deemed impossible.

Core Idea
Proving four elements requires refuting every alternative—an impossible demand.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif surveys several alternative views on the fundamental elements of matter. Some philosophers argued for three elements, others for indivisible bodies, and still others for surfaces. After laying out these alternatives, the author makes a critical methodological point: to prove that the elements are exactly four, one must refute each of these conflicting theories with evidence. Since that is practically impossible, the standard doctrine of four elements remains open to challenge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the alternative opinions
Read the passage and list each alternative view mentioned: three elements (with variants), bodies/atoms, and surfaces.
Note that there are two variants for the three-element view: one with earth, water, air; another with earth, air, fire; al-Amidi's variant replaces water with air.
2
Understand the reasoning behind each alternative
For each view, note the brief justification given. For example, three elements are based on need for moist/dry, fire is managing heat; bodies are solid indivisible atoms; surfaces are needed for composition through contact.
3
Grasp the refutation requirement
The passage states that proving four elements requires refuting all five contrary opinions with evidence. Merely refuting some is insufficient.
The five opinions include the two variants of three elements, the body view, the surface view, and possibly the four-element view itself? Actually the target is the four-element view, so the contrary opinions are the alternatives.
4
Reflect on the impossibility
Consider why the author says complete refutation is impossible. This highlights the difficulty of establishing a definitive number of elements.
This methodological point is a key part of the passage's argument against the certainty of the four-element theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل : ( العناصر ( ثلاثة هي : الأرض والماء والهواء لما مر من افتقار الكائنات إلى رطب ويابس ) والنار للحرارة المديرة وقد وقع في كلام الآمدي الهواء بدل الماء ولذلك قال : فالماء هواء متكاثف وفي كلام بعضهم أن الثلاثة ما عدا النار ) وقيل : ( أصول المركبات ليست أربعة أو مادونها على ما مر بل هي ( أجسام) وفي كلام الآمدي جواهر (صلبة غير متجزئة لا نهاية لها ) . وقيل : أصول المركبات هي ( السطوح ) لأن التركيب إنما يكون بالتلاقي والتماس وأول ما يكون ذلك بين السطوح المستقيمة ( ولا يكفي ) في إثبات كون العناصر أربعة (إبطال بعضها ) أي بعض هذه الأقوال الخمسة المنافية له بالحجة، بل لا بد في إثباته (من إبطال الجميع وهو مما لا سبيل إليه
And it was said: The elements are three: earth, water, and air, due to the aforementioned need of generated things for moist and dry, and fire for the managing heat. And it has occurred in the speech of al-Amidi that air replaces water, and therefore he said: water is condensed air. And in the speech of some, the three are excluding fire. And it was said: The principles of compounds are not four or less than that, as previously, but they are bodies; and in the speech of al-Amidi, solid indivisible atoms without end. And it was said: The principles of compounds are surfaces, because composition only occurs through meeting and contact, and the first of that occurs between straight surfaces. And it is not sufficient in proving that the elements are four to refute some of them—i.e., some of these five opinions contradictory to it by argument—rather, it is necessary in proving it to refute all of them, and that is something impossible.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents alternative opinions on the number and nature of elements. First, some say the elements are three: earth, water, and air, with fire serving as a managing heat rather than an element. Al-Amidi is noted to replace water with air, considering water as condensed air. Others exclude fire altogether. A second opinion posits that the principles of compounds are not four but bodies—al-Amidi specifies solid, indivisible atoms without end. A third view holds that surfaces are the principles, since composition requires contact and straight surfaces. The passage then argues that proving the elements are four requires refuting each of these five alternative opinions with evidence; merely refuting some is insufficient, and complete refutation is impossible, thereby establishing a key methodological point.

Key Terms
عناصر
أصول المركبات
أجسام
جواهر
سطوح
إبطال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is sufficient to refute one alternative theory to establish the four-element doctrine.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that all alternative views must be refuted; otherwise the proof is incomplete.

Checkpoint
Why is it considered impossible to prove the elements are exactly four according to this passage?
A) Because there are only three elements
B) Because the refutation of all five alternative opinions is required and unattainable
C) Because matter is composed of surfaces
D) Because al-Amidi said air replaces water

Key Takeaway
Proving the four-element theory requires refuting all alternative theories, which is practically impossible, thus leaving the theory unproven.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a belief you hold about God or existence that you have never honestly confronted with its strongest alternative? What would it cost you to examine it as this passage demands—refuting every opposing view?
?
The passage shows that partial refutation is insufficient for certainty. In your own life, where might you be settling for partial evidence while claiming full conviction? Will you today identify one opposing idea and seek to refute it fully?
?
You may feel certain about your theological commitments. But have you ever tested them by rigorously proving that every alternative is false, or have you simply accepted them because they are familiar? What is one alternative worldview you could study with the aim of disproving it from your own sources?

The Three Opinions on Earth's Motion
Sanusi states the three opinions on earth's motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three traditional opinions about the earth's motion: stationary, rising, and falling.

Core Idea
The earth's motion is debated as falling, rising, or stationary.

Sanusi begins the sixth objective by clearly stating the three possible views on earth's motion: it is stationary, it is constantly rising, or it is perpetually falling. This sets the stage for the refutations that follow. Let's read the opening lines.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for the phrasing. Note how the author introduces the three opinions concisely.
The passage uses 'قيل' (it is said) to indicate alternative views.
2
Identify the three opinions
List the three opinions: stationary, rising, and falling. Notice that the falling opinion includes a description of perpetual descent in infinite void.
3
Understand the 'falling' view
The falling view claims the earth moves downward forever due to its weight, into an infinite void. Reflect on why someone might think this.
This is the view that will be refuted in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد السادس : الأرض ساكنة أم هاوية ] الأرض ساكنة وقيل : صاعدة، وقيل: هاوية ) أي متحركة ( إلى أسفل أبداً فلا تزال ) الأرض ( تنزل في خلاء غير متناه لما في طبعها من الاعتماد ) والثقل ( الهابط، ويبطله بيان تناهي (الأبعاد التي يتصور حركة الجسم فيها (سيما عند من يبطل الخلاء) . وأيضاً لو كانت هابطة لوجب أن تصغر أجرام الكواكب في كل يوم حسناً. ولو فرضت صاعدة دائماً لكنا كل يوم أقرب إلى الفلك فكان يزداد عظم الكواكب في قوله : (الأرض) ساكنة) القائلون بسكونها منهم من جعلها غير متناهية من جهة العقل فليس لها محيط فينزل ومنهم من قال : بتناهيها وهم فرقتان : فرقة زعموا أن ليس شكلهما الكرة فمنهم من قال : إن حدبة الأرض وسطحها أسفل وذلك السطح موضوع على الماء والهواء ومن شأن الثقيل إذا انبسط أن يتدعم على الماء والهواء مثل الرصاصة . قوله: ( ويبطله بیان تناهي (الأبعاد وهو إنما يبطله على القول بقدم الأرض وأما على القول بحدوثها فلم يلزم عدم تناهي الأبعاد وإن فرض أنها تنزل في خلاء غير متناهية. إذ يكون حينئذ ما بين المبدأ أو المنتهى متناهياً دائماً. وقوله : وأيضاً لو كانت هابطة إلخ . ولعلهم كانوا يزعمون أن الأفلاك هاوية أيضاً بقدر هوى الأرض فلم يلزم أن تصغر أجرام الكواكب في كل يوم حسناً. ثم إن هذه المنوع إنما يرد إذا كان المدعى نظرياً والحق أن كون الأرض ساكناً ضروري معلوم بالمشاهدة. فما ذكر هاهنا لبيانه إنما هو لمجرد التنبيه وكلام الخصم هاهنا كلام في مقابلة الضرورة فلا يعتد به. وقوله : لجواز أن يشايعها الهواء. فهو أيضا باطل بالمشاهدة كما لا يخفى على المصنف . إذا بسطت طفت على الماء وإن جمعت رست الأرض أسفل ووسطها فوق وهو الذي بينا وفرقة قالت بكرويتها فمنهم من جعل سكونها بسبب جذب الفلك لها من جميع الجوانب، ومنهم من زعم أن وقع الفلك بحركة من كل الجوانب.
The sixth objective: Is the earth stationary or falling? The earth is stationary, and it is said: rising, and it is said: falling, i.e. moving downwards forever; the earth does not cease descending in infinite void because of its inclination and heavy weight that falls. And it is refuted by the exposition of the finitude of dimensions in which the movement of a body is conceivable, especially for those who reject the void. Also, if it were falling, it would be necessary that the sizes of the planets become smaller every day visibly. And if it were assumed to be constantly rising, we would every day be closer to the sphere, so the size of the planets would increase in His saying: (The earth) stationary. Those who say it is stationary: among them are those who consider it infinite from the aspect of intellect, so it has no circumference that would descend; and among them are those who say it is finite, and they are two groups: a group claimed that its shape is not spherical, so among them are those who said that the convexity of the earth and its surface are lower, and that surface is placed on water and air, and the nature of a heavy thing when spread out is to become supported on water and air, like a bullet. His saying: (And it is refuted by the exposition of the finitude of dimensions) and it is only refuted on the view that the earth is eternal; as for the view that it is originated, the non-finitude of dimensions does not follow, even if it is assumed to descend in infinite void, because then what is between the beginning or the end is always finite. And His saying: Also, if it were falling etc. Perhaps they claim that the spheres are also falling by the amount of the earth's fall, so it would not be necessary that the sizes of the planets become smaller every day visibly. Then these objections only apply if the claim is theoretical, but the truth is that the earth being stationary is necessary, known by observation. What is mentioned here for its demonstration is only for mere attention, and the speech of the opponent here is speech in opposition to necessity, so it is not regarded. And His saying: It is possible that the air accompanies it. That is also invalid by observation, as is not hidden from the author. If it spreads out, it floats on water, and if it is gathered, it sinks. The earth is lower and its center is above, and that is what we have explained; and a group said it is spherical, and among them are those who made its stationariness due to the attraction of the sphere from all sides, and among them are those who claimed that the sphere presses it with motion from all sides.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage opens the sixth objective, which investigates whether the earth is stationary, rising, or falling. The author begins by stating the three opinions: stationary, rising, and falling. He then refutes the theory that the earth is falling (or rising) using two arguments. First, if the earth were falling into infinite void, the dimensions of space must be finite for motion to occur, but the void is infinite, so the argument is problematic; however, he notes that this refutation only holds if the earth is eternal, not if it is created. Second, if the earth were falling, the planets would appear to shrink daily, which is not observed. The author then turns to the opinions of those who hold the earth stationary. He divides them into those who consider the earth infinite (no circumference) and those who consider it finite. Among the latter are those who deny its sphericity, claiming a flat earth resting on water and air, comparing it to a bullet that spreads out. Others affirm sphericity, and they attribute the earth's stationariness to attraction by the sphere or pressure from all sides. The author concludes this section by asserting that the earth's stationariness is known by necessity and observation, so the theoretical arguments are mere illustrations. The final example of a bullet spreading out on water illustrates the flat-earth view.

Key Terms
هاوية
صاعدة
خلاء غير متناه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many opinions about the earth's motion does Sanusi mention at the beginning?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) One

Key Takeaway
The earth's motion has three traditional views: stationary, rising, and falling.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read these three opinions on the earth's motion, which one feels most natural to you? Why do you think that is? What does your gut reaction reveal about your underlying assumptions about Allah's creation and His power?
?
The 'falling' view describes an eternal descent through an infinite void. Does the idea of an endless physical process conflict with your understanding of a purposeful creation? How does this challenge you to think more deeply about the relationship between reason and revelation?
?
The author presents three opinions without immediately refuting them. If you were in his place, how would you begin to sift through these views to find the truth? What principles would guide you? Commit to applying those same principles when you encounter differing opinions in your own life.

Refutation of Falling and Rising Theories
Sanusi refutes the falling and rising theories with two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main arguments Sanusi uses to refute the falling and rising theories of the earth.

Core Idea
Falling earth is refuted by the finitude of dimensions and the observed constancy of planet sizes.

After presenting the opinions, Sanusi now refutes the falling and rising theories with two arguments. The first relies on the finitude of dimensions, and the second uses the observable fact that planet sizes do not change daily. Let's examine these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic text that begins with 'ويبطله' (and it is refuted by...). Note the two distinct arguments: one about dimensions and one about planetary sizes.
2
Understand the first argument
The first argument: the earth cannot fall infinitely because dimensions are finite, especially if one rejects the void. Ask yourself why infinite descent would require infinite dimensions.
This argument is especially relevant for those who deny the existence of void.
3
Understand the second argument
The second argument: if the earth were falling, the planets would appear smaller daily; if rising, they would appear larger. Since this is not observed, the theories fail.
This uses empirical observation against the theories.

?

Opinions on Earth's Shape: Flat Earth
Sanusi classifies views on the stationary earth: infinite vs finite, flat vs spherical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various opinions on the earth's shape among those who consider it finite, especially the flat earth view.

Core Idea
Proponents of a stationary earth disagree on whether it is flat or spherical.

Having refuted the falling and rising theories, Sanusi now turns to those who affirm the earth's stationariness. Among them, there is a range of opinions: some say the earth is infinite, others finite. Within the finite group, some deny sphericity and propose a flat earth resting on water and air. Let's explore these views.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the Arabic that begins with 'القائلون بسكونها' (those who say it is stationary). Note the hierarchy: infinite vs finite, then spherical vs flat.
2
Understand the infinite view
Some say the earth is infinite in extent, so it has no circumference to fall from. Ponder why this would make it stationary.
This view avoids the falling problem entirely.
3
Understand the finite flat view
Among those who say finite, a group denies sphericity and says the earth's convex surface is below, resting on water and air, like a bullet that flattens and floats.
This is a sophisticated version of a flat-earth model.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The sixth objective: Is the earth stationary or falling? The earth is stationary, and it is said: rising, and it is said: falling, i.e. moving downwards forever; the earth does not cease descending in infinite void because of its inclination and heavy weight that falls. And it is refuted by the exposition of the finitude of dimensions in which the movement of a body is conceivable, especially for those who reject the void. Also, if it were falling, it would be necessary that the sizes of the planets become smaller every day visibly. And if it were assumed to be constantly rising, we would every day be closer to the sphere, so the size of the planets would increase in His saying: (The earth) stationary. Those who say it is stationary: among them are those who consider it infinite from the aspect of intellect, so it has no circumference that would descend; and among them are those who say it is finite, and they are two groups: a group claimed that its shape is not spherical, so among them are those who said that the convexity of the earth and its surface are lower, and that surface is placed on water and air, and the nature of a heavy thing when spread out is to become supported on water and air, like a bullet. His saying: (And it is refuted by the exposition of the finitude of dimensions) and it is only refuted on the view that the earth is eternal; as for the view that it is originated, the non-finitude of dimensions does not follow, even if it is assumed to descend in infinite void, because then what is between the beginning or the end is always finite. And His saying: Also, if it were falling etc. Perhaps they claim that the spheres are also falling by the amount of the earth's fall, so it would not be necessary that the sizes of the planets become smaller every day visibly. Then these objections only apply if the claim is theoretical, but the truth is that the earth being stationary is necessary, known by observation. What is mentioned here for its demonstration is only for mere attention, and the speech of the opponent here is speech in opposition to necessity, so it is not regarded. And His saying: It is possible that the air accompanies it. That is also invalid by observation, as is not hidden from the author. If it spreads out, it floats on water, and if it is gathered, it sinks. The earth is lower and its center is above, and that is what we have explained; and a group said it is spherical, and among them are those who made its stationariness due to the attraction of the sphere from all sides, and among them are those who claimed that the sphere presses it with motion from all sides.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage opens the sixth objective, which investigates whether the earth is stationary, rising, or falling. The author begins by stating the three opinions: stationary, rising, and falling. He then refutes the theory that the earth is falling (or rising) using two arguments. First, if the earth were falling into infinite void, the dimensions of space must be finite for motion to occur, but the void is infinite, so the argument is problematic; however, he notes that this refutation only holds if the earth is eternal, not if it is created. Second, if the earth were falling, the planets would appear to shrink daily, which is not observed. The author then turns to the opinions of those who hold the earth stationary. He divides them into those who consider the earth infinite (no circumference) and those who consider it finite. Among the latter are those who deny its sphericity, claiming a flat earth resting on water and air, comparing it to a bullet that spreads out. Others affirm sphericity, and they attribute the earth's stationariness to attraction by the sphere or pressure from all sides. The author concludes this section by asserting that the earth's stationariness is known by necessity and observation, so the theoretical arguments are mere illustrations. The final example of a bullet spreading out on water illustrates the flat-earth view.

Key Terms
حدبة الأرض
ماء
هواء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the flat earth view, on what does the earth rest?
A) On a giant tortoise
B) On water and air
C) On pillars
D) On nothing

Key Takeaway
Some proponents of a stationary earth held a finite, flat model resting on water and air.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed an ancient belief (like the flat earth) as foolish without understanding the reasoning behind it? What does that reveal about your own humility in the face of differing worldviews? Will you commit to seeking first to understand before judging?

Spherical Earth and the Necessity of Observation
Sanusi concludes that earth's rest is observed and presents spherical earth theories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the earth's stationariness is known by immediate observation, not mere speculation, and that some argued for a spherical earth with celestial influences.

Core Idea
The earth's rest is evident to the senses; theoretical arguments are just confirmations.

Sanusi concludes the objective by emphasizing that the earth's state of rest is a necessary truth known by direct observation, not something that requires elaborate proof. He also presents the spherical earth theories, which attribute its stationary position to attraction or pressure from the celestial sphere. Let's see his final remarks.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding passage
Read from 'ثم إن هذه المنوع' to the end. Note the shift: the author asserts that the stationariness is known by necessity and observation.
2
Understand the epistemological stance
The author argues that the arguments given are just illustrations, because the truth is self-evident. The opponent's speech is against necessity and should be ignored.
This is a strong statement about the role of kalam arguments.
3
Learn the spherical earth theories
Two explanations: either the sphere attracts the earth from all sides, or it presses upon it uniformly, keeping it centered.
These reflect ancient Greek and Islamic cosmological ideas.

The Rotation Argument and the Ship Analogy
The rotation argument and the ship analogy to explain apparent sky motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the claim that Earth rotates daily and how the ship analogy is used to argue that the sky's motion is an optical illusion.

Core Idea
Earth's rotation turns the sky's motion into a mere illusion, like a ship passenger seeing the shore move.

Before we examine the refutation, we need to understand the argument for Earth's rotation. The author presents a claim that the apparent daily motion of the sky is not real but arises from Earth's own rotation. A vivid analogy—a passenger on a ship—illustrates how we might mistake our own motion for that of our surroundings. This lesson unpacks that claim and its key analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the rotation claim
Read the Arabic sentence starting with 'وقيل : إنها تدور' and its translation. Note that the claim says Earth rotates from west to east.
2
Identify the optical illusion
The author says the daily motion of the sky does not actually exist; it is imagined because of Earth's motion. Look for the phrase 'الحركة اليومية لا توجد'.
The sky appears to move, but that is relative to us.
3
Consider the ship analogy
Read the analogy of the ship passenger. The passenger sees the ship as stationary and the shore as moving. This parallels how we see the sky moving while Earth remains still in our perception.
The analogy uses 'كراكب السفينة' as a key comparison.
4
Reflect on the implications
Think about what this argument implies: there is no outermost sphere moving daily; the motion is only apparent. The author lists other examples (moon and cloud) to reinforce the idea.
This sets the stage for the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل : إنها تدور متحركة ( على ) مركز ( نفسها من المغرب إلى المشرق خلاف الحركة اليومية ( التي اعتقدها الجمهور ( والحركة اليومية لا توجد ) على هذا التقدير ( وإنما تتخيل بسبب حركة الأرض إذ يتبدل الوضع من الفلك ) بالقياس إلينا ) دون أجزاء الأرض) إذ لا يتغير الوضع بيننا وبينها. فإنا على جزء معين منها فإذا تحركت من المغرب إلى المشرق ظهر علينا من جانب المشرق كواكب كانت مختفية عنا بحدبة الأرض وخفي عنا بحدبتها من جانب المغرب كواكب كانت ظاهرة علينا . ( فيظن ) لذلك ) أن الأرض ساكنة ) في مكانها ) والمتحرك هو الفلك ) فيكون حينئذ متحركاً من المشرق إلى المغرب ) بل ليس ثمة فلك أطلس) حتى يتحرك بالحركة اليومية على خلاف التوالي ) وذلك كراكب السفينة) فإنه يرى السفينة ساكنة مع حركتها حيث لا يتبدل وضع أجزائها منه و ) يرى (الشط متحر و) متحركاً مع سكونه حيث يتبدل وضعه منه مع ظن أنه ساكن في مكانه . أي ليس متحركاً أصلاً لا بالذات ولا بالعرض ) وكذلك يرى القمر سائرا إلى الغيم حين يسير الغيم إليه و ) كذا يرى ( غيره ) متحركاً مع سكونه أو ساكناً مع حركته ( من أمور قدمناها في غلط الحس، وأبطلوا ذلك أي تحركها على الاستدارة كما زعمه هذا القائل ( بوجوه ) ثلاثة : ( الأول : إن الأرض لو كانت متحركة في اليوم بليلة دورة واحدة لكان ينبغي أن السهم إذا رمي إلى جهة حركة (الأرض وهي الشرق ( أن لا يسبق موضعه الذي رمى منه بل تسبقه الأرض، وذلك لأن الأرض على ذلك التقدير تقطع في ساعة واحدة ألف ميل وفي عشر ساعة مائة ،ميل ولا يتصور في السهم وغيره من المتحركات السفلية حركة بهذه السرعة فيجب تخلفها عن الأرض. (و) ينبغي للسهم (إذا رمي إلى خلاف جهة ( حركتها أن يمر) عن الموضع الذي منه رمي ويتجاوزه ( بقدر حركته وحركة الأرض جميعاً. واللازم باطل لاستواء المسافة ) التي يقطها السهم ( من الجانبين بالتجربة ) . الوجه ( الثاني : الحجر يرمي إلى فوق فيعود إلى موضعه الذي رمي منه ( راجعا بخط مستقيم ولو كانت الأرض متحركة إلى المشرق لكان ( الحجر ( ينزل من مكانه إلى جانب المغرب بقدر حركة الأرض في الأرض أسفل ووسطها فوق وهو الذي بينا وفرقة قالت بكرويتها فمنهم من جعل سكونها بسبب جذب الفلك لها من جميع الجوانب، ومنهم من زعم أن وقع الفلك بحركة من كل الجوانب. والقائلون بحركتها فمنهم من قال بحركتها المستديرة فهذه هي الوجوه الفاسدة في سكونها وحركتها، والحق أنها ساكنة لكونها حاصلة في حيزها الطبيعي .
And it is said that it rotates, moving on its center from west to east contrary to the daily motion which the majority believed. The daily motion does not exist on this assumption but is imagined due to the earth's motion, as the position relative to the sphere changes with respect to us but not to the parts of the earth, because the position between us and it does not change. For we are on a specific part of it, so if it moves from west to east, there appear to us from the east side planets that were hidden from us by the earth's convexity, and there disappear from us by its convexity from the west side planets that were visible to us. So it is therefore thought that the earth is stationary in its place and the moving thing is the sphere, so then it is moving from east to west; rather there is no outermost sphere that moves with the daily motion contrary to the order. This is like the ship passenger, for he sees the ship stationary with its motion when the position of its parts does not change from him, and he sees the shore moving with its rest when its position changes from him, while thinking that he is stationary in his place, i.e. not moving at all, neither essentially nor accidentally. Likewise he sees the moon moving towards the cloud when the cloud moves towards it, and likewise he sees other things moving while at rest or at rest while moving, from matters we have presented in the error of the senses. And they refuted that, i.e. its circular motion as this claimant claimed, with three aspects: The first: If the earth were moving in a day and night one complete rotation, then it would be necessary that if an arrow is shot towards the direction of the earth's motion (which is the east) it should not precede its place from which it was shot, but rather the earth would precede it, because the earth on that assumption covers a thousand miles in one hour and ten miles in ten seconds[?], and it is not conceivable for the arrow and other sublunary moving objects to have motion at this speed, so they must lag behind the earth. And it would be necessary for the arrow if shot opposite the direction of its motion to pass beyond the place from which it was shot and exceed it by the amount of its own motion and the earth's motion together. The consequence is false because the distance the arrow covers from both sides is equal by experiment. The second aspect: A stone is thrown upward and returns to its place from which it was thrown, coming back in a straight line. If the earth were moving towards the east, the stone would descend from its place to the west side by the amount of the earth's motion in... The earth is lower and its center is above, and that is what we have explained; and a group said it is spherical, and among them are those who made its stationariness due to the attraction of the sphere from all sides, and among them are those who claimed that the sphere presses it with motion from all sides. And those who say it moves, among them are those who say it has circular motion. These are the invalid aspects in its stationariness and its motion, and the truth is that it is stationary because it is located in its natural place.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage presents the argument for the earth's rotation and its refutation. It begins by describing the claim that the earth rotates on its axis from west to east, which would make the apparent daily motion of the sky illusory, like a ship passenger who feels stationary while the shore moves. The author explains that on this view, the sky's motion is just an optical illusion caused by the earth's rotation, and there is no outermost sphere. He then provides two refutations (out of three claimed). The first refutation uses the example of an arrow shot east or west: if the earth spins rapidly, an arrow shot east should fall behind because the earth moves faster; an arrow shot west should travel farther. But experiment shows equal distances. The second refutation, which is incomplete, considers a stone thrown straight up: if the earth moves east, the stone should land to the west. The passage then concludes with a summary: among those who say the earth is stationary, some hold it stationary because of attraction or pressure from the sphere; those who say it moves propose circular motion. The author declares all these opinions invalid, affirming that the earth is stationary in its natural place.

الحركة اليومية
فلك أطلس
كراكب السفينة
غلط الحس

Key Takeaway
The rotation claim uses the ship analogy to argue that the sky's daily motion is an illusion produced by Earth's own rotation from west to east.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that our perception of the sky's motion can be an illusion. When have you been absolutely certain about something, only to discover later that your perspective was limited or distorted? What daily ‘certainties’ might you need to reexamine in light of Allah's greater reality?
?
The ship passenger sees the shore moving while feeling stationary. In your spiritual life, where might you be wrongly attributing movement to external factors when the real change needs to happen within you?
?
The analogy warns that our senses can mislead us. Are there aspects of your faith where you rely too heavily on what you see or feel, rather than on the unchanging truth of revelation? What step will you take today to align your perception with the truth, even if it feels uncomfortable?

Refutation of Earth's Rotation: Arrow and Stone Proofs
Two refutations of Earth's rotation (arrow and stone) and the final conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two key refutations of Earth's rotation—the arrow experiment and the stone drop—and the author's conclusion that Earth is stationary in its natural place.

Core Idea
If Earth rotated, arrows and falling stones would behave differently; experiments show otherwise, so Earth must be still.

Having presented the rotation claim, the author now refutes it with three 'aspects' (wujuh). The first is a complete argument based on shooting an arrow in opposite directions. The second, concerning a stone thrown upward, is incomplete in our text but still conveys the idea. The author then rejects all alternative views and affirms the traditional position: Earth is stationary because it resides in its natural place.

Guided Path
1
Read the first refutation (arrow)
Read the Arabic from 'وأبطلوا ذلك بوجوه ثلاثة' through the first wajh up to 'بالتجربة'. Understand the logic: if Earth spins rapidly, an arrow shot east should fall short, and an arrow shot west should overshoot.
The key assumption is Earth's speed: 1000 miles per hour.
2
Examine the second refutation (stone)
Read the fragmentary second wajh. Despite the incomplete text, the idea is clear: a stone thrown vertically should not return to its starting point if Earth moves eastward.
The text cuts off; we treat this as proof-by-concept.
3
Review the conclusion
Read the final sentences starting from 'والحق أنها ساكنة'. The author dismisses all alternative theories (attraction, pressure, circular motion) and states the truth: Earth is still because it is in its natural place.
This conclusion relies on Aristotelian physics of natural places.
4
Consider the three aspects as a whole
Note that the author claims three refutations but only the first is fully given. The second is fragmentary, and the third is not mentioned in our text. This is a reminder of the text's incomplete state.
In a complete text, the third aspect would be present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل : إنها تدور متحركة ( على ) مركز ( نفسها من المغرب إلى المشرق خلاف الحركة اليومية ( التي اعتقدها الجمهور ( والحركة اليومية لا توجد ) على هذا التقدير ( وإنما تتخيل بسبب حركة الأرض إذ يتبدل الوضع من الفلك ) بالقياس إلينا ) دون أجزاء الأرض) إذ لا يتغير الوضع بيننا وبينها. فإنا على جزء معين منها فإذا تحركت من المغرب إلى المشرق ظهر علينا من جانب المشرق كواكب كانت مختفية عنا بحدبة الأرض وخفي عنا بحدبتها من جانب المغرب كواكب كانت ظاهرة علينا . ( فيظن ) لذلك ) أن الأرض ساكنة ) في مكانها ) والمتحرك هو الفلك ) فيكون حينئذ متحركاً من المشرق إلى المغرب ) بل ليس ثمة فلك أطلس) حتى يتحرك بالحركة اليومية على خلاف التوالي ) وذلك كراكب السفينة) فإنه يرى السفينة ساكنة مع حركتها حيث لا يتبدل وضع أجزائها منه و ) يرى (الشط متحر و) متحركاً مع سكونه حيث يتبدل وضعه منه مع ظن أنه ساكن في مكانه . أي ليس متحركاً أصلاً لا بالذات ولا بالعرض ) وكذلك يرى القمر سائرا إلى الغيم حين يسير الغيم إليه و ) كذا يرى ( غيره ) متحركاً مع سكونه أو ساكناً مع حركته ( من أمور قدمناها في غلط الحس، وأبطلوا ذلك أي تحركها على الاستدارة كما زعمه هذا القائل ( بوجوه ) ثلاثة : ( الأول : إن الأرض لو كانت متحركة في اليوم بليلة دورة واحدة لكان ينبغي أن السهم إذا رمي إلى جهة حركة (الأرض وهي الشرق ( أن لا يسبق موضعه الذي رمى منه بل تسبقه الأرض، وذلك لأن الأرض على ذلك التقدير تقطع في ساعة واحدة ألف ميل وفي عشر ساعة مائة ،ميل ولا يتصور في السهم وغيره من المتحركات السفلية حركة بهذه السرعة فيجب تخلفها عن الأرض. (و) ينبغي للسهم (إذا رمي إلى خلاف جهة ( حركتها أن يمر) عن الموضع الذي منه رمي ويتجاوزه ( بقدر حركته وحركة الأرض جميعاً. واللازم باطل لاستواء المسافة ) التي يقطها السهم ( من الجانبين بالتجربة ) . الوجه ( الثاني : الحجر يرمي إلى فوق فيعود إلى موضعه الذي رمي منه ( راجعا بخط مستقيم ولو كانت الأرض متحركة إلى المشرق لكان ( الحجر ( ينزل من مكانه إلى جانب المغرب بقدر حركة الأرض في الأرض أسفل ووسطها فوق وهو الذي بينا وفرقة قالت بكرويتها فمنهم من جعل سكونها بسبب جذب الفلك لها من جميع الجوانب، ومنهم من زعم أن وقع الفلك بحركة من كل الجوانب. والقائلون بحركتها فمنهم من قال بحركتها المستديرة فهذه هي الوجوه الفاسدة في سكونها وحركتها، والحق أنها ساكنة لكونها حاصلة في حيزها الطبيعي .
And it is said that it rotates, moving on its center from west to east contrary to the daily motion which the majority believed. The daily motion does not exist on this assumption but is imagined due to the earth's motion, as the position relative to the sphere changes with respect to us but not to the parts of the earth, because the position between us and it does not change. For we are on a specific part of it, so if it moves from west to east, there appear to us from the east side planets that were hidden from us by the earth's convexity, and there disappear from us by its convexity from the west side planets that were visible to us. So it is therefore thought that the earth is stationary in its place and the moving thing is the sphere, so then it is moving from east to west; rather there is no outermost sphere that moves with the daily motion contrary to the order. This is like the ship passenger, for he sees the ship stationary with its motion when the position of its parts does not change from him, and he sees the shore moving with its rest when its position changes from him, while thinking that he is stationary in his place, i.e. not moving at all, neither essentially nor accidentally. Likewise he sees the moon moving towards the cloud when the cloud moves towards it, and likewise he sees other things moving while at rest or at rest while moving, from matters we have presented in the error of the senses. And they refuted that, i.e. its circular motion as this claimant claimed, with three aspects: The first: If the earth were moving in a day and night one complete rotation, then it would be necessary that if an arrow is shot towards the direction of the earth's motion (which is the east) it should not precede its place from which it was shot, but rather the earth would precede it, because the earth on that assumption covers a thousand miles in one hour and ten miles in ten seconds[?], and it is not conceivable for the arrow and other sublunary moving objects to have motion at this speed, so they must lag behind the earth. And it would be necessary for the arrow if shot opposite the direction of its motion to pass beyond the place from which it was shot and exceed it by the amount of its own motion and the earth's motion together. The consequence is false because the distance the arrow covers from both sides is equal by experiment. The second aspect: A stone is thrown upward and returns to its place from which it was thrown, coming back in a straight line. If the earth were moving towards the east, the stone would descend from its place to the west side by the amount of the earth's motion in... The earth is lower and its center is above, and that is what we have explained; and a group said it is spherical, and among them are those who made its stationariness due to the attraction of the sphere from all sides, and among them are those who claimed that the sphere presses it with motion from all sides. And those who say it moves, among them are those who say it has circular motion. These are the invalid aspects in its stationariness and its motion, and the truth is that it is stationary because it is located in its natural place.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage presents the argument for the earth's rotation and its refutation. It begins by describing the claim that the earth rotates on its axis from west to east, which would make the apparent daily motion of the sky illusory, like a ship passenger who feels stationary while the shore moves. The author explains that on this view, the sky's motion is just an optical illusion caused by the earth's rotation, and there is no outermost sphere. He then provides two refutations (out of three claimed). The first refutation uses the example of an arrow shot east or west: if the earth spins rapidly, an arrow shot east should fall behind because the earth moves faster; an arrow shot west should travel farther. But experiment shows equal distances. The second refutation, which is incomplete, considers a stone thrown straight up: if the earth moves east, the stone should land to the west. The passage then concludes with a summary: among those who say the earth is stationary, some hold it stationary because of attraction or pressure from the sphere; those who say it moves propose circular motion. The author declares all these opinions invalid, affirming that the earth is stationary in its natural place.

Key Terms
السهم
الحجر
التجربة
الحيز الطبيعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Earth rotates from west to east, causing the sky's motion.
Correction: The author refutes this with experimental arguments (arrow, stone) and concludes Earth is stationary in its natural place.

Checkpoint
What does the arrow experiment supposedly demonstrate about Earth's motion?
A) Earth moves very fast
B) Earth does not move
C) Arrows fly faster eastward
D) The experiment is inconclusive

Key Takeaway
Using empirical reasoning with arrows and stones, the author refutes Earth's rotation and reaffirms the doctrine that Earth is stationary in its natural place.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Are you willing to submit to this teaching as a matter of faith, or do you need further proof?
?

What does your hesitation reveal about your trust in the scholars of the past and your attachment to contemporary views?

Mizaj and Imtizaj: Definition and Al-Razi's Analysis
Defines mizaj and imtizaj, clarifies similarity, raises an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between mizaj (temperament) and imtizaj (mixing), the definition of mizaj as a similar quality from interaction of elements, and al-Razi's clarification on why it is called similar, as well as an objection regarding the necessity of all four moderate qualities.

Core Idea
Mizaj is the harmonious quality from balanced interaction of all four qualities, not the mixing process.

This passage formally defines mizaj and distinguishes it from imtizaj. Imam al-Razi offers a crucial clarification on what 'similarity' means in this context, and a later note raises an important objection about the definition only applying when all four moderate qualities are present. Understanding this helps grasp the physical basis of temperament in Islamic medicine and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Note the key terms and overall flow.
The passage includes a commentary note from the text; pay attention to the objection at the end.
2
Distinguish mizaj from imtizaj
Notice that imtizaj is defined as the process leading to the quality, while mizaj is the resulting quality itself. Understand the difference.
3
Examine al-Razi's clarification on similarity
Al-Razi explains that the quality is called 'similar' because each part has a quality equal in species, not identical in individual. This resolves a potential confusion.
4
Consider the objection
The note argues that the definition might mistakenly include composites of only two or three moderate qualities, but true mizaj requires all four. Reflect on why this matters.
This objection highlights the precision of the classical definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قبل ذلك الاجتماع المؤدي إلى الكيفية المذكورة ( يسمى امتزاجاً) واختلاطاً لا مزاجاً. فحد المزاج) بناء على ما تقرر ) بأنه كيفية متشابهة تحصل من تفاعل عناصر متصغرة الأجزاء المتماسة ( بحيث تكسر سورة كل منها ( سورة كيفية الآخر . قال الإمام الرازي : لا شبهة في أن الشيء لا يوصف بكونه مشابهاً لنفسه. وإنما قلنا: للكيفية المزاجية إنها متشابهة لأن كل جزء من أجزاء المركب ممتاز بحقيقته عن الآخر فتكون الكيفية القائمة به غير الكيفية القائمة بالآخر. إلا أن تلك الكيفيات القائمة بتلك الأجزاء متساوية في النوع وهذا معنى تشابهها. وقال أيضاً: الكاسر ليس هو الكيفية لأن انكسار الكيفيتين المتضادتين إما معا أو على التعاقب، فإن حصل الانكساران معاً والعلة واجبة الحصول مع المعلول لزم أن تكون الكيفيتان الكاسرتان قوله : فحد المزاج بأنه كيفية متشابهة يمكن تطبيق هذا الحد على كل واحد من الاحتمالين المذكورين آنفا . لكن على الاحتمال الأول يتوجه على هذا الحد النقض بالمجموع المركب من اليبوسة والرطوبة المتوسطتين، أو من الحرارة والبرودة المتوسطتين أو من الثلاثة المتوسطة منها إذ يصدق على كل واحد من هذه المجموعات أنه كيفية متشابهة تحصل من تفاعل عناصر متصغرة الأجزاء مع أن المزاج عندهم هو الحقيقة الملتئمة من الكيفيات الأربع المتوسطة دون الملتئمة من الاثنين أو الثلاث
And what is before that union leading to the mentioned quality is called imtizāj (mixing) and khalṭ (blending), not mizāj. So the definition of mizāj, based on what has been established, is: a similar quality that arises from the interaction of elements with small parts that are in contact, such that each breaks the intensity of the other's form. Imam al-Rāzī said: There is no doubt that a thing is not described as being similar to itself. Rather, we said that the mizājī quality is similar because each part of the compound is distinguished in its essence from another, so the quality subsisting in it is different from the quality subsisting in the other. However, those qualities subsisting in the parts are equal in species, and that is the meaning of their similarity. He also said: The breaker is not the quality, because the breaking of two opposing qualities occurs either together or successively. If the two breakings occur together and the cause necessarily accompanies the effect, then the two breaking qualities would be... His saying: 'The definition of mizāj as a similar quality' can be applied to each of the two probabilities mentioned earlier. However, on the first probability, a refutation can be directed against this definition by the composite formed from the moderate dryness and wetness, or from the moderate heat and cold, or from the three moderate ones, because each of these composites would be described as a similar quality arising from interaction of elements with small parts, whereas according to them, mizāj is the reality composed of the four moderate qualities, not that composed of two or three.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage provides the formal definition of mizāj and distinguishes it from imtizāj (the mixing process). Mizāj is defined as a similar quality resulting from the interaction of finely divided elements in contact, where each breaks the other's intensity. Al-Rāzī then clarifies that similarity does not mean identity; each part has its own individual quality, but these qualities are equal in species, hence similar. He begins an argument that the 'breaker' is not the quality itself (text cuts off). A following note raises an objection against the definition under the first probability: the definition would incorrectly include composites of only two or three moderate qualities, whereas true mizāj requires all four moderate qualities coming together.

Key Terms
المزاج
الامتزاج
الكيفية المتشابهة
الانكسار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that mizaj can be formed from only two or three moderate qualities, such as moderate heat and cold alone.
Correction: The passage clarifies that true mizaj is the reality composed of all four moderate qualities (heat, cold, dryness, wetness) in balanced proportion, not just two or three.

Checkpoint
According to al-Rāzī, why is the mizājī quality considered 'similar'?
A) Because all parts have exactly the same individual quality.
B) Because the qualities in each part are the same in species, not in individual.
C) Because the qualities are broken and become identical.
D) Because the parts are in contact.

Key Takeaway
Mizaj is the similar quality resulting from interaction of all four moderate qualities, not the mixing process itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You learned that true mizaj requires all four moderate qualities, not just two or three. In your own life, do you seek balance by addressing only some aspects (e.g., only work and worship) while neglecting others (e.g., rest or relationships)? What would it take to pursue a complete, four-fold balance as this lesson describes?
?
Al-Razi distinguishes the process of mixing (imtizaj) from the resulting state (mizaj). Are you more focused on the actions you do (the process) or on the inner state of your heart (the result)? Reflect: Which does Allah evaluate more, and are you prioritizing the right one?
?
The lesson emphasizes that the mizaji quality is similar but not identical across parts. Do you expect every area of your life to feel the same or look the same? How can you accept that different roles (as a parent, worker, worshipper) are equal in worth but distinct in expression, without forcing artificial uniformity?

The Problem of Two Pure Qualities Breaking Each Other
Sets up the problem that leads to four objections about forms and qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why two pure qualities cannot break each other and why the form, not the quality, must be the agent of change.

Core Idea
Pure qualities cannot break each other; the form is the real agent of change.

This passage from the philosophical tradition poses a logical puzzle about how qualities interact in simple bodies. Before we can understand the four objections that follow, we need to grasp the core problem: if two pure qualities could break each other, we would face a circularity. The solution points to the form as the true cause of qualitative change.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense, logical style of the argument.
2
Identify the impossible scenario
The passage begins by stating that if two pure qualities break each other simultaneously, that is impossible. Then it considers sequential breaking, which leads to circularity. Find both impossibilities.
3
Understand the solution and the definition
The passage concludes that the breaker must be the form, not the quality, because qualities do not admit intensification or diminution. Breaking is defined as removal of pure qualities from simples. Grasp this reasoning.
This solution sets the stage for four objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
موجودتين على صرافتهما عند حصول انكساريهما وهو محال وإن كان انكسار إحداهما متقدماً على انكسار الأخرى لزم أن يعود المكسور المغلوب كاسراً غالباً . وهو أيضاً باطل فوجب أن يكون الكاسر هو الصورة التي هي مبادئ الكيفيات، وأما المنكسر فليس أيضاً الكيفية لأن الكيفية الواحدة بالذات لا يعرض لها الاشتداد والتنقص بل هما يعرضان لمحلها فالانكسار عبارة عن زوال الكيفيات الصرفة عن تلك البسائط ) والإشكال عليه ) . أي على ما قالوه من وجوه ) أربعة :
Two [qualities] existing in their pure state at the occurrence of their breaking each other, and that is impossible. And if the breaking of one precedes the breaking of the other, the broken and defeated would become the victorious breaker. That is also false. So it is necessary that the breaker be the form which is the principle of the qualities, while the broken is not the quality, because a single quality in itself does not admit intensification and diminution; rather they occur to its subject. So breaking is the removal of pure qualities from those simples. And the objection to it—that is, to what they said—has four aspects:
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage defines a problem in the theory of forms and qualities. It begins with a hypothetical: if two pure qualities are present and cause breakage in each other, this is impossible because if one break occurs first, the broken quality would become the breaker, which is circular. Therefore the actual breaker must be the form (the substantial principle), not the quality itself. The quality does not undergo intensification or diminution; these occur in its subject. The breaking is the removal of the pure quality from the simple body. This problem leads to four objections.

Key Terms
انكسار
صرافة
الكيفية
الصورة
اشتداد
تنقص
بسائط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The pure qualities here are accidental modifications that can act on each other.
Correction: The passage treats pure qualities as essential properties of simples (elements). They are not agents; change is attributed to the form.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can the quality itself not be the agent of breakage?
A) Because qualities are not real.
B) Because qualities do not admit intensification and diminution.
C) Because qualities are always pure.
D) Because forms are not principles.

Key Takeaway
The problem shows that qualitative change in simples cannot be due to the qualities themselves but must be caused by the underlying form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Write a specific commitment to examine the root cause of one such change this week, rather than just blaming the quality itself.
?
If the form (الصورة) is the real agent of change, then what does that imply about your perception of external appearances? Are you more focused on the surface-level qualities of people and situations than on the underlying essence? How can you train yourself to seek the 'form' behind the qualities you encounter daily? End with a concrete action you will take tomorrow to practice this.

Intensification and Diminution as Motions in Quality
Definition of intensification and diminution as motions in quality attributed to the subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of intensification and diminution as motions in quality, and why they are attributed to the subject rather than the quality itself.

Core Idea
Intensification is motion, not a change in the essence of the quality.

This lesson unpacks a key passage from the commentary that defines intensification and diminution. It clarifies that these are forms of motion in quality that occur to the subject (the thing possessing the quality), not to the quality as an abstract attribute. Understanding this distinction is essential for later theological arguments about change and persistence.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Focus on the definitions of intensification (اشتداد) as motion from a lower to a stronger quality, and diminution (تنقص) as the reverse.
Notice that both are categorized as motion in quality (حركة في الكيف).
2
Identify the subject of motion
The passage argues that motion in quality occurs to the subject (محل الكيف), not to the quality itself. Ask yourself: why must the subject persist for motion to occur?
The key phrase: 'معروض الحركة لا بد أن يكون باقيا بشخصه'.
3
Apply the concept
Consider how this definition impacts theological discussions—e.g., if Allah's attributes are said to intensify, would that imply change in His essence? Use the commentary's reasoning to answer.
This connects to the broader aqeedah context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي قوله: (لا يعرض لهما الاشتداد والتنقص) وذلك لأن الاشتداد هو الحركة من الكيف الأدنى إلى الكيف الأقوى والتنقص بالعكس فيكون كل منها من قبيل الحركة في الكيف ولا شك أن الحركة في الكيف إنما تكون عارضة لمحل الكيف لا لنفس الكيف فإن معروض الحركة لا بد أن يكون باقيا بشخصه من المبدأ إلى المنتهي . قوله : ( وهو يكفينا إذ نحن بصدد المنع وليس غرضنا إيراد النقض الإجمالي فما ذكر من الاحتمال العقلي يكفينا . وقوله : إن التأثير بينهما أي بين العنصرين بلا تلاق محتمل أي محتمل في غير ما نحن فيه بعدده لا فيما نحن فيه بصدده إذ قد كان حاله مكشوفاً كما ذكره .
That is his statement: 'They do not admit intensification and diminution.' That is because intensification is the motion from the lower quality to the stronger quality, and diminution is the opposite. So each of them is a kind of motion in quality, and there is no doubt that motion in quality only occurs to the subject of the quality, not to the quality itself. For the subject of motion must persist in its identity from the beginning to the end. His statement: 'And it suffices us, for we are in the context of denial, and our purpose is not to present a general refutation; what we mentioned of rational possibility suffices us.' And his statement: 'Indeed, the effect between them—i.e., between the two elements—without contact is possible,' meaning possible outside our present subject, not within it, since its state is already revealed as he mentioned.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining two points from the earlier text. First, it defines intensification as motion from a lower quality to a stronger one, and diminution as the opposite. These belong to the subject of the quality, not to the quality itself, because the subject must persist through the change. Second, it comments on the first objection's response: the rational possibility of action without contact is sufficient for the purpose of denial, and such possibility only applies outside the specific context of mixture components, where observation already shows contact is necessary.

Key Terms
اشتداد
تنقص
حركة
كيف
محل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that intensification or diminution changes the quality itself (e.g., 'whiteness' becoming 'more white' as a change in the essence of whiteness).
Correction: The commentary clarifies that such changes are motions of the subject that possesses the quality, not modifications of the quality as an abstract attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what is intensification?
A) A change in the substance of the quality.
B) Motion from a lower quality to a stronger one.
C) A sudden transformation of the subject.
D) The removal of quality.

Key Takeaway
Intensification and diminution are forms of motion that belong to the subject of the quality, not to the quality itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your faith feels stronger or weaker, do you see it as a change in you (the subject) or as a change in the quality of faith itself? How does this passage challenge you to take ownership of your spiritual growth?
?
Think of a character trait you have been trying to improve. Are you focusing on the quality as an abstract ideal, or on your own persistent self as the subject of change? What would it look like to treat your effort as a motion that you, as a servant of Allah, undergo?
?
The passage says the subject must persist for motion to occur. In your repentance and return to Allah, what part of you remains constant? How does knowing that your soul endures through change give you confidence in Allah's mercy and your own capacity for transformation?

The Sufficiency of Rational Possibility in Denial
Commentary on the objection's response about action without contact.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that rational possibility of action without contact is sufficient for denial, and that such possibility is only applicable outside contexts where observation shows necessity.

Core Idea
Rational possibility is enough for denial; observation determines actuality within its scope.

This lesson focuses on the second part of the commentary, which addresses an objection about action without contact. The response argues that the mere rational possibility of such action suffices for the purpose of denial (منع), and that this possibility is only entertained outside the specific case where observation reveals contact to be necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection response
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the two statements: the rational possibility suffices for denial, and the possibility is only applicable outside the present context.
The phrase 'إذ نحن بصدد المنع' indicates the context of denial.
2
Understand 'rational possibility'
The response invokes 'الاحتمال العقلي' (rational possibility). Reflect on why such possibility is considered sufficient for denying a claim, even if it is not actual.
This is a common kalam method: refuting by showing something is not necessary.
3
Recognize the contextual limitation
The commentary specifies that this rational possibility is only 'في غير ما نحن فيه' (outside our present subject). Why? Because in the present case, observation (كشف) shows contact is necessary.
This prevents the argument from being used to deny observable facts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي قوله: (لا يعرض لهما الاشتداد والتنقص) وذلك لأن الاشتداد هو الحركة من الكيف الأدنى إلى الكيف الأقوى والتنقص بالعكس فيكون كل منها من قبيل الحركة في الكيف ولا شك أن الحركة في الكيف إنما تكون عارضة لمحل الكيف لا لنفس الكيف فإن معروض الحركة لا بد أن يكون باقيا بشخصه من المبدأ إلى المنتهي . قوله : ( وهو يكفينا إذ نحن بصدد المنع وليس غرضنا إيراد النقض الإجمالي فما ذكر من الاحتمال العقلي يكفينا . وقوله : إن التأثير بينهما أي بين العنصرين بلا تلاق محتمل أي محتمل في غير ما نحن فيه بعدده لا فيما نحن فيه بصدده إذ قد كان حاله مكشوفاً كما ذكره .
That is his statement: 'They do not admit intensification and diminution.' That is because intensification is the motion from the lower quality to the stronger quality, and diminution is the opposite. So each of them is a kind of motion in quality, and there is no doubt that motion in quality only occurs to the subject of the quality, not to the quality itself. For the subject of motion must persist in its identity from the beginning to the end. His statement: 'And it suffices us, for we are in the context of denial, and our purpose is not to present a general refutation; what we mentioned of rational possibility suffices us.' And his statement: 'Indeed, the effect between them—i.e., between the two elements—without contact is possible,' meaning possible outside our present subject, not within it, since its state is already revealed as he mentioned.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining two points from the earlier text. First, it defines intensification as motion from a lower quality to a stronger one, and diminution as the opposite. These belong to the subject of the quality, not to the quality itself, because the subject must persist through the change. Second, it comments on the first objection's response: the rational possibility of action without contact is sufficient for the purpose of denial, and such possibility only applies outside the specific context of mixture components, where observation already shows contact is necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Classification of Minerals: Malleable Bodies and the Seven Metals
Classification of minerals into malleable and non-malleable, with enumeration of seven metals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic natural philosophy classifies inanimate composites into minerals, specifically the distinction between malleable and non-malleable bodies, and the identity of the seven metals.

Core Idea
Minerals are classified by their response to hammering; the seven metals are the malleable ones.

This passage introduces the study of inanimate composites that have temperament but no soul, namely minerals. The author defines two categories based on whether they can be hammered without breaking. This classification leads to the list of seven malleable metals, which are formed from mercury and sulfur. Understanding this foundational taxonomy helps explain early theories of metal formation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author begins a new chapter on inanimate composites and defines two categories.
Pay attention to the definition of 'malleable' (munṭariqa) – it is not just bendable but specifically resistant to breaking under hammering.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage divides minerals into malleable and non-malleable. Understand the criteria: can it be hammered without breaking or dispersing? If yes, it is malleable.
Non-malleable minerals are not listed here; only the malleable ones are further enumerated.
3
List the seven metals
The passage enumerates seven metals: gold, silver, lead, tin, iron, copper, and zinc. Try to memorize them in the original Arabic order.
Note that 'al-usrub' is tin and 'al-khārṣīnī' is zinc. These are the traditional seven metals of medieval science.
4
Reflect on the causal explanation
The passage states that these metals are formed from a mixture of mercury and sulfur, which themselves come from vapors. This reflects the sulfur-mercury theory of metal formation.
This theory was common in alchemy and early chemistry.

Source Text

[ الفصل الثاني فيما لا نفس له من المركبات ] المزاجية (وتسمى المعادن وتنقسم إلى قسمين منطرقة ) أي قابلة لضرب المطرقة بحيث لا تنكسر ولا تتفرق بل تلين وتندفع إلى عمقها فتنبسط. [The first section: the seven bodies] the malleable bodies are the seven bodies: gold, silver, lead, tin, iron, copper, and zinc (formed from the mixing of mercury and sulfur, which are formed from vapors.

Commentary
This passage is a section heading and definition for a new chapter on inanimate composites that possess temperament but no soul, namely minerals. The author divides minerals into two categories: malleable (munṭariqa) and non-malleable (ghayr munṭariqa). Malleable bodies are defined as those that can be hammered without breaking or shattering; they soften and spread. Non-malleable bodies do not have this property. The passage then specifies the first section, which lists the seven malleable bodies: gold, silver, lead, tin (al-usrub), iron, copper, and zinc (al-khārṣīnī). These metals are formed from the mixture of mercury and sulfur, which themselves originate from vapors. This classification sets the foundation for further discussion of metallic properties.
