Definition of the Vegetative Soul
Definition of the vegetative soul with term-by-term analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of the vegetative soul and how each qualifier (first, body, natural, organic) refines the concept.

Core Idea
The vegetative soul is the first perfection of a natural organic body.

This lesson unpacks the definition of the vegetative soul, a foundational concept in Islamic philosophical psychology. The author defines it as the first perfection of a natural organic body, then explains each term to avoid common misconceptions. You will see how the definition excludes artificial bodies, separate substances, elements, and minerals, and why temperaments are not souls.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and its parts
Start by reading the Arabic definition and its translation. Note the four key qualifiers: first perfection, body, natural, organic.
2
Analyze each qualifier
The commentary explains each term. Focus on what 'first' excludes (second perfections), what 'body' excludes (separate substances), what 'natural' excludes (artificial bodies), and what 'organic' excludes (elements and minerals).
Notice the grammatical discussion about 'organic' being an adjective of 'perfection' or 'body'.
3
Consider the objection about temperaments
The passage raises an objection: why aren't temperaments considered first perfections? Read the answer carefully—temperaments are conditions, not constitutive.
This clarifies the internal vs. external distinction.
4
Check your understanding with the quiz
Answer the checkpoint question to test if you understood why elements and minerals are excluded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - الفصل الثالث : المقدمة في تعريف النفس [ الفصل الثالث في المركبات التي لها نفس وفيه مقدمة وثلاثة أقسام ] [ المقدمة : في تعريف النفس ] في تعريف النفس وهي ثلاث الأولى : ( النفس ( النيابة وهي كمال أول لجسم طبيعي آلي من حيث يتغذى وينمو، فالكمال (جنس) يتناول المحدود وغيره لأنه عبارة عما يتم به النوع إما في ذاته ويسمى كمالاً أولاً ومنوعاً كصورة السرير مثلاً فإنها كمال للخشب السريري لا يتم السرير في حد ذاته إلا بها. وإما في صفاته كالبياض فإنه كمال للجسم الأبيض لا يكمل في صفته إلابه ويسمى كمالاً ثانياً. ( وبأول يخرج) عن الحد (الكمالات الثانية ) المتأخرة عن تحصل النوع في نفسه ( كتوابع) الكمال (الأول) المحصل للنوع من العلم والقدرة وغيرهما من الصفات المتفرعة على تحصل الأنواع في ذواتها ( وبالجسم يخرج) عنه (كمال المجردات ) أي منوعها، وبالطبيعي يخرج الجسم ( الصناعي) أي يخرج صور الأجسام الصناعية ( كالسرير (والكرسي فإن صورتهما لا تسمى نفساً ) وبالآلي) يخرج (العناصر ) أي صورها (إذ لا يصدر عنها أفعالها بواسطة الآلات)، وكذلك الصور المعدنية فلفظ آلي يجوز رفعه على أنه صفة لكمال أول أي كمال ذو آلة ويجوز جره على أنه صفة لجسم أي جسم مشتمل على الآلة، وهذا أظهر وعلى قوله : ( المتأخرة عن تحصل النوع فإن قيل : فعلى هذا يلزم أن تكون الأمزجة النباتية والحيوانية والإنسانية كمالات أول لعدم تأخرها عن تحصل تلك الأنواع مع أن المزاج لا يسمى نفسا . قلنا : المراد بالكمال الأول للشيء هو ما كان محصلاً في نفسه وداخلاً في قوامه. كما أشار إليه بقوله : ما يتم به النوع في ذاته، وظاهر أن الأمزجة المذكورة ليست بداخلة في قوام تلك الأنواع. وإنما هي شروط لتحصلها في أنفسها . وقوله : أي منوعها المشهور بينهم أن المجردات أمور بسيطة في الخارج . وإن كان بها فصول متنوعة لها في الذهن لكن المتبادر من الكمال الأول هاهنا ما هو المنوع الخارجي. قوله : ( وهذا أظهر لأن كون الجسم ذا آلة ولأن الموصوف حينئذ يكون أقرب في اللفظ لكنه لا يناسب أن يكون طبيعي مرفوعاً كما هو الأقرب . لأنه حينئذ يقع الفصل بالأجنبي بين الموصوف والصفة وقوله : ذا أجزاء متخالفة فقط. وإلا يلزم أن تكون المعادن البتة فإن لها أجزاء متخالفة مع أنها ليس بآلية إذ ليس لها إلا قوة حافظة.
The First Observatory - Chapter Three: Introduction to the Definition of the Soul [Chapter Three on compounds that have a soul, containing an introduction and three sections] [Introduction: On the Definition of the Soul] On the definition of the soul. It is three: the first is the vegetative soul, which is a first perfection for a natural organic body inasmuch as it nourishes and grows. 'Perfection' (al-kamal) is a genus that includes the defined and other things, because it is an expression of that by which the species is completed, either in itself—and it is called first perfection and specific differentia, like the form of a bed for example, for it is a perfection of the bed-wood; the bed is not completed in its essence except by it—or in its attributes, like whiteness, for it is a perfection of the white body; its attribute is not completed except by it, and it is called second perfection. 'By first' excludes from the definition the second perfections that are posterior to the realization of the species in itself, like the consequences of the first perfection that realizes the species, such as knowledge, power, and other attributes that branch off from the realization of species in their essences. 'By body' excludes from it the perfection of separate substances, i.e., their specific differentia. 'By natural' excludes artificial body, i.e., it excludes the forms of artificial bodies like the bed and the chair, for their forms are not called soul. 'By organic' excludes the elements, i.e., their forms, since their actions do not proceed by means of instruments. Similarly, mineral forms. The term 'organic' can be raised as an adjective of 'first perfection', i.e., a perfection having an instrument, or it can be declined as an adjective of 'body', i.e., a body containing an instrument, and this is more apparent. And regarding his statement 'posterior to the realization of the species', if it is said: According to this, it follows that plant, animal, and human temperaments would be first perfections because they are not posterior to the realization of those species, while temperament is not called soul. We say: What is meant by the first perfection of a thing is that which is constitutive of itself and internal to its constitution, as he indicated by his statement 'that by which the species is completed in itself'. It is clear that the mentioned temperaments are not internal to the constitution of those species; rather they are conditions for their realization in themselves. And his statement: 'i.e., their specific differentia'—it is well-known among them that separate substances are simple things externally, although they have diverse differentiae in the mind; but the understood meaning of 'first perfection' here is the external specific differentia. His statement: 'This is more apparent' because the body having an instrument, and because the described would then be closer in expression, but it is not appropriate for 'natural' to be raised as it is closer; because then the separation by a foreign element between the described and the attribute would occur. And his statement: 'having heterogeneous parts only'. Otherwise, it would follow that minerals [would be souls] because they have heterogeneous parts while they are not organic, as they have only a preserving power.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage provides the definition of the vegetative soul (al-nafs al-nabatiyya). It begins with the full definition: 'a first perfection for a natural organic body inasmuch as it nourishes and grows.' Then it analyzes each term in the definition. 'Perfection' (al-kamal) is a genus that includes both the defined and other things, because perfection can be either in the essence (first perfection, which constitutes the species, like the form of a bed) or in attributes (second perfection, like whiteness). The word 'first' excludes second perfections that come after the species is realized, such as knowledge and power that branch off from the first perfection. The word 'body' excludes perfections of separate substances (mujarradat) because they are not bodies. 'Natural' excludes artificial bodies like beds and chairs, whose forms are not called souls. 'Organic' (ali) excludes elements and minerals because their actions do not proceed through instruments. The passage then discusses the grammatical possibility of 'ali' as an adjective for 'first perfection' (a perfection having organs) or for 'body' (a body containing organs), preferring the latter. An objection is raised: according to this definition, temperaments (amzija) of plants, animals, and humans would be first perfections because they are not posterior to the species, yet temperaments are not called souls. The answer: first perfection is what is internal and constitutive of the thing, while temperaments are only conditions for its realization. Finally, the passage clarifies that separate substances are simple externally but have differentiae in the mind; the intended meaning of 'first perfection' here is the external differentia. It also notes that if 'organic' is not taken to mean heterogenous parts, minerals would incorrectly be considered souls since they have heterogenous parts but lack organic instruments.

كمال أول
جسم طبيعي آلي
كمال ثانٍ
مجردات
منوع
آلي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that temperament (mizaj) is the first perfection of a living being.
Correction: The text clarifies that temperament is only a condition for the realization of the species, not constitutive of it, and therefore not a first perfection.

Checkpoint
According to the definition, why are the forms of elements and minerals not considered souls?
A) They are not natural bodies
B) They lack the property of nourishment and growth
C) Their actions do not proceed through instruments
D) They are artificial

Key Takeaway
The vegetative soul is precisely defined as the first perfection of a natural organic body, meaning it is the constitutive form that gives life, excluding anything that is not intrinsic to the species.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your soul is defined as the first perfection of your body—the very thing that makes your body a living human body. If you truly believed that your soul is not just something you 'have' but something you *are*, how would your relationship with your body change? Would you still treat it as a mere vehicle, or as an integral part of your identity? Commit to one concrete change in how you treat your body this week.
?
The text warns against confusing second perfections (knowledge, power, skills) with the first perfection (the soul itself). Think honestly: which second perfection do you most rely on for your sense of worth? Is your identity built on what you know or can do, rather than on the soul that God created? What would it look like to shift your foundation?
?
Temperament is only a condition for the soul, not the soul itself. Consider your own temperament—your natural inclinations, moods, and habits. Have you ever mistaken a passing mood or a ingrained habit for the state of your soul? How can you distinguish between what is truly constitutive of your spiritual life and what is merely a condition? Make a list of three conditions you tend to over-identify with.

Defining the Soul: General and Via Potential Life
Two general definitions of soul with restrictions and celestial souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main definitions of the soul given in Islamic philosophy: the disjunctive definition covering all three souls and the unified definition using 'potential life', including the key restrictions and how celestial souls are handled.

Core Idea
The soul is the first perfection of a natural organic body with potential life.

After defining each type of soul individually, the author presents two general definitions. The first uses a disjunction that explicitly mentions the functions of the plant, animal, and human souls. The second condenses that into a single attribute: 'possessing potential life'. This lesson unpacks both definitions, explaining the crucial terms 'organic' and 'potential' and how they exclude elements, minerals, and celestial souls depending on one's view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice that the passage has two main parts: the initial disjunctive definition and the alternative 'potential life' definition, followed by commentary on the restrictions.
Pay attention to how the author shifts from a detailed definition to a more concise one.
2
Understand the disjunctive definition
The first definition says the soul is a first perfection of a natural organic body that does X, Y, or Z (where X,Y,Z correspond to the three souls). See how this covers all cases with a brief expression.
3
Analyze the alternative definition and its restrictions
The second definition uses 'possessing potential life'. Break down the two restrictions: 'organic' excludes inanimate bodies; 'potential' excludes beings whose life-acts are always actual. Consider how celestial souls are treated differently on two views.
The text explains that on one view, the 'potential' restriction alone excludes celestial souls; on another view, an extra restriction is needed.
4
Learn Imam al-Razi's interpretation
Imam al-Razi interprets 'potential life' as the nature to live by growth, nourishment, sensation, and movement. Compare this with the earlier disjunctive functions.
This ties the two definitions together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا إذا عرفنا كل واحدة من النفوس الثلاث على حدة وإن أردنا تعريف النفس مطلقاً قلنا : النفس كمال أول لجسم طبيعي آلي ما يتغذى وينمو ويحس ويتحرك لإرادة أو يعقل الكليات ويستنبط بالرأي فإن هذا الترديد راجع إلى أقسام المعرف ومتناول إياها والتحقيق أنه بحسب المعنى تعريفات ثلاثة لتلك الأقسام مع وجازة في العبارة وقد يعبر عنها بلازم واحد شامل لها وهو من حيث أنه ذو حياة بالقوة فيقال : النفس كمال أول لجسم طبيعي آلي ذي حياة بالقوة فقيد الآلي احتراز عن صور العناصر والمعادن فإنها وإن كانت كمالات أولية أجسام طبيعية إلا أنها غير آلية كما مر . ويخرج به أيضاً النفوس الفلكية على رأى من ذهب إلى أن لكل فلك من الأفلاك نفساً . وأما على رأى من ذهب إلى أن النفوس للأفلاك الكلية فقط والأفلاك الجزئية كالخارج والتدوير بمنزلة آلات لها فلا تخرج به فاحتيج إلى القيد الأخير لتخرج عن التعريف على المذهبين وذلك لأن النفوس الفلكية وإن كانت كمالات أولية أجسام طبيعية آلية لكنها ليس يصدر عنها أفاعيل الحياة بالقوة بل يصدر عنها ما يصدر من أفاعيل الحياة كالحركة الإرادية مثلا دائما بخلاف النفوس الحيوانية، فإن أفعالها قد تكون بالقوة إذ ليس لحيوان في التغذية والتنمية وتوليد المثل والإدراك والحركة دائماً بل قد يكون كل واحد من هذه الأفعال فيه بالقوة وكذا حال النفس الإنسانية بالقياس إلى تعقل الكليات والاستنباط بالآراء وحال النفس النباتية بالنسبة إلى ما يصدر عنها. فمعنى قوله : ذي حياة أنه يصدر عنه بعض أفاعيل الحياة ومعنى قوله بالقوة أن ذلك الصدور لا يكون بالفعل دائماً. وفسرها الإمام الرازي بقوله : أي من شأنه أن يحيا بالنشوء ويبقى بالغذاء وربما يحيا بالإحساس والتحريك .
This is if we define each of the three souls separately. But if we wish to define the soul absolutely, we say: The soul is a first perfection of a natural organic body that is nourished, grows, senses, moves voluntarily, or cognizes universals and deduces by opinion. For this disjunction refers to the divisions of the definiendum and encompasses them. The verification is that it is, according to meaning, three definitions for those divisions with brevity in expression. And it may be expressed concerning them by a single necessary attribute inclusive of them, which is that it is a being with potential life. So it is said: The soul is a first perfection of a natural organic body possessing potential life. The restriction 'organic' excludes the forms of the elements and minerals, for although they are first perfections of natural bodies, they are not organic, as has passed. And by it are also excluded the celestial souls according to the view of those who hold that every sphere (falk) of the spheres has a soul. As for the view that souls belong only to the universal spheres and the partial spheres are like instruments for them, then they are not excluded by it, so the last restriction is needed to exclude them from the definition according to both schools. This is because the celestial souls, although they are first perfections of natural organic bodies, yet they do not produce acts of life potentially, but rather they produce what they produce of acts of life like voluntary movement always, unlike the animal souls, for their acts may be potential, since an animal does not always have nourishment, growth, reproduction, perception, and movement, but each of these acts may be potential in it. Similarly the human soul with respect to cognizing universals and deduction by opinions, and the plant soul with respect to what proceeds from it. So the meaning of his saying 'possessing life' is that it produces some acts of life, and the meaning of 'potential' is that this production is not always actual. And Imam al-Razi interpreted it by his saying: i.e., it is of its nature to live by growth and remain by nourishment, and perhaps it lives by sensation and movement.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage first gives a general definition of soul that covers all three types using a disjunction. Then it offers an alternative definition using a single attribute: 'possessing potential life'. It explains the restrictions in this definition: 'organic' excludes elements and minerals, and 'potential' excludes celestial souls. It discusses two views on celestial souls and why the third restriction is needed. Then explains the meaning of 'potential life'. Finally, it quotes Imam al-Razi's interpretation.

Key Terms
النفس مطلقاً (soul absolutely)
ذي حياة بالقوة (possessing potential life)
النفوس الفلكية (celestial souls)
الأفلاك الكلية (universal spheres)
الأفلاك الجزئية (partial spheres)
أفاعيل الحياة (acts of life)
بالقوة (potential)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the definition with 'potential life' excludes celestial souls in all cases without further qualification.
Correction: The text shows that on the view where only universal spheres have souls, the 'potential' restriction is necessary to exclude celestial souls; on the other view, 'organic' alone does not exclude them, so an additional restriction is required.

Checkpoint
According to the text, why does the definition 'soul is a first perfection of a natural organic body possessing potential life' require an additional restriction to exclude celestial souls on the view that souls belong only to universal spheres?
A) Because celestial souls are not organic
B) Because celestial souls produce acts of life always actually, not potentially
C) Because celestial souls are not natural
D) Because celestial souls are not first perfections

Key Takeaway
The soul is defined as the first perfection of a natural organic body with potential life, meaning it has the capacity to perform acts of life, though not always actually.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that your soul is defined by its potential for life acts—growth, sensation, thought. But are you actually living out that potential? When you waste time in mindless distraction, which faculty of your soul are you neglecting? Commit today to one hour of focused reflection (tafakkur) on a universal truth.
?
The passage shows that even the celestial souls always act, yet your soul often lies dormant. Does this not stir a sense of urgency? What would it mean for you to live as if your soul's acts are not guaranteed—that every moment of thought and worship is a gift? Write down one intention to 'actualize' a dormant act of your soul this week, like a conscious act of gratitude or a moment of deep contemplation of Allah's signs.
?
Imam al-Razi says the soul's nature is to live by growth and nourishment. But what are you nourishing your soul with? Is it the same care you give your body? Be honest: when was the last time you fed your soul with knowledge that deepens your iman? Name one book or surah you will study this week as spiritual nourishment.

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
6 min
The Difficulty of Defining the Soul
Al-Jurjani critiques the standard definition of the soul and identifies its flaws.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the standard definition of the soul as 'a principle of actions' fails to cover all types of souls, and how the term 'soul' may be used homonymously.

Core Idea
The simple definition 'soul as principle of actions' is both too broad and too narrow.

Al-Jurjani begins by addressing a methodological issue: when defining a relational term, we must include the relata even if they are extraneous to the essence. He then applies this to the soul and critiques the common definition, showing that it fails to uniformly cover vegetative, animal, and celestial souls. This sets the stage for Ibn Sina's solution.

Guided Path
1
Understand the methodological point
Read the first part of the passage about defining relational terms. Notice that when a thing has a name based on its relation to another, that other must be included in the definition, even if it is not essential to the thing's essence.
Examples like father/son and head/hand help illustrate this.
2
Identify the standard definition of the soul
The standard definition is 'the soul is a principle of actions'. This is what the author examines critically.
3
Analyze the three problems
The passage lists three issues: (1) it would include inanimate forces like elemental nature, (2) adding intention excludes vegetative souls, (3) requiring diverse actions excludes celestial souls. Note how each condition fails to cover one type.
These problems show the definition is neither universal nor exclusive.
4
Conclude the need for a different approach
Because no simple definition works, the author concludes that 'soul' is used homonymously for different kinds. This motivates Ibn Sina's solution in the next lesson.
This homonymy is a key insight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - الفصل الثالث : المقدمة في تعريف النفس هو آخر فإذا أردنا تعريفه من الجهة الثانية فلا بد أن يأخذ فيه المضاف إليه وهي ) أي الأمور المضاف إليها ( وإن لم تكن ذاتية (لها أي للأشياء التي أريد تعريفها (في جوهرها فهي (ذاتية) لها ( من جهة التسمية). وتوضيحه ما في المباحث المشرقية أن الشيء قد يكون له في ذاته، وجوهره اسم يخصه وباعتبار إضافته إلى غيره اسم آخر كالفاعل والمنفعل والأب والابن وقد لا يكون له اسم إلا باعتبار إضافته إلى غيره كالرأس واليد والجناح . فمتى أردنا أن نعطيها حدودها من جهة أسمائها بما هي مضافة أخذنا الأشياء الخارجة عن جواهرها في حدودها لأنها ذاتيات لها بحسب الأسماء التي لها تلك الحدود التنبيه ( الثالث : هذا الحد ) الذي ذكروه للنفس على الإطلاق ( لا يتناول النفوس الفلكية لأن أفعالها إن لم تكن بالآلات كما المشهور فقد خرجت عن التعريف بقيد الآلي، وإن كانت بالآلات كما ذهب إليه جمع فقد خرجت عنه بقيد ذي حياة بالقوة على ما مر . وكذا لا يتناولها الحد المستفاد مما ذكرناه في التنبيه الأول ) لما عرفت أنا أعطيناها اسم النفس من حيث ( يختلف أفعالها والنفوس ( الفلكية ليست كذلك فإن أفعالها غير مختلفة بل هي على نهج واحد، والاختلافات المشاهدة فيها مستندة إلى تركب حركات كل واحدة منها على وتيرة واحدة ) ولا تعلم رسما يتناولها ) أي ويتناول النفوس الثلاث معا عن النباتية والحيوانية والفلكية . ( فإنا لو قلنا : ( النفس ما يكون ( مبدأ للأفعال ) أي ما يصدر عنه فعل ( كان كل قوة كالطبيعة العنصرية والصورة المعدنية (نفساً. ولو شرطنا ) مع صدور الفعل ( القصد خرجت ) النفس ( النباتية ) ، والحاصل أن الاكتفاء بصدور الفعل يبطل طرد الحد واعتبار اختلاف الأفعال يخرج النفوس الفلكية. واعتبار القصد يخرج النباتية فلم يتحقق عندنا رسم صحيح يتناول النفوس الثلاث. فإطلاق النفس على النفوس الأرضية والسماوية ليس إلا بحسب الاشتراك اللفظي هذا. وقد صرح ابن سينا في الشفاء بأن كل ما يكون مبدأ لصدور أفاعيل ليست على وتيرة واحدة عادمة للإرادة فإنا نسميه نفسا وهذا المعنى مشترك بين النفوس كلها لأن ما يكون مبدأ لأفاعيل موصوفة بما ذكر إما أن يكون مبدأ لأفاعيل مختلفة، وهو النفس الأرضية أعني النباتية والحيوانية أو يكون مبدأ لأفاعيل على وتيرة واحدة لكن لا تكون عادمة للإرادة بل واجدة لها وهو النفس الفلكية فقد علمنا رسماً يتناولها بأسرها .
The first observation – Chapter three: Introduction to the definition of the soul. It is the last [؟]. So if we want to define it from the second respect, it is necessary to include in it the thing to which it is added, and they (i.e., the things to which it is added) even if they are not essential (to them, i.e., to the things that are intended to be defined) in their essence, they are (essential) to them (from the perspective of nomenclature). Its clarification is what is in al-Mabāhith al-Mashriqiyya: a thing may have in its essence a name specific to it, and by consideration of its addition to another, another name, like agent and patient, father and son; and it may have no name except by consideration of its addition to another, like head, hand, and wing. So when we want to give them their definitions from the perspective of their names insofar as they are added, we take things external to their essences in their definitions, because they are essential to them according to the names for which those definitions [apply]. The third reminder: This definition that they mentioned for the soul absolutely does not encompass the celestial souls, because if their actions are not by instruments, as is well-known, then they have exited the definition by the condition of instrumentality; and if they are by instruments, as a group held, then they have exited from it by the condition of possessing life potentially, as has passed. Likewise, it is not encompassed by the definition derived from what we mentioned in the first reminder, because you know that we gave it the name 'soul' insofar as its actions differ, and the celestial souls are not like that, for their actions are not different but are uniform, and the observed differences in them are due to the composition of each one's motions on a single pattern. And we do not know a definition that encompasses them (i.e., that encompasses the three souls together: the vegetative, animal, and celestial). For if we said: 'The soul is that which is a principle of actions' (i.e., that from which an act issues), then every power like the elemental nature and mineral form would be a soul. And if we stipulate intention along with the issuing of the act, then the vegetative soul would be excluded. The conclusion is that being content with the issuing of the act invalidates the universality of the definition, and considering the difference of actions excludes the celestial souls, and considering intention excludes the vegetative soul. So we have not achieved a correct definition encompassing the three souls. So the application of 'soul' to earthly and celestial souls is only according to verbal homonymy. And Ibn Sina stated explicitly in al-Shifā' that whatever is a principle for the issuing of acts that are not uniform and are devoid of will, we call it a soul, and this meaning is common to all souls, because whatever is a principle for acts described as mentioned, either it is a principle for different acts—which is the earthly soul, namely the vegetative and animal—or it is a principle for acts on a uniform pattern but are not devoid of will but possess it—which is the celestial soul. So we have arrived at a definition that encompasses them all.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of the definition of the soul (nafs). It begins by addressing how to define the soul when considering its relational aspects. The author explains that when defining something from the perspective of its relations, the things to which it is related must be included in the definition, even if they are not essential to its essence, because they are essential from the perspective of the name. He illustrates this with examples from al-Mabāhith al-Mashriqiyya: a single entity can have different names based on its relations (e.g., agent/patient). Thus, when defining such terms, relational aspects become part of the definition. The passage then examines the standard definition of the soul as 'a principle of actions' and finds it problematic. First, this definition fails to exclude inanimate forces (like elemental nature) that also produce actions. Second, if we add the condition of intention, vegetative souls (which lack intention) are excluded. Third, celestial souls (whose actions are uniform) are excluded if we require diversity of actions. Therefore, no single definition covers all three types of souls (vegetative, animal, celestial), and the term 'soul' is used only homonymously. The passage then presents Ibn Sina's solution from al-Shifā': the soul is a principle for actions that are either non-uniform and devoid of will (earthly souls) or uniform but possess will (celestial souls). This definition successfully encompasses all souls by using a disjunction that captures both types uniformly. The passage thus demonstrates the need for a carefully crafted definition that accounts for the specific characteristics of different kinds of souls.

Key Terms
نفس (nafs)
حد (definition)
رسم (description)
الاشتراك اللفظي (verbal homonymy)
مبدأ الأفعال (principle of actions)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a simple single definition like 'principle of actions' can cover all souls.
Correction: The passage shows that such a definition fails to exclude inanimate forces and fails to include all soul types, necessitating a more complex definition.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one problem with the definition 'soul is a principle of actions'?
A) It excludes vegetative souls because they lack intention.
B) It includes inanimate forces like elemental nature.
C) It excludes celestial souls because their actions are diverse.
D) It requires the soul to have instruments.

Key Takeaway
The standard definition of the soul fails because it cannot simultaneously exclude inanimate forces and include all types of souls, forcing the conclusion that 'soul' is used homonymously.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran and encounter the word 'nafs', do you assume it always refers to the same reality? What if – as Al-Jurjani argues – it is used homonymously? How would that change your interpretation of verses like 'And the soul (nafs) and what proportioned it' (Quran 91:7)?
?
Al-Jurjani's critique shows that our easy definitions often fail under scrutiny. Is there an area of your own faith where you rely on a comfortable but imprecise definition? What would it cost you to admit that definition is inadequate?
?
If the soul is not straightforwardly definable, how does that affect your personal understanding of your own 'self'? Do you rely on a clear definition to guide your spiritual growth, or can you accept some mystery while still striving for purification of the soul?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
5 min
Ibn Sina's Comprehensive Definition of the Soul
Al-Jurjani presents Ibn Sina's successful definition of the soul as a solution to the earlier problems.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Sina's disjunctive definition successfully encompasses all three types of souls (vegetative, animal, celestial) by considering both the uniformity of actions and the presence of will.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina defines the soul as a principle of non-uniform acts without will, or uniform acts with will.

After showing that no simple definition works, Al-Jurjani presents Ibn Sina's solution from the Shifā'. Ibn Sina defines the soul disjunctively: it is either a principle of non-uniform actions devoid of will (earthly souls) or a principle of uniform actions endowed with will (celestial souls). This definition covers all souls without overextending to inanimate forces.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn Sina's definition
Read the passage where Ibn Sina states: 'Whatever is a principle for the issuing of acts that are not uniform and are devoid of will, we call it a soul.' Note that this is the earthly soul.
There is a second part for celestial souls: acts on a uniform pattern but possessing will.
2
Understand the disjunction
The definition splits into two cases: (A) principle of different acts without will (earthly: vegetative and animal), (B) principle of uniform acts with will (celestial). Together they cover all souls.
This disjunction avoids the earlier problems: it excludes inanimate forces (which act uniformly and without will) and includes both earthly and celestial souls.
3
Compare with earlier failed definitions
Notice how Ibn Sina's definition solves each earlier problem: it excludes elemental nature (which acts uniformly without will) by requiring either different acts or will. It includes vegetative soul (different acts, no will) and celestial soul (uniform acts, will).
The key innovation is using a disjunction of two property clusters.
4
Appreciate the solution
Ibn Sina provides a 'rasm' (description) that successfully picks out all instances of 'soul' while excluding non-souls. This shows careful attention to the philosophical demands of definition.
This definition is widely accepted in later Islamic philosophy.

Source Text

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina's definition, what distinguishes earthly souls from celestial souls?
A) Earthly souls have will, celestial souls do not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Sina distinguishes souls by whether their acts are uniform or varied. Look at your own actions: which parts of your daily routine are uniform yet possess will? When you pray, do you pray with will or just out of habit? What does that say about your soul's principle?
?
The definition includes both earthly and celestial souls. If you were to define your own soul not by its substance but by the pattern of your actions and your will, what would that definition look like? And does it align with what you truly are or what you pretend to be?
?
‘Soul’ is not a simple label but a common principle with two branches. Which branch do you find yourself in—do your actions vary because you lack will (like plants) or do they stay uniform because you have will (like the celestial)? Be honest: what is the driving force behind your acts today?

The Vegetative Soul and Its Nutritive Power
Al-Jurjani defines the vegetative soul and details its nutritive power's three actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the vegetative soul, its natural powers, and the three sequential actions of the nutritive power that preserve the individual.

Core Idea
The nutritive power preserves life through three precise actions: acquisition, adhesion, and assimilation.

Al-Jurjani begins the discussion of souls by starting with the lowest, the vegetative soul. He explains that its powers are called 'natural' because they act without will, and they are shared by plants and animals. The most fundamental of these powers is the nutritive, which requires three steps to transform food into the body's substance. This passage details those steps.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the technical description. Notice the structured enumeration of the three actions.
2
Identify the three actions
Find the three actions listed for the nutritive power: acquisition of humor, adhesion, and assimilation. Understand each term in context.
3
Consider the impairments
Note that each action can be impaired, and the passage provides examples. Ask yourself how this biological model fits into the larger theological framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الأول : النفس النباتية ( في النفس النباتية ) سلك في ذكر النفوس أولاً وبيان قواها ثانياً طريقة الترقي من الأدنى إلى الأعلى فقدم النفوس النباتية ) وقواها تسمى طبيعية ) بناء على أن الطبيعة تطلق على ما يفعل بغير إرادة. وهذه القوى تشترك فيها النباتات والحيوانات كلها . ( وهي أربع مخدومة لأربع أخرى خادمة لها ( منها ) أي من الأربع المخدومة اثنتان يحتاج إليهما لبقاء الشخص)، وتكميله في ذاته ( وهي ) أي القوة المحتاج إليها لأجل الشخص ( الغاذية والنامية ) والقياس المنمية إلا أنه روعي المزاوجة فأسند الفعل إلى السبب ( فالغاذية) التي لا بد منها في بقاء الشخص مدة حياته (تشبه الغذاء بالمتغذي أي تحيل جسماً آخر إلى مشاكلة الجسم الذي تغذوه بدلاً لما يتحلل عنه ) فيتم فعلها بأمور ثلاثة الأول : تحصيل الخلط الذي هو بالقوة القريبة من الفعل شبيه بالعضو وقد يخل به عند عدم الغذاء في نفسه أو لضعف الجاذبة. الثاني: الألزق وهو أن يلصق ذلك الحاصل بالعضو ويجعله جزءا منه بالفعل. وقد يخل به كما في الاستسقاء اللحمي فإن الغذاء فيه منبري عن العضو ولذلك يصير البدن مترهلاً أي مسترخياً . الثالث : أن يجعله بعد الإلصاق شبيهاً به من كل جهة حتى في
The first section: The vegetative soul. (Concerning the vegetative soul.) He proceeded in mentioning the souls first and then explaining their powers in an ascending order from the lowest to the highest, so he presented the vegetative souls first. Their powers are called natural, based on the fact that 'nature' is applied to that which acts without will. These powers are shared by all plants and animals. They are four, served by four others that serve them. Of them (i.e., of the four served), two are needed for the preservation of the individual and its completion in itself, namely the nutritive and the growth [powers]. The analogy is 'growth' (al-nāmiya), but he observed pairing, so he attributed the action to the cause. The nutritive (power) is necessary for the preservation of the individual throughout its life. It assimilates food to the organism, i.e., it transforms another body into similarity with the body it nourishes, as a replacement for what dissolves from it. Its action is completed through three things: First: the acquisition of the humor which is in potentiality close to actuality, similar to the organ, and this may be impaired by the absence of food in itself or due to the weakness of the attractive power. Second: adhesion, which is that it attaches that acquired matter to the organ and makes it actually a part of it. This may be impaired, as in fleshy dropsy, for the food therein is separated from the organ, and therefore the body becomes flabby, i.e., relaxed. Third: that it makes it, after adhesion, similar to it in every respect, even in
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the vegetative soul (al-nafs al-nabātiyya). It is the first section of the discussion on souls. The author follows an ascending order from lower to higher, starting with the vegetative soul. Its powers are called 'natural' because they act without will (irāda), and they are common to both plants and animals. The passage states that there are four primary powers (served) and four secondary powers (serving them), but only two of the served powers are necessary for the individual's preservation and completion: the nutritive (ghādhiya) and the growth (nāmiya) powers. The author explains that the term 'growth' is used as a metaphorical extension, but he observes pairing in the phrasing. He then focuses on the nutritive power, describing its function: it assimilates food by transforming another body into similarity with the nourished body, replacing what is lost through dissolution. The nutritive action involves three steps: (1) acquisition of a humor that is potentially similar to the organ; (2) adhesion of that humor to the organ, making it part of the organ; and (3) complete assimilation, making it exactly similar in every respect. The text mentions that each step can be impaired: acquisition fails due to lack of food or weak attraction; adhesion fails in diseases like fleshy dropsy where food separates from the organ, causing flabbiness; and the third step is cut off in the text but presumably continues the idea of complete assimilation. The passage thereby provides a detailed analysis of the vegetative soul's fundamental operation.

Key Terms
نفس نباتية (vegetative soul)
قوى طبيعية (natural powers)
غاذية (nutritive)
نامية (growth)
تمثيل (assimilation)
خلط (humor)
إلزاق (adhesion)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'natural powers' means they are like inanimate forces, but the passage clarifies they are shared by all living things (plants and animals) and involve specific biological processes.
Correction: Natural powers here refer to the inherent biological functions of living beings, not to mechanical or inanimate forces. The passage specifies that these powers are found in all plants and animals and are responsible for growth and nutrition.

Checkpoint
What are the three actions of the nutritive power according to this passage?
A) Acquisition, adhesion, and assimilation.
B) Growth, reproduction, and movement.
C) Digestion, absorption, and excretion.
D) Heating, cooling, and mixing.

Key Takeaway
The nutritive power preserves the individual by transforming food through three precise steps: acquisition, adhesion, and assimilation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage describes how the nutritive power's three steps can be impaired. In what areas of your spiritual life are you neglecting the 'acquisition, adhesion, and assimilation' of beneficial knowledge or deeds? What specific step do you need to focus on improving today?
?
If your very survival depends on a process you cannot consciously control, how does that challenge your sense of self-sufficiency? What does this teach you about your complete dependence on Allah every moment?
?
The vegetative soul's powers are shared with plants. How does this humble your view of human superiority? In what way can this reminder prevent arrogance in your worship and dealings with others?

What Is Kalam?

Classification of Souls: Plant, Animal, and Celestial
Defines three types of souls by action diversity and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the three types of souls are defined based on the diversity of their actions and the presence or absence of will.

Core Idea
Souls are defined by either diverse actions or uniform actions with will.

This passage from al-Mawaqif introduces a foundational classification of souls in Islamic philosophy and theology. The author defines the soul as a principle of actions, and then distinguishes three kinds: the vegetative (plant), the animal, and the celestial. The key is whether the actions are diverse or uniform, and whether they involve will. This classification is essential for understanding later discussions about the nature of the soul and its faculties.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the soul
Read the Arabic passage carefully. The author begins by defining the soul as a principle for actions described as either diverse or uniform with will.
Notice that the definition is comprehensive, covering all souls.
2
Identify the two main categories
The first division is between earthly souls (vegetative and animal) that have diverse actions, and the celestial soul that has uniform actions but possesses will.
Earthly souls are further subdivided, but the celestial soul is a single type.
3
Understand the negation logic
The author explains how to negate the definition: by negating uniform pattern (plants), negating lack of will (celestial), or both (animals). This clarifies the boundaries.
This logic helps differentiate the three types.
4
Note what is excluded
Elemental and mineral natures are excluded because their actions are on a uniform pattern and lack will.
This shows that the definition is not applicable to all natural things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - الفصل الثالث : القسم الأول : النفس النباتية
ما يكون مبدأ لأفاعيل موصوفة بما ذكر إما أن يكون مبدأ لأفاعيل مختلفة، وهو النفس الأرضية أعني النباتية والحيوانية أو يكون مبدأ لأفاعيل على وتيرة واحدة لكن لا تكون عادمة للإرادة بل واجدة لها وهو النفس الفلكية فقد علمنا رسماً يتناولها بأسرها .
نفي هذا المجموع إما بنفي كونها على وتيرة واحدة أو بنفي كونها عادمة للإرادة أو بنفي هذين المذكورين معا. فالأول في النباتات والثاني في الأفلاك والثالث في الحيوانات . وقد خرج عن التعريف الطبائع العنصرية أو المعدنية فإن الأفعال الصادرة عنها تكون على وتيرة واحدة عادمة للإرادة. وقوله : موضوفة بما ذكر أي موصوفة بأنها ليست على وتيرة عادمة للإرادة .
First Observatory - Third Chapter: First Section: The Vegetative Soul
What is a principle for actions described as mentioned: either it is a principle for diverse actions, and it is the earthly soul, namely the vegetative and animal; or it is a principle for actions on a single uniform pattern, but not lacking will, rather possessing it, and it is the celestial soul. Thus we have a definition encompassing all of them.
The negation of this totality is either by negating its being on a uniform pattern, or by negating its lacking will, or by negating both mentioned together. The first applies to plants, the second to the celestial spheres, and the third to animals. And elemental or mineral natures are excluded from the definition, for the actions proceeding from them are on a uniform pattern, lacking will. And his saying: 'described as mentioned' means described as not being on a uniform pattern lacking will.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies souls based on the actions they originate. It first gives a comprehensive definition: the soul is a principle for actions either diverse (earthly: plant and animal) or uniform but with will (celestial). Then it clarifies that the negation of this definition can occur in three ways: negating uniform pattern (plants), negating lacking will (celestial), or both (animals). Elemental and mineral natures are excluded because they act uniformly and without will. Finally, it explains the phrase 'described as mentioned' as meaning 'not being on a uniform pattern lacking will', confirming that the definition includes only souls that either have diverse actions or have will.

Key Terms
النفس النباتية
النفس الحيوانية
النفس الفلكية
الطبائع العنصرية
الطبائع المعدنية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all natural things have souls.
Correction: Elemental and mineral natures are excluded because their actions are uniform and without will, so they do not have souls according to this definition.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT included in the definition of soul according to this passage?
A) The plant soul
B) The celestial soul
C) The animal soul
D) The mineral nature

Key Takeaway
The definition of soul requires either diverse actions or uniform actions with will, excluding things that act uniformly without will, like minerals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Look at your daily actions: are they diverse and self-driven like the animal soul, or uniform and mechanical like a mineral? What does your pattern of action reveal about the state of your soul, and what one change will you make today to align it with the celestial soul's willful obedience to its Creator?
?
The celestial soul acts uniformly yet with will. In your worship, do you maintain consistency (uniformity) while actively choosing (will) to obey? Is there a ritual you perform out of habit rather than conscious intention? How can you reawaken your will in that act today?
?
You are neither mineral nor celestial—you possess diverse actions and will, like the animal soul. But are you using that will to ascend toward the celestial or descend toward the vegetative? Be honest: which direction does your will actually pull you, and what step can you take right now to change course?

The Method of Ascent and the Natural Powers of the Vegetative Soul
Explains the method of ascent and introduces the vegetative soul's natural powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the methodological principle of ascending from lower to higher souls and the definition of natural powers as those acting without will.

Core Idea
Understanding souls begins from the bottom up, starting with the vegetative soul's natural powers.

In this passage, the author lays out the key methodological approach for studying the soul: a step-by-step ascent from the simplest to the most complex. He begins with the vegetative soul, whose powers are termed 'natural' because they operate without conscious will. This sets the stage for the detailed discussion of the powers that follow.

Guided Path
1
Understand the method of ascent
The passage tells us that the author presents souls and their powers in ascending order, starting from the lowest. This is a deliberate teaching method.
2
Define 'natural' powers
Notice that the term 'natural' (طبيعية) is defined operationally: it refers to actions that occur without will. This is a key technical term.
3
Identify the scope of these powers
The passage explicitly states that these powers are common to both plants and animals. This is important to avoid thinking they are only for plants.
4
Prepare for the enumeration
The passage ends by stating that these powers are four, each served by four others. This structure will be elaborated next.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في النفس النباتية سلك في ذكر النفوس أولاً وبيان قواها ثانياً طريقة الترقي من الأدنى إلى الأعلى فقدم النفوس النباتية وقواها تسمى طبيعية بناء على أن الطبيعة تطلق على ما يفعل بغير إرادة. وهذه القوى تشترك فيها النباتات والحيوانات كلها . وهي أربع مخدومة لأربع أخرى خادمة لها
Regarding the vegetative soul, he followed, in mentioning souls first and explaining their powers second, the method of ascent from the lower to the higher, so he presented the vegetative souls. Their powers are called natural, based on the fact that 'nature' is applied to what acts without will. These powers are shared by all plants and animals. They are four, served by four others that serve them.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage introduces the method of discussing souls: ascending from lower to higher, starting with the vegetative soul. The powers of the vegetative soul are called 'natural' because 'nature' in this context means acting without will. These natural powers are common to both plants and animals. The passage then states that there are four principal powers (مخدومة) each of which is served by other powers (خادمة) that assist them. This sets up the enumeration to follow.

Key Terms

القوى الطبيعية
الطبيعة
مخدومة
خادمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think natural powers are unique to plants, but the passage says they are shared by animals.
Correction: The passage explicitly states: 'These powers are shared by all plants and animals.'

Checkpoint

B) Because they act without will
C) Because they are shared by animals
D) Because they are the foundation of the soul

Key Takeaway
The study of souls begins with the vegetative level, where natural powers act without will and are shared by plants and animals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage teaches that we must start from the lowest, the vegetative level, to understand the higher souls. Have you ever tried to understand your own spiritual state by starting from your most basic, natural tendencies? When was the last time you honestly examined your automatic reactions—those that happen 'without will'—and asked yourself: is this serving or hindering my spiritual growth? Commit today to observe one natural impulse and consciously involve your will before acting.
?
Everything in the vegetative soul acts without will—it is mechanical. But you have been given a rational soul that can choose. Are there areas in your life where you are living like a plant, simply reacting without engaging your will? In what part of your daily routine do you need to stop being driven by nature and start making intentional choices for the sake of Allah? Name one specific action today where you will replace automatic behavior with conscious worship.
?
The vegetative soul's powers are shared by animals and plants. What does this tell you about your own dignity? You are not meant to remain at that level. Reflect: Are you content with just growing and consuming like a plant, or are you striving to actualize your rational soul? What is one practical step you can take today to rise from the vegetative to the rational in your journey of knowledge? This lesson is not just information—it is a call to ascend.

The Nourishing Power: Function and Three Steps
Definition and steps of the nourishing power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nourishing power's essential role in survival and its three-step process of transforming food into bodily substance.

Core Idea
The nourishing power transforms food into a substitute for what is lost from the body.

The passage begins by distinguishing two powers needed for the individual: nourishing and growing. Before diving into details, it sets the stage by explaining that the nourishing power is what keeps the body alive, replenishing substances that dissolve. The author then outlines three steps that complete this process, each with potential disruptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the introductory context
Read the first sentence that introduces the two powers. Notice that the nourishing and growing power are distinguished here.
The author uses a pairing pattern in naming.
2
Understand the nourishing power's definition
Read the definition of the nourishing power: it assimilates food to the body, replacing what is lost.
3
Learn the three steps
Identify each of the three steps: acquisition of humor, attachment, and full assimilation. Note the disruptions for each step.
The disruptions are illustrated with medical examples.
4
Reflect on the overall process
Consider why these steps are necessary for survival and how they maintain the body's integrity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منها اثنتان يحتاج إليهما لبقاء الشخص، وتكميله في ذاته وهي الغاذية والنامية. والقياس المنمية إلا أنه روعي المزاوجة فأسند الفعل إلى السبب. فالغاذية التي لا بد منها في بقاء الشخص مدة حياته تشبه الغذاء بالمتغذي أي تحيل جسماً آخر إلى مشاكلة الجسم الذي تغذوه بدلاً لما يتحلل عنه. فيتم فعلها بأمور ثلاثة: الأول: تحصيل الخلط الذي هو بالقوة القريبة من الفعل شبيه بالعضو وقد يخل به عند عدم الغذاء في نفسه أو لضعف الجاذبة. الثاني: الألزق وهو أن يلصق ذلك الحاصل بالعضو ويجعله جزءاً منه بالفعل. وقد يخل به كما في الاستسقاء اللحمي فإن الغذاء فيه منبري عن العضو ولذلك يصير البدن مترهلاً أي مسترخياً. الثالث: أن يجعله بعد الإلصاق شبيهاً به من كل جهة حتى في قوامه ولونه وقد يخل به كما في البرص والبهق. وقد يثبت وقوفها أي وقوف الغاذية عن فعلها ضرورة الموت، وحينئذ لفساد المزاج بأن القوى الجسمانية متناهية في آثارها كما تقدم. وفي بعض النسخ وقد يثبت وقوفها بضرورة الموت وبأن القوى الجسمانية يعني أن ضرورة الموت تدل على وقوفها أيضاً. وإنما كان ضرورياً لأن الرطوبة الغريزية تنتقص بعد بنفس الوقوف وذلك أن الحرارة الغريزية والحرارة الخارجة والحركات النفسانية والبدنية تتعاضد في تحليلها حتى تنحل بالكلية فتغلب اليبوسة والرطوبة الغريبة وتنطفئ الحرارة الغريزية كانطفاء المصباح عند انتفاء الدهن وغلبة الماء ويحل الموت. والنامية التي لا بد منها في وصول الشخص إلى كماله تداخل الغذاء بين الأجزاء فتضمه إليها فتزيد في الأقطار الثلاثة بنسبة طبيعية أي تزيد في تلك الأقطار بنسبة تقتضيها طبيعة ذلك الشخص الذي له تلك القوة إلى غاية ما هي غاية النشو في ذلك الشخص ثم تقف عن فعلها لا كالورم فإنه ليس على النسبة الطبيعية بل خارج عن المجرى الطبيعي. والسمن فإنه قد يكون بعد حال النشو أيضاً كالورم وقد مر ما قيل من أن السمن لا يكون إلا في قطرين ومن أنه مخصوص باللحم وما في حكمه دون الأعضاء الأصلية كالعظم ونظائره. وذلك أي بيان وقوف النامية أنه لما كان البدن متولداً من الدم والمني فهو في الأول رطب في الغاية فيتأتى حينئذ نفوذ الغذاء بين أجزائه بسهولة ثم يجف يسيراً يسيراً ويتعسر النفوذ قليلاً ونفوذ الغذاء لا يكون إلا بتمدد الأعضاء فإذا جفت الأعضاء جفافاً كاملاً لم تقبل ذلك التمدد فلم يتصور نفوذ الغذاء فيها فوقفت النامية عن فعلها ضرورة وهل تبطل حينئذ بالكلية أو تبقى ذاتها فيه تردد والغاذية عدم النامية بتحصيل ما يتعلق به فعلها وهو ما زاد من على بدل مايتحلل فإذا ساواه الغذاء أو نقص عنه فات محل فعل النامية . قالوا : والغاذية في الأعضاء متخالفة الماهية فإن غاذية العظم تحيل الغذاء إلى ما يشبهه، وكذا غاذية اللحم وسائر الأعضاء فلو اتحدت طبائعها لا تحدت أفعالها .
Among them are two that are needed for the preservation of the individual and his perfection in himself: they are the nourishing and the growing. The pattern is that of growing, except that pairing was considered, so the action is attributed to the cause. The nourishing (power), which is necessary for the survival of the individual during his life, assimilates food to the organism being nourished, i.e., it transforms another body into a similarity to the body it nourishes, replacing what is dissolved from it. Its act is completed by three things: first, the acquisition of the humor that is potentially close to act, similar to the organ; this may be disrupted by lack of food itself or weakness of the attractive (power). Second, the attachment, which is to attach that acquired to the organ and make it actually part of it; this may be disrupted as in fleshy dropsy, for the food in it is separated from the organ, and therefore the body becomes flabby, i.e., relaxed. Third, to make it after attachment similar to it in every respect, even in its consistency and color; this may be disrupted as in vitiligo and leprosy. And its cessation, i.e., the cessation of the nourishing (power) from its action, is proven by the necessity of death, and then due to corruption of the temperament, because the bodily powers are finite in their effects, as previously mentioned. In some copies: its cessation is proven by the necessity of death and because the bodily powers; meaning that the necessity of death also indicates its cessation. And it was necessary because the innate moisture decreases after the cessation itself, and that is because the innate heat, external heat, and psychic and bodily movements cooperate in its analysis until it dissolves completely, then dryness and extrinsic moisture prevail, and the innate heat is extinguished like the extinguishing of a lamp when the oil is exhausted and water prevails, and death occurs. And the growing (power), which is necessary for the individual to reach his perfection, inserts food between the parts, attaches them to it, and increases in the three dimensions by a natural proportion, i.e., it increases in those dimensions according to a proportion required by the nature of that individual who has that power, up to the limit that is the limit of growth in that individual, then it stops from its action—not like a tumor, for it is not according to the natural proportion, but is outside the natural course. And obesity may occur after the state of growth as well, like a tumor, and it has been mentioned that obesity occurs only in two dimensions, and that it is specific to flesh and its like, not to original organs like bone and its peers. That is, the explanation of the cessation of the growing (power) is that since the body is generated from blood and semen, it is at first extremely moist; then the penetration of food between its parts is easy; then it dries little by little, and penetration becomes difficult a little; and the penetration of food is only by the expansion of the organs; when the organs dry completely, they do not accept that expansion, so the penetration of food is inconceivable in them, so the growing (power) stops from its action necessarily. And there is hesitation whether it then becomes completely invalid or its essence remains. And the nourishing (power) makes the growing (power) cease by acquiring that which its act is attached to, which is the increase over the replacement of what dissolves; if the food equals it or falls short, the locus of the growing (power)'s act is lost. They said: and the nourishing (power) in the organs is diverse in essence, for the nourishing (power) of bone transforms food into something similar, and likewise the nourishing (power) of flesh and the rest of the organs; if their natures were one, their acts would not be diverse.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed explanation of the two powers required for the individual: the nourishing power (الغاذية) and the growing power (النامية). It first explains that the nourishing power acts by transforming food into a substance similar to the body to replace what is lost. Its completion involves three steps: 1) acquiring a humor similar to the organ, 2) attaching it to the organ, and 3) making it fully similar in consistency and color. Disruptions are given for each: lack of food or weak attraction, dropsy, and skin diseases. The cessation of the nourishing power is proven by death, which occurs due to finite bodily powers and the loss of innate moisture caused by various heats and motions. The growing power then is described as inserting food between parts to increase size proportionally until the natural limit of growth, after which it stops—unlike a tumor which is unnatural. Obesity is noted as occurring after growth, but only in two dimensions and in flesh. The cessation of growth is explained by the body's initial moisture, gradual drying, and the inability of dry organs to expand for food penetration. There is a question whether the growing power becomes entirely invalid after cessation. Finally, it notes that the nourishing power hinders the growing when it only produces enough to replace loss, and that the nourishing power is diverse in different organs, as each organ's nourishing power transforms food specifically for that organ.

Key Terms
الغاذية
الخلط
الإلزاق
الاستسقاء اللحمي
البرص
البهق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the nourishing power only involves eating and digestion, not a three-step process.
Correction: The passage details that proper nourishing requires acquisition, attachment, and full assimilation, each with potential medical disruptions.

Checkpoint
What is the second step in the nourishing power's act?
A) Acquiring the humor
B) Attaching the food to the organ
C) Making it similar in consistency and color
D) Expanding the organ

Key Takeaway
The nourishing power acts through three precise steps—acquisition, attachment, and full assimilation—each susceptible to specific disruptions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The nourishing power replaces what is dissolved from the body every day. What is being 'dissolved' from your soul daily, and what consistent spiritual nourishment are you actively replacing it with? Name one practical change you will make this week.
?
Which of the three steps—acquisition, attachment, or full assimilation—do you personally struggle with most when trying to internalize beneficial knowledge or habits? Describe a specific example and what you will do to strengthen that step.
?
The passage warns of disruptions at each stage: lack of attraction, separation from the organ, or imperfect similarity. In your own life, what is a 'disruption' that prevents a virtue from truly becoming part of your character? How can you remove or overcome it starting today?

What Is Kalam?

Cessation of the Nourishing Power and Death
Proof and explanation of the nourishing power's cessation at death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the cessation of the nourishing power is necessary for death and the process involving the loss of innate moisture.

Core Idea
The nourishing power stops necessarily at death due to the finitude of bodily powers and the extinction of innate moisture.

After explaining the nourishing power's function, the author turns to its cessation. He argues that the nourishing power must stop acting when death occurs, which is inevitable. He then explains the mechanism: the body's innate moisture is constantly analyzed by heat and movements until it is exhausted, leading to death. This passage ties the bodily power's finitude to the logical necessity of death.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim about cessation
Read the first sentence: the cessation of the nourishing power is proven by the necessity of death and corruption of temperament.
The author references previous discussions about bodily powers being finite.
2
Understand the variant reading
Note the variant in some copies: both death and the finitude of bodily powers are cited.
3
Learn the process leading to death
Read the explanation: innate moisture decreases due to various heats and movements until it dissolves completely, causing dryness and extinguishing innate heat.
The analogy of a lamp extinguishing when oil runs out is used.
4
Reflect on the necessity
Consider why this cessation is 'necessary' and how it connects to the finite nature of bodily powers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منها اثنتان يحتاج إليهما لبقاء الشخص، وتكميله في ذاته وهي الغاذية والنامية. والقياس المنمية إلا أنه روعي المزاوجة فأسند الفعل إلى السبب. فالغاذية التي لا بد منها في بقاء الشخص مدة حياته تشبه الغذاء بالمتغذي أي تحيل جسماً آخر إلى مشاكلة الجسم الذي تغذوه بدلاً لما يتحلل عنه. فيتم فعلها بأمور ثلاثة: الأول: تحصيل الخلط الذي هو بالقوة القريبة من الفعل شبيه بالعضو وقد يخل به عند عدم الغذاء في نفسه أو لضعف الجاذبة. الثاني: الألزق وهو أن يلصق ذلك الحاصل بالعضو ويجعله جزءاً منه بالفعل. وقد يخل به كما في الاستسقاء اللحمي فإن الغذاء فيه منبري عن العضو ولذلك يصير البدن مترهلاً أي مسترخياً. الثالث: أن يجعله بعد الإلصاق شبيهاً به من كل جهة حتى في قوامه ولونه وقد يخل به كما في البرص والبهق. وقد يثبت وقوفها أي وقوف الغاذية عن فعلها ضرورة الموت، وحينئذ لفساد المزاج بأن القوى الجسمانية متناهية في آثارها كما تقدم. وفي بعض النسخ وقد يثبت وقوفها بضرورة الموت وبأن القوى الجسمانية يعني أن ضرورة الموت تدل على وقوفها أيضاً. وإنما كان ضرورياً لأن الرطوبة الغريزية تنتقص بعد بنفس الوقوف وذلك أن الحرارة الغريزية والحرارة الخارجة والحركات النفسانية والبدنية تتعاضد في تحليلها حتى تنحل بالكلية فتغلب اليبوسة والرطوبة الغريبة وتنطفئ الحرارة الغريزية كانطفاء المصباح عند انتفاء الدهن وغلبة الماء ويحل الموت. والنامية التي لا بد منها في وصول الشخص إلى كماله تداخل الغذاء بين الأجزاء فتضمه إليها فتزيد في الأقطار الثلاثة بنسبة طبيعية أي تزيد في تلك الأقطار بنسبة تقتضيها طبيعة ذلك الشخص الذي له تلك القوة إلى غاية ما هي غاية النشو في ذلك الشخص ثم تقف عن فعلها لا كالورم فإنه ليس على النسبة الطبيعية بل خارج عن المجرى الطبيعي. والسمن فإنه قد يكون بعد حال النشو أيضاً كالورم وقد مر ما قيل من أن السمن لا يكون إلا في قطرين ومن أنه مخصوص باللحم وما في حكمه دون الأعضاء الأصلية كالعظم ونظائره. وذلك أي بيان وقوف النامية أنه لما كان البدن متولداً من الدم والمني فهو في الأول رطب في الغاية فيتأتى حينئذ نفوذ الغذاء بين أجزائه بسهولة ثم يجف يسيراً يسيراً ويتعسر النفوذ قليلاً ونفوذ الغذاء لا يكون إلا بتمدد الأعضاء فإذا جفت الأعضاء جفافاً كاملاً لم تقبل ذلك التمدد فلم يتصور نفوذ الغذاء فيها فوقفت النامية عن فعلها ضرورة وهل تبطل حينئذ بالكلية أو تبقى ذاتها فيه تردد والغاذية عدم النامية بتحصيل ما يتعلق به فعلها وهو ما زاد من على بدل مايتحلل فإذا ساواه الغذاء أو نقص عنه فات محل فعل النامية . قالوا : والغاذية في الأعضاء متخالفة الماهية فإن غاذية العظم تحيل الغذاء إلى ما يشبهه، وكذا غاذية اللحم وسائر الأعضاء فلو اتحدت طبائعها لا تحدت أفعالها .
Among them are two that are needed for the preservation of the individual and his perfection in himself: they are the nourishing and the growing. The pattern is that of growing, except that pairing was considered, so the action is attributed to the cause. The nourishing (power), which is necessary for the survival of the individual during his life, assimilates food to the organism being nourished, i.e., it transforms another body into a similarity to the body it nourishes, replacing what is dissolved from it. Its act is completed by three things: first, the acquisition of the humor that is potentially close to act, similar to the organ; this may be disrupted by lack of food itself or weakness of the attractive (power). Second, the attachment, which is to attach that acquired to the organ and make it actually part of it; this may be disrupted as in fleshy dropsy, for the food in it is separated from the organ, and therefore the body becomes flabby, i.e., relaxed. Third, to make it after attachment similar to it in every respect, even in its consistency and color; this may be disrupted as in vitiligo and leprosy. And its cessation, i.e., the cessation of the nourishing (power) from its action, is proven by the necessity of death, and then due to corruption of the temperament, because the bodily powers are finite in their effects, as previously mentioned. In some copies: its cessation is proven by the necessity of death and because the bodily powers; meaning that the necessity of death also indicates its cessation. And it was necessary because the innate moisture decreases after the cessation itself, and that is because the innate heat, external heat, and psychic and bodily movements cooperate in its analysis until it dissolves completely, then dryness and extrinsic moisture prevail, and the innate heat is extinguished like the extinguishing of a lamp when the oil is exhausted and water prevails, and death occurs. And the growing (power), which is necessary for the individual to reach his perfection, inserts food between the parts, attaches them to it, and increases in the three dimensions by a natural proportion, i.e., it increases in those dimensions according to a proportion required by the nature of that individual who has that power, up to the limit that is the limit of growth in that individual, then it stops from its action—not like a tumor, for it is not according to the natural proportion, but is outside the natural course. And obesity may occur after the state of growth as well, like a tumor, and it has been mentioned that obesity occurs only in two dimensions, and that it is specific to flesh and its like, not to original organs like bone and its peers. That is, the explanation of the cessation of the growing (power) is that since the body is generated from blood and semen, it is at first extremely moist; then the penetration of food between its parts is easy; then it dries little by little, and penetration becomes difficult a little; and the penetration of food is only by the expansion of the organs; when the organs dry completely, they do not accept that expansion, so the penetration of food is inconceivable in them, so the growing (power) stops from its action necessarily. And there is hesitation whether it then becomes completely invalid or its essence remains. And the nourishing (power) makes the growing (power) cease by acquiring that which its act is attached to, which is the increase over the replacement of what dissolves; if the food equals it or falls short, the locus of the growing (power)'s act is lost. They said: and the nourishing (power) in the organs is diverse in essence, for the nourishing (power) of bone transforms food into something similar, and likewise the nourishing (power) of flesh and the rest of the organs; if their natures were one, their acts would not be diverse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that your body's innate heat and moisture are finite. When you feel your anger rising or your desires pulling you, which movements (psychic or bodily) are consuming your finite resources? How will you change your daily habits to use your body's energy for what actually pleases Allah, knowing that every movement brings you closer to the extinguishing of your lamp?
?
The author describes death as the extinguishing of a lamp when the oil is gone. Have you ever considered that your life is being consumed by your own movements? If you were to receive your account today, what movements—both apparent and hidden—would you wish you had directed towards your Creator rather than your whims?
?
What does the finitude of your bodily powers tell you about your need for the Eternal One? Do you truly live as if your strength is loaned to you for a limited time? What would it look like to start living every moment with the awareness that your lamp is burning out?

The Growing Power: Definition and Cessation
Definition and cessation of the growing power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the growing power's function in increasing the body proportionally and why it stops at maturity.

Core Idea
The growing power inserts food between parts to increase dimensions proportionally until the natural limit of growth, then stops.

The passage now turns to the growing power, which is necessary for reaching physical perfection. It explains that this power inserts food between the parts to increase the body in three dimensions according to a natural proportion. It then contrasts this with abnormal growth like tumors and notes that obesity can occur after growth but is limited. Finally, it explains the cessation of growth due to the drying of the body, which prevents the expansion needed for growth.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the growing power
Read the first part: the growing power inserts food between parts and increases in three dimensions by a natural proportion.
Note that it is for achieving perfection, not just survival.
2
Understand the contrast with tumors and obesity
Read the comparison: the growing power's increase is natural and proportional, unlike a tumor. Obesity is also discussed as a post-growth phenomenon in two dimensions only.
3
Learn the explanation of cessation
Read the explanation: the body initially moist allows easy food penetration, but as it dries, expansion becomes impossible, so growth stops necessarily.
There is a debate about whether the power becomes invalid or remains in essence.
4
Reflect on the natural limit
Consider why growth has a natural limit and how this relates to the body's material composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body grows to a limit set by your nature. Do you accept that limit, or do you resent your height, weight, or abilities? How does this affect your gratitude to your Creator?
?
The growing power stops when the organs dry and cannot expand. In your spiritual life, have you allowed your heart to dry to the point where it cannot expand to receive divine guidance? What can you do to moisten it again with humility and repentance?

Interaction of Nourishing and Growing Powers
Relationship between nourishing and growing powers, and diversity of nourishing power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the nourishing power can cause the growing power to cease and why the nourishing power varies among organs.

Core Idea
The nourishing power stops growth when it only supplies what is lost, and it differs in essence across organs.

After explaining the individual powers, the author addresses their relationship. He explains that the nourishing power can cause the growing power to cease by providing only enough to replace what is lost, leaving no excess for growth. He also notes that the nourishing power is not uniform; it varies in essence across different organs, as each organ's nourishing power transforms food specifically for that organ.

Guided Path
1
Read the relationship statement
Read the first sentence: the nourishing power causes the growing power to cease by acquiring only what is needed for replacement.
When food equals or falls short of the replacement, there is no excess for growth.
2
Understand the diversity of nourishing power
Read the second part: the nourishing power in each organ is different in essence, as it transforms food into that organ's specific substance.
If they were the same, the acts would not be diverse.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this diversity is necessary for the body's integrity and how it complements the functions of the nourishing and growing powers.
