Among them are two that are needed for the preservation of the individual and his perfection in himself: they are the nourishing and the growing. The pattern is that of growing, except that pairing was considered, so the action is attributed to the cause. The nourishing (power), which is necessary for the survival of the individual during his life, assimilates food to the organism being nourished, i.e., it transforms another body into a similarity to the body it nourishes, replacing what is dissolved from it. Its act is completed by three things: first, the acquisition of the humor that is potentially close to act, similar to the organ; this may be disrupted by lack of food itself or weakness of the attractive (power). Second, the attachment, which is to attach that acquired to the organ and make it actually part of it; this may be disrupted as in fleshy dropsy, for the food in it is separated from the organ, and therefore the body becomes flabby, i.e., relaxed. Third, to make it after attachment similar to it in every respect, even in its consistency and color; this may be disrupted as in vitiligo and leprosy. And its cessation, i.e., the cessation of the nourishing (power) from its action, is proven by the necessity of death, and then due to corruption of the temperament, because the bodily powers are finite in their effects, as previously mentioned. In some copies: its cessation is proven by the necessity of death and because the bodily powers; meaning that the necessity of death also indicates its cessation. And it was necessary because the innate moisture decreases after the cessation itself, and that is because the innate heat, external heat, and psychic and bodily movements cooperate in its analysis until it dissolves completely, then dryness and extrinsic moisture prevail, and the innate heat is extinguished like the extinguishing of a lamp when the oil is exhausted and water prevails, and death occurs. And the growing (power), which is necessary for the individual to reach his perfection, inserts food between the parts, attaches them to it, and increases in the three dimensions by a natural proportion, i.e., it increases in those dimensions according to a proportion required by the nature of that individual who has that power, up to the limit that is the limit of growth in that individual, then it stops from its action—not like a tumor, for it is not according to the natural proportion, but is outside the natural course. And obesity may occur after the state of growth as well, like a tumor, and it has been mentioned that obesity occurs only in two dimensions, and that it is specific to flesh and its like, not to original organs like bone and its peers. That is, the explanation of the cessation of the growing (power) is that since the body is generated from blood and semen, it is at first extremely moist; then the penetration of food between its parts is easy; then it dries little by little, and penetration becomes difficult a little; and the penetration of food is only by the expansion of the organs; when the organs dry completely, they do not accept that expansion, so the penetration of food is inconceivable in them, so the growing (power) stops from its action necessarily. And there is hesitation whether it then becomes completely invalid or its essence remains. And the nourishing (power) makes the growing (power) cease by acquiring that which its act is attached to, which is the increase over the replacement of what dissolves; if the food equals it or falls short, the locus of the growing (power)'s act is lost. They said: and the nourishing (power) in the organs is diverse in essence, for the nourishing (power) of bone transforms food into something similar, and likewise the nourishing (power) of flesh and the rest of the organs; if their natures were one, their acts would not be diverse.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed explanation of the two powers required for the individual: the nourishing power (الغاذية) and the growing power (النامية). It first explains that the nourishing power acts by transforming food into a substance similar to the body to replace what is lost. Its completion involves three steps: 1) acquiring a humor similar to the organ, 2) attaching it to the organ, and 3) making it fully similar in consistency and color. Disruptions are given for each: lack of food or weak attraction, dropsy, and skin diseases. The cessation of the nourishing power is proven by death, which occurs due to finite bodily powers and the loss of innate moisture caused by various heats and motions. The growing power then is described as inserting food between parts to increase size proportionally until the natural limit of growth, after which it stops—unlike a tumor which is unnatural. Obesity is noted as occurring after growth, but only in two dimensions and in flesh. The cessation of growth is explained by the body's initial moisture, gradual drying, and the inability of dry organs to expand for food penetration. There is a question whether the growing power becomes entirely invalid after cessation. Finally, it notes that the nourishing power hinders the growing when it only produces enough to replace loss, and that the nourishing power is diverse in different organs, as each organ's nourishing power transforms food specifically for that organ.

النامية
الماهية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the nourishing power is identical in all organs.
Correction: The passage argues that the nourishing power varies in essence across organs, as evidenced by the diversity of its acts.

Key Takeaway
The nourishing power can halt growth by providing only replacement, and it is essentially diverse across organs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body's nourishing power works daily to sustain you, but it only sustains—it does not cause growth unless there is excess. In your spiritual life, are you content with just 'replacement'—going through the motions to maintain your faith—or are you seeking the excess that leads to real growth? What is one area where you need to go beyond mere maintenance and strive for increase in nearness to Allah?
?
The nourishing power is diverse in each organ, tailored to its purpose. Allah has designed your unique strengths and weaknesses as well. Are you trying to be like someone else's 'nourishing power' instead of embracing the distinct way Allah has made you to serve Him? What specific gift has He given you that you are neglecting because you compare yourself to others?
?
If the nourishing power only provides enough to replace what is lost, growth stops. In your relationship with the Qur'an and worship, are you only doing enough to 'replace' what you lose from sin and distraction, or are you giving extra so that you can truly grow? Commit today to add one small act of worship that goes beyond your usual 'replacement' level.

The Generative and Formative Powers for the Species
Describes the generative and formative powers and the Hippocratic view on semen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the roles of the generative and formative powers in preserving the species, and the Hippocratic view on the nature of semen as derived from all organs.

Core Idea
The generative power produces seed from every organ, ensuring the likeness of the species.

Having covered the nourishing and growing powers that sustain the individual, we now turn to two powers that ensure the continuation of the species: the generative and the formative. This passage explains how the generative power works according to Hippocrates, who held that semen is produced from all parts of the body, making it diverse in essence yet uniform in mixture.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to get a sense of the author's flow.
Notice the passage ends abruptly; the focus is on the generative power.
2
Identify the two powers
Look for the names of the two powers: 'al-muwallidah' (generative) and 'al-musawwirah' (formative). Understand that they are needed for species preservation, not individual survival.
The text says they rely on the first two powers (nourishing and growing) mediated through the individual.
3
Understand the generative power's function
The generative power separates suitable matter from food after final digestion to become seed (bazr) for a like individual. This process mimics the asexual propagation of plants.
4
Examine the Hippocratic theory
Hippocrates held that the generative power exists throughout the body, and semen is formed from contributions of each organ: bone from bone, flesh from flesh, etc. Hence, semen is diverse in essence but uniformly mixed.
This theory explains how offspring resemble parents from all parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أي من الأربع المخدومة اثنتان يحتاج إليهما لبقاء النوع فقط مع كون بقائه محتاجاً إلى الأولين أيضاً بتوسط الشخص. وهما المولدة والمصورة فالمولدة تفصل من الغذاء بعد الهضم الأخير ما يصلح أن يكون مادة للمثل أي لمثل ذلك الشخص الذي فصلت منه البذر وهي في كل البدن كما ذهب إليه بقراط وأتباعه فإن المني عندهم يخرج من جميع الأعضاء فيخرج من العظم مثله ومن اللحم مثله . وعلى هذا فالمني متخالف الحقيقة متشابه الامتزاج لأن الحس لا يميز بين تلك
And among them, i.e., among the four served (powers), there are two that are needed for the preservation of the species only, while its preservation also needs the first two by means of the individual. They are the generative and the formative. The generative separates from the food after the last digestion what is suitable to be matter for the likeness, i.e., the likeness of that individual from which the seed was separated. And it (the generative power) is in all parts of the body, as held by Hippocrates and his followers, for according to them the semen comes out of all organs: from bone comes its like, and from flesh its like. On this basis, the semen is diverse in essence, similar in mixture, because the sense does not distinguish between those
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage presents the two remaining principal powers of the vegetative soul: the generative (المولدة) and the formative (المصورة). These are said to be needed for the preservation of the species, though they also depend on the earlier two (nourishing and growing) mediated through the individual. The generative power separates from food, after final digestion, material suitable to become seed for a like individual. According to Hippocrates and his followers, this power exists throughout the body, and semen is derived from all organs: bone from bone, flesh from flesh, etc. Consequently, semen has a diverse but uniformly mixed nature, indistinguishable by the senses.

The Generative and Formative Powers
Defines generative and formative powers with Aristotle and Canon.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the generative power (المولدة) and the formative power (المصورة) and their roles in embryonic development according to Aristotle and the Canon.

Core Idea
Generative power produces seed; formative power gives form to parts.

This lesson introduces two fundamental powers in the generation of living beings: the generative power, which produces semen, and the formative power, which organizes the parts into specific forms. These concepts come from Aristotle and the Canon of Medicine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the content.
2
Identify the two types of generative power
Notice that the passage divides the generative power into two types: one that generates semen in males and females, and another that separates the temperamental qualities in the semen.
The second type blends the parts according to each organ.
3
Understand the formative power
The formative power exists in the semen when in the womb and bestows forms, powers, shapes, and measurements upon the parts, actualizing them.
4
Reflect on Aristotle's view
Note that Aristotle said the power does not separate from the two, meaning the resulting semen is uniform in reality.
This sets the stage for the discussion of temperaments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأجزاء. وعند أرسطو أن تلك القوة لا تفارق الاثنتين فيكون المني المتولد هناك متشابه الحقيقة وفي كليات القانون أن المولدة نوعان نوع يولد المني في الذكر والأنثى . ونوع يفصل القوى التي في المني أي الكيفيات المزاجية لأن أجزاءه متخالفة الأمزجة فيمزجها تمزيجات بحسب عضو عضو فيخص للعصب مزاجاً خاصاً وكذا للعظم والشريان وغيرهما وذلك من مني متشابه الأجزاء أو متشابه الامتزاج ) والمصورة وهي توجد في المني عند كونه ( في الرحم خاصة تفيد تلك الأجزاء ) أي الأجزاء المتخالفة الحقيقة أو الاستعداد التي في المني ( الصور والقوى والأشكال والمقادير ( التي بها تصير مثلاً بالفعل بعد ما كانت مثلاً بالقوة وهاتان القوتان أعني المولدة والمصورة تخدمهما الغاذية وهو ظاهر، والنامية أيضا وذلك بأن تعظم الأعضاء وتوسع مجاريها حتى تصير إلى الهيئة الصالحة للتوليد. ولذلك لا يتكون المني إلا بعد عظم الأعضاء ( وهذه الأربع تخدمها أربع أخرى جعلها خادمة للأربع ) السابقة كلها لأنها تخدم الغاذية للنامية مع كونهما خادمتين للباقيتين كما . قوله : (لأن الحسن لا يميز متعلق بقوله متشابة الامتزاج وقوله في كليات القانون إلخ أي فيه إشارة إلى المذهبين المذكورين. وقوله : أي الكيفيات المزاجية تفسير للقوى التي كانت في المعنى . قوله : ( لأن أجزاءه متخالفة (الأمزجة فكيف يصح ما ذكره من كون أجزائه متشابهة الحقيقة؟ قلنا : يجوز اختلاف أجزاء المني في الأمزجة مع كونها متشابهة في الحقيقة لما عرفت أن مزاج كل نوع له عرض عريض فيتصور هناك اختلاف في الأمزجة ذلك عدم خروجها عن مچ [؟] الغرض فبهذا الاعتبار يكون بعضه صالحاً لأن يكون عظماً وبعضه صالحاً لأن يكون لحماً إلى غير ذلك . وقوله : فيمزجها أي يمزج ذلك النوع من المولدة تلك الأجزاء تمزيجات بحسب عضو عضو إلخ. وقوله : تفيد فاعل هذا الفعل هو الضمير المستتر راجع إلى المصورة، ومفعوله الأول هو قوله تلك الأجزاء ومفعوله الثاني هو قوله : الصور وقوله جعلها أي جعل هذه الأربع الأخرى. وقوله : لأنها أي لأن هذه الأربع الأخرى. وقوله : كما أي مر آنفاً من قوله : وهاتان القوتان إلخ . وقوله : فيجب أن تكون أي حركة الغذاء. وقوله : حتى تخرج أي المعدة. وقوله : عن قریب تفسير في المعنى لقوله : بعيد وهو تصغير بعد. وقوله : ويليق به أي بذلك .العضو.
The parts. And according to Aristotle, that power does not separate from the two, so the semen generated there is similar in reality. And in the Summaries of the Canon, the generative power is of two types: a type that generates semen in the male and female, and a type that separates the powers in the semen, i.e., the temperamental qualities, because its parts are of different temperaments, so it blends them with blendings according to each organ, so it assigns a specific temperament to the nerve, and similarly for bone, artery, and others, and that from semen of similar parts or similar mixture. And the formative power, which exists in the semen when it is in the womb specifically, benefits those parts—i.e., the parts of different reality or disposition that are in the semen—with forms, powers, shapes, and measurements by which they become actual examples after they were potential examples. These two powers, I mean the generative and the formative, are served by the nutritive, which is apparent, and also by the growth power, by enlarging the organs and widening their passages until they reach a suitable state for generation. Therefore, semen is not formed except after the organs are enlarged. And these four are served by four others, which He made servants of the previous four all, because they serve the nutritive and the growth power while they are servants of the remaining two, as [follows]. His saying: (Because the beautiful does not distinguish) is related to his saying: similar in mixture, and his saying: in the Summaries of the Canon etc., i.e., it indicates the two mentioned schools. And his saying: i.e., the temperamental qualities, is an explanation of the powers that were in the meaning. His saying: (Because its parts are of different temperaments, so how can what he mentioned about its parts being similar in reality be correct? We say: it is permissible for the parts of semen to differ in temperaments while being similar in reality, because you know that the temperament of each type has a wide range, so a difference in temperaments can be conceived such that they do not exit from the [؟] purpose, so by this consideration, some of it is suitable to be bone, some suitable to be flesh, etc.) And his saying: he blends them, i.e., that type of generative power blends those parts with blendings according to each organ etc. And his saying: benefits, the subject of this verb is the implicit pronoun referring to the formative power, its first object is his words: those parts, and its second object is his words: the forms. And his saying: He made them, i.e., He made these four others. And his saying: because they, i.e., because these four others. And his saying: as, i.e., has just passed from his saying: And these two powers etc. And his saying: so it must be, i.e., the movement of food. And his saying: until it exits, i.e., the stomach. And his saying: soon, an interpretation in meaning of his saying: far, it is a diminutive of after. And his saying: and it befits it, i.e., that organ.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification. It first presents Aristotle's view that a certain power (likely the generative) does not separate from two others, resulting in semen similar in reality. Then it cites the Canon (Qānūn) to divide the generative power into two types: one that generates semen in males and females, and another that separates the powers in the semen (the temperamental qualities) because the semen's parts have different temperaments. This second type blends these parts according to each organ's needs, assigning specific temperaments to nerves, bones, arteries, etc., from semen that is either uniform in parts or in mixture. Next, it defines the formative power, which exists in the semen when in the womb, and gives forms, powers, shapes, and measurements to the parts, actualizing them. These two powers (generative and formative) are served by the nutritive and growth powers: the nutritive power (apparent) and the growth power enlarge organs and widen passages for generation, which is why semen forms only after organs are mature. These four powers are served by four others, designated as servants for all the previous four, because they serve the nutritive and growth while those two serve the remaining two. The commentary then explains specific phrases: the connection of 'the beautiful does not distinguish' to 'similar in mixture'; the reference to two schools in the Canon; the explanation of 'temperamental qualities'; a question about how parts of different temperaments can be similar in reality, answered by noting that each temperament type has a wide range such that differences remain within the purpose; the blending by the generative power; the grammatical structure of 'benefits'; the meaning of 'He made them' and 'because they'; the reference to 'as' pointing back to earlier text; and interpretations of phrases about movement, exiting, and soon.

Key Terms

المصورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the generative and formative powers are the same or interchangeable.
Correction: They are distinct: the generative power produces and blends semen, while the formative power organizes parts into specific forms and structures.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many types does the generative power have according to the Canon?
A) One type
B) Two types
C) Three types
D) Four types

Key Takeaway
The generative power produces semen in two ways, while the formative power gives form and structure to the parts of the body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the formative power only acts in the womb, and it is responsible for giving you your unique form and structure, how does that change your sense of gratitude for the moment of your conception? Do you truly appreciate that your entire physical being was shaped by a divine purpose from the very beginning?
?
This passage describes a power that assigns specific temperaments to each organ — nerve, bone, artery. When you look at your own body, do you see it as a purposive design or as a random arrangement? What does this classification demand of you in terms of humility and wonder before the Creator?
?
The passage distinguishes between generative and formative powers. In your own life, are you more focused on 'producing' (generating) or 'shaping' (forming) your character and actions? Which power do you need to activate more consciously to become the person Allah wants you to be?

What Is Kalam?

The Nutritive, Growth, and Four Servient Powers
Describes servient powers serving generative and formative powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the nutritive and growth powers serve the generative and formative powers, and how four additional powers serve all of them.

Core Idea
Four servient powers assist the nutritive and growth powers in generation.

This lesson explains the hierarchy of powers in the body: the generative and formative powers are served by the nutritive and growth powers, which in turn are served by four other powers: attracting, digesting, retaining, and expelling. These work together in the process of generation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on servient powers
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully.
2
Identify the first two servants
Notice that the nutritive and growth powers serve the generative and formative powers. The nutritive power is apparent, while the growth power enlarges organs.
3
Understand the role of growth
The growth power enlarges organs and widens passages until they are suitable for generation, which is why semen forms only after organs are large.
4
Identify the four additional servants
These four powers (attracting, digesting, retaining, expelling) serve the four previous powers (generative, formative, nutritive, growth).
They serve the nutritive and growth, which in turn serve the first two.

الأجزاء. وعند أرسطو أن تلك القوة لا تفارق الاثنتين فيكون المني المتولد هناك متشابه الحقيقة وفي كليات القانون أن المولدة نوعان نوع يولد المني في الذكر والأنثى . ونوع يفصل القوى التي في المني أي الكيفيات المزاجية لأن أجزاءه متخالفة الأمزجة فيمزجها تمزيجات بحسب عضو عضو فيخص للعصب مزاجاً خاصاً وكذا للعظم والشريان وغيرهما وذلك من مني متشابه الأجزاء أو متشابه الامتزاج ) والمصورة وهي توجد في المني عند كونه ( في الرحم خاصة تفيد تلك الأجزاء ) أي الأجزاء المتخالفة الحقيقة أو الاستعداد التي في المني ( الصور والقوى والأشكال والمقادير ( التي بها تصير مثلاً بالفعل بعد ما كانت مثلاً بالقوة وهاتان القوتان أعني المولدة والمصورة تخدمهما الغاذية وهو ظاهر، والنامية أيضا وذلك بأن تعظم الأعضاء وتوسع مجاريها حتى تصير إلى الهيئة الصالحة للتوليد. ولذلك لا يتكون المني إلا بعد عظم الأعضاء ( وهذه الأربع تخدمها أربع أخرى جعلها خادمة للأربع ) السابقة كلها لأنها تخدم الغاذية للنامية مع كونهما خادمتين للباقيتين كما . قوله : (لأن الحسن لا يميز متعلق بقوله متشابة الامتزاج وقوله في كليات القانون إلخ أي فيه إشارة إلى المذهبين المذكورين. وقوله : أي الكيفيات المزاجية تفسير للقوى التي كانت في المعنى . قوله : ( لأن أجزاءه متخالفة (الأمزجة فكيف يصح ما ذكره من كون أجزائه متشابهة الحقيقة؟ قلنا : يجوز اختلاف أجزاء المني في الأمزجة مع كونها متشابهة في الحقيقة لما عرفت أن مزاج كل نوع له عرض عريض فيتصور هناك اختلاف في الأمزجة ذلك عدم خروجها عن مچ [؟] الغرض فبهذا الاعتبار يكون بعضه صالحاً لأن يكون عظماً وبعضه صالحاً لأن يكون لحماً إلى غير ذلك . وقوله : فيمزجها أي يمزج ذلك النوع من المولدة تلك الأجزاء تمزيجات بحسب عضو عضو إلخ. وقوله : تفيد فاعل هذا الفعل هو الضمير المستتر راجع إلى المصورة، ومفعوله الأول هو قوله تلك الأجزاء ومفعوله الثاني هو قوله : الصور وقوله جعلها أي جعل هذه الأربع الأخرى. وقوله : لأنها أي لأن هذه الأربع الأخرى. وقوله : كما أي مر آنفاً من قوله : وهاتان القوتان إلخ . وقوله : فيجب أن تكون أي حركة الغذاء. وقوله : حتى تخرج أي المعدة. وقوله : عن قریب تفسير في المعنى لقوله : بعيد وهو تصغير بعد. وقوله : ويليق به أي بذلك .العضو.
The parts. And according to Aristotle, that power does not separate from the two, so the semen generated there is similar in reality. And in the Summaries of the Canon, the generative power is of two types: a type that generates semen in the male and female, and a type that separates the powers in the semen, i.e., the temperamental qualities, because its parts are of different temperaments, so it blends them with blendings according to each organ, so it assigns a specific temperament to the nerve, and similarly for bone, artery, and others, and that from semen of similar parts or similar mixture. And the formative power, which exists in the semen when it is in the womb specifically, benefits those parts—i.e., the parts of different reality or disposition that are in the semen—with forms, powers, shapes, and measurements by which they become actual examples after they were potential examples. These two powers, I mean the generative and the formative, are served by the nutritive, which is apparent, and also by the growth power, by enlarging the organs and widening their passages until they reach a suitable state for generation. Therefore, semen is not formed except after the organs are enlarged. And these four are served by four others, which He made servants of the previous four all, because they serve the nutritive and the growth power while they are servants of the remaining two, as [follows]. His saying: (Because the beautiful does not distinguish) is related to his saying: similar in mixture, and his saying: in the Summaries of the Canon etc., i.e., it indicates the two mentioned schools. And his saying: i.e., the temperamental qualities, is an explanation of the powers that were in the meaning. His saying: (Because its parts are of different temperaments, so how can what he mentioned about its parts being similar in reality be correct? We say: it is permissible for the parts of semen to differ in temperaments while being similar in reality, because you know that the temperament of each type has a wide range, so a difference in temperaments can be conceived such that they do not exit from the [؟] purpose, so by this consideration, some of it is suitable to be bone, some suitable to be flesh, etc.) And his saying: he blends them, i.e., that type of generative power blends those parts with blendings according to each organ etc. And his saying: benefits, the subject of this verb is the implicit pronoun referring to the formative power, its first object is his words: those parts, and its second object is his words: the forms. And his saying: He made them, i.e., He made these four others. And his saying: because they, i.e., because these four others. And his saying: as, i.e., has just passed from his saying: And these two powers etc. And his saying: so it must be, i.e., the movement of food. And his saying: until it exits, i.e., the stomach. And his saying: soon, an interpretation in meaning of his saying: far, it is a diminutive of after. And his saying: and it befits it, i.e., that organ.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification. It first presents Aristotle's view that a certain power (likely the generative) does not separate from two others, resulting in semen similar in reality. Then it cites the Canon (Qānūn) to divide the generative power into two types: one that generates semen in males and females, and another that separates the powers in the semen (the temperamental qualities) because the semen's parts have different temperaments. This second type blends these parts according to each organ's needs, assigning specific temperaments to nerves, bones, arteries, etc., from semen that is either uniform in parts or in mixture. Next, it defines the formative power, which exists in the semen when in the womb, and gives forms, powers, shapes, and measurements to the parts, actualizing them. These two powers (generative and formative) are served by the nutritive and growth powers: the nutritive power (apparent) and the growth power enlarge organs and widen passages for generation, which is why semen forms only after organs are mature. These four powers are served by four others, designated as servants for all the previous four, because they serve the nutritive and growth while those two serve the remaining two. The commentary then explains specific phrases: the connection of 'the beautiful does not distinguish' to 'similar in mixture'; the reference to two schools in the Canon; the explanation of 'temperamental qualities'; a question about how parts of different temperaments can be similar in reality, answered by noting that each temperament type has a wide range such that differences remain within the purpose; the blending by the generative power; the grammatical structure of 'benefits'; the meaning of 'He made them' and 'because they'; the reference to 'as' pointing back to earlier text; and interpretations of phrases about movement, exiting, and soon.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the four servient powers serve only the generative and formative directly.
Correction: They serve the nutritive and growth powers, which then serve the generative and formative.

Checkpoint
What condition is necessary for semen to form according to the passage?
A) The generative power must be active
B) The organs must be enlarged by the growth power
C) The formative power must enter the womb
D) The four servient powers must be present

Key Takeaway
Generation requires a chain of servient powers: the nutritive and growth powers enlarge the body, enabling the generative and formative powers to produce semen and form the parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this passage, the generative power cannot act until the body has been enlarged by the growth power. In your own spiritual life, are you trying to produce ‘fruit’—like character, worship, or service—without allowing the necessary prior growth through patience, nourishment, and training? What specific ‘growth’ step have you been skipping?
?
The four servant powers (attractive, digestive, retentive, expulsive) work indirectly for the higher powers. Consider the habits and daily routines that serve your own higher goals. Are you giving proper attention to the ‘servant’ tasks—like sleep, diet, or planning—that enable your deeper aspirations? What one servant power do you neglect most?
?
The passage describes a clear chain of service: each level supports the one above. In your relationships—with family, community, or with Allah—is your role one of humble service that enables others to grow? Or do you demand immediate results without first being a servant? How can you better align yourself with the order of service you see here?

The Attractive Power in the Stomach (First Three Proofs)
First three proofs for the attractive power in the stomach

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first three proofs that establish the existence of an attractive power in the stomach, based on the movement of food, vomiting experiments, and animal behavior.

Core Idea
Food's upward journey proves a pulling force in the stomach.

The first of the five proofs for the attractive power focuses on the stomach. By examining how food moves from mouth to stomach despite being heavy, and how the stomach attracts sweet substances and even rises in some animals, we see clear evidence of an inherent attractive force.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: definition followed by three proofs.
The first proof uses logical deduction (not natural, not voluntary -> compulsory).
2
Identify the three observations
List the three observations: (1) food moves upward when swallowed upside-down, (2) sweet food comes out last in vomiting, (3) stomach rises in short-esophagus animals.
3
Understand the conclusion
Each observation leads to the conclusion that a pulling force must exist in the stomach. Follow the reasoning for each.
The common thread is that these phenomena cannot be explained by natural tendency or voluntary action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأولى : الجاذبة وهي التي تجذب المحتاج إليه من الغذاء ( وتدل على وجودها وجوه ) خمسة . ( الأول : حركة الغذاء من الفم إلى المعدة ليست طبيعية وإلا لامتنع تحركه إلى جهة العلو) بل كان يجب أن يتحرك إلى السفل وحده لكونه ثقيلا ( والتالي باطل إذا قد يزدرد) أي يبتلع (المنتكس) الغذاء ابتلاعاً تاماً. وحينئذ تكون حركته إلى علو ) ولا إرادية إما من الغذاء فإذ لا شعور (له فلا يتصور منه إرادة، وإما من المغتذى فإذ قد ينقلب الغذاء من الفم إلى المعدة عند شدة الحاجة إليه بلا إرادة من المغتذى بل قد يريد الإنسان منعه ليمضغه (فيغلبه) الغذاء وينجذب إلى داخل فوجب أن تكون قسرية فلا بد من ،قاسر وهو إما دفع من فوق بأن يقال : الحيوان يدفعه باختياره وقد ظهر بطلانه وإما جذب من تحت وهو أن تجذبه المعدة بقوة جاذبة فيها وهو المطلوب. الوجه ( الثاني : أنه متى تغذى الإنسان بغذاء ثم يتناول بعده شيئاً ( حلواً واستعمل القيء وجد آخر ما يخرج بالقيء الحلو وليس) ذلك ) إلا لجذب المعدة له أي للحلو ) إلى قعرها بواسطة محبتها إياه طبعا ( وإذا تناول الإنسان دواءً ) مراً كريهاً فالمريء والمعدة يرومان نفضه ولفظه ولا يزدردانه إلا بعسر فربما اندفع بالقيء بلا اختياره الوجه ( الثالث : قد تصعد المعدة لجذب الغذاء في بعض الحيوان القصير المريء ) كالتمساح حتى تخرج عند الاغتذاء بحيث تلاقي فمه لكونه واسعاً وما ذلك إلا لشوقها إلى اجتذاب الغذاء. فدلت هذه الوجوه الثلاثة على أن في المعدة قوة جاذبة. الوجه الرابع : الرحم بعد انقطاع كأنه ( الطمث ) عن قريب ( إذا خلا عن الفضول يشتد شوقه إلى المني حتى يحس يجذب الإحليل إلى داخله جذب المحجمة (الدم إلى داخلها وقد سمى بعضهم الرحم حيوانا مشتاقا المني . فثبت بهذا الوجه وجود الجاذبة في الرحم . الوجه ( الخامس : الدم الذي يكون في الكبد مخلوطاً بالفضلات الثلاث أعني البلغم والصفراء والسوداء ( ثم تتمايز) تلك الأمور المختلطة ) وينصب إلى كل عضو نوع من الرطوبة يليق به فلولا أن في كل عضو قوة جاذبة لتلك الرطوبة ( اللائقة به ( لامتنع ذلك ) التمايز وانصباب كل رطوبة إلى عضو على حده دائماً أو أكثرياً وهذه حجة واضحة على وجود القوة الجاذبة في جملة الأعضاء
The first: the attractive power, which is that which attracts the needed part of the food. And its existence is indicated by five proofs. The first: the movement of food from the mouth to the stomach is not natural, otherwise its movement upward would be prevented, but it would have to move downward alone because it is heavy. And the consequent is false, since indeed a person may swallow (i.e., gulp) food when inverted, completely. Then its movement is upward. And it is not voluntary, either on the part of the food, for it has no consciousness so no will can be imagined from it, or on the part of the eater, for indeed food may move from the mouth to the stomach when there is severe need for it, without the will of the eater; rather, the person may want to prevent it to chew it, but the food overwhelms him and is attracted inward. So it must be compulsory, so there must be a compeller, which is either a push from above by saying: the animal pushes it voluntarily, and its falsehood has become clear, or an attraction from below, which is that the stomach attracts it by an attractive power in it, which is what is sought. The second: when a person eats some food, then takes something sweet after it and induces vomiting, the last thing that comes out by vomiting is found to be sweet, and that is only due to the stomach's attraction of it (i.e., the sweet) to its bottom by means of its natural love for it. And when a person takes a bitter medicine, the esophagus and stomach attempt to expel and reject it and do not swallow it except with difficulty, and perhaps it is ejected by vomiting involuntarily. The third: the stomach may ascend to attract food in some animals with a short esophagus, like the crocodile, until it comes out at the time of feeding so that it meets its mouth because it is wide, and that is only due to its desire to attract food. So these three proofs indicate that there is an attractive power in the stomach. The fourth: the uterus, after the cessation of menstruation, when it is free from excess fluids, its desire for semen intensifies until it is felt to attract the urethra into its interior like the cupping glass attracts blood into its interior. And some have called the uterus an animal longing for semen. So by this proof, the existence of the attractive power in the uterus is established. The fifth: the blood that is in the liver mixed with the three waste products, namely phlegm, yellow bile, and black bile, then these mixed matters become differentiated, and each organ receives a type of moisture suitable for it. Were it not that in each organ there is an attractive power for that moisture suitable for it, that differentiation and flow of each moisture to its organ constantly or mostly would be prevented. And this is a clear proof of the existence of the attractive power in all organs.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage presents five proofs for the existence of the attractive power (الجاذبة) in the body. The first proof observes that food moves upward from the mouth to the stomach, which is not natural for a heavy substance, nor voluntary (since the food has no will and sometimes the eater cannot prevent it). Thus, a compeller is needed, which can only be a pulling force from the stomach. The second proof uses the example of vomiting: sweet food put after a meal comes out last because the stomach attracts it to its bottom due to natural affinity; bitter medicine is rejected. The third proof notes that some animals (like the crocodile) bring their stomach up to meet the food, showing attraction. These three prove an attractive power in the stomach. The fourth proof shows the uterus attracting semen, seen in its strong desire post-menstruation. The fifth proof argues from the differentiation of blood in the liver: each organ attracts the moisture that suits it, proving an attractive power in all organs collectively. The passage thus establishes the attractive power as a necessary faculty.

Key Terms
الجاذبة
الطبيعي
الإرادي
القسري
المعدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The attractive power is only in the stomach.
Correction: While these proofs focus on the stomach, later proofs will show that the attractive power exists in all organs.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument of the first proof for the attractive power?
A) Food moves downward naturally.
B) Food moves upward voluntarily.
C) Food's upward movement must be forced because it is neither natural nor voluntary.
D) The stomach pushes food upward.

Key Takeaway
The stomach's attraction of food is proven by three empirical and logical observations, establishing an inherent attractive power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your stomach pulls food to sustain you without your conscious effort. How often do you reflect on the countless involuntary blessings that keep you alive? What would change in your gratitude if you recognised Allah's hand in every bite you swallow?
?
The crocodile's stomach rises to meet its food out of desire. What desires drive your own inner attractions—towards what is beneficial or harmful? Are you aware of the 'pull' of your habits?
?
This proof shows that even the movement of food demands a compelling force. What is the compelling force in your life that directs your actions toward Allah? If it is weak, what can you do to strengthen that attraction?

The Attractive Power in the Uterus (Fourth Proof)
Fourth proof: the attractive power in the uterus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof for the attractive power, which demonstrates its existence in the uterus based on its longing for semen after menstruation.

Core Idea
The uterus's eager attraction of semen proves a separate attractive power.

The fourth proof for the attractive power shifts focus from the stomach to the uterus. After menstruation, the uterus displays a strong desire to draw in semen, which can be physically felt, analogous to a cupping glass attracting blood. This observation establishes the attractive power in the uterus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the fourth proof. Notice the description of the uterus's behavior.
The proof uses an analogy to cupping glass.
2
Understand the phenomenon
The uterus, after menstruation, has a strong longing for semen. This longing causes it to attract the urethra inward.
3
Recognize the conclusion
This attraction cannot be explained by the uterus's own will or natural inclination alone; it implies a specific attractive power.
The passage also notes that some have called the uterus 'an animal longing for semen'.

الأولى : الجاذبة وهي التي تجذب المحتاج إليه من الغذاء ( وتدل على وجودها وجوه ) خمسة . ( الأول : حركة الغذاء من الفم إلى المعدة ليست طبيعية وإلا لامتنع تحركه إلى جهة العلو) بل كان يجب أن يتحرك إلى السفل وحده لكونه ثقيلا ( والتالي باطل إذا قد يزدرد) أي يبتلع (المنتكس) الغذاء ابتلاعاً تاماً. وحينئذ تكون حركته إلى علو ) ولا إرادية إما من الغذاء فإذ لا شعور (له فلا يتصور منه إرادة، وإما من المغتذى فإذ قد ينقلب الغذاء من الفم إلى المعدة عند شدة الحاجة إليه بلا إرادة من المغتذى بل قد يريد الإنسان منعه ليمضغه (فيغلبه) الغذاء وينجذب إلى داخل فوجب أن تكون قسرية فلا بد من ،قاسر وهو إما دفع من فوق بأن يقال : الحيوان يدفعه باختياره وقد ظهر بطلانه وإما جذب من تحت وهو أن تجذبه المعدة بقوة جاذبة فيها وهو المطلوب. الوجه ( الثاني : أنه متى تغذى الإنسان بغذاء ثم يتناول بعده شيئاً ( حلواً واستعمل القيء وجد آخر ما يخرج بالقيء الحلو وليس) ذلك ) إلا لجذب المعدة له أي للحلو ) إلى قعرها بواسطة محبتها إياه طبعا ( وإذا تناول الإنسان دواءً ) مراً كريهاً فالمريء والمعدة يرومان نفضه ولفظه ولا يزدردانه إلا بعسر فربما اندفع بالقيء بلا اختياره الوجه ( الثالث : قد تصعد المعدة لجذب الغذاء في بعض الحيوان القصير المريء ) كالتمساح حتى تخرج عند الاغتذاء بحيث تلاقي فمه لكونه واسعاً وما ذلك إلا لشوقها إلى اجتذاب الغذاء. فدلت هذه الوجوه الثلاثة على أن في المعدة قوة جاذبة. الوجه الرابع : الرحم بعد انقطاع كأنه ( الطمث ) عن قريب ( إذا خلا عن الفضول يشتد شوقه إلى المني حتى يحس يجذب الإحليل إلى داخله جذب المحجمة (الدم إلى داخلها وقد سمى بعضهم الرحم حيوانا مشتاقا المني . فثبت بهذا الوجه وجود الجاذبة في الرحم . الوجه ( الخامس : الدم الذي يكون في الكبد مخلوطاً بالفضلات الثلاث أعني البلغم والصفراء والسوداء ( ثم تتمايز) تلك الأمور المختلطة ) وينصب إلى كل عضو نوع من الرطوبة يليق به فلولا أن في كل عضو قوة جاذبة لتلك الرطوبة ( اللائقة به ( لامتنع ذلك ) التمايز وانصباب كل رطوبة إلى عضو على حده دائماً أو أكثرياً وهذه حجة واضحة على وجود القوة الجاذبة في جملة الأعضاء
The first: the attractive power, which is that which attracts the needed part of the food. And its existence is indicated by five proofs. The first: the movement of food from the mouth to the stomach is not natural, otherwise its movement upward would be prevented, but it would have to move downward alone because it is heavy. And the consequent is false, since indeed a person may swallow (i.e., gulp) food when inverted, completely. Then its movement is upward. And it is not voluntary, either on the part of the food, for it has no consciousness so no will can be imagined from it, or on the part of the eater, for indeed food may move from the mouth to the stomach when there is severe need for it, without the will of the eater; rather, the person may want to prevent it to chew it, but the food overwhelms him and is attracted inward. So it must be compulsory, so there must be a compeller, which is either a push from above by saying: the animal pushes it voluntarily, and its falsehood has become clear, or an attraction from below, which is that the stomach attracts it by an attractive power in it, which is what is sought. The second: when a person eats some food, then takes something sweet after it and induces vomiting, the last thing that comes out by vomiting is found to be sweet, and that is only due to the stomach's attraction of it (i.e., the sweet) to its bottom by means of its natural love for it. And when a person takes a bitter medicine, the esophagus and stomach attempt to expel and reject it and do not swallow it except with difficulty, and perhaps it is ejected by vomiting involuntarily. The third: the stomach may ascend to attract food in some animals with a short esophagus, like the crocodile, until it comes out at the time of feeding so that it meets its mouth because it is wide, and that is only due to its desire to attract food. So these three proofs indicate that there is an attractive power in the stomach. The fourth: the uterus, after the cessation of menstruation, when it is free from excess fluids, its desire for semen intensifies until it is felt to attract the urethra into its interior like the cupping glass attracts blood into its interior. And some have called the uterus an animal longing for semen. So by this proof, the existence of the attractive power in the uterus is established. The fifth: the blood that is in the liver mixed with the three waste products, namely phlegm, yellow bile, and black bile, then these mixed matters become differentiated, and each organ receives a type of moisture suitable for it. Were it not that in each organ there is an attractive power for that moisture suitable for it, that differentiation and flow of each moisture to its organ constantly or mostly would be prevented. And this is a clear proof of the existence of the attractive power in all organs.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage presents five proofs for the existence of the attractive power (الجاذبة) in the body. The first proof observes that food moves upward from the mouth to the stomach, which is not natural for a heavy substance, nor voluntary (since the food has no will and sometimes the eater cannot prevent it). Thus, a compeller is needed, which can only be a pulling force from the stomach. The second proof uses the example of vomiting: sweet food put after a meal comes out last because the stomach attracts it to its bottom due to natural affinity; bitter medicine is rejected. The third proof notes that some animals (like the crocodile) bring their stomach up to meet the food, showing attraction. These three prove an attractive power in the stomach. The fourth proof shows the uterus attracting semen, seen in its strong desire post-menstruation. The fifth proof argues from the differentiation of blood in the liver: each organ attracts the moisture that suits it, proving an attractive power in all organs collectively. The passage thus establishes the attractive power as a necessary faculty.

الجاذبة
المني
المحجمة

Key Takeaway
The uterus's active longing for semen after menstruation demonstrates a second instance of the attractive power.

?

Starting today, pause each morning to reflect on one bodily function as a reminder of the Creator's care and precision.
?
If the uterus actively seeks out semen, this challenges any notion of randomness in reproduction. What is one concrete step you can take to align your life with the purposeful design of creation, rather than living as if things happen by chance?

The Attractive Power in All Organs (Fifth Proof)
Fifth proof: the attractive power in all organs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth proof for the attractive power, which argues that the differentiation of blood in the liver proves that each organ has an attractive power to pull its suitable moisture.

Core Idea
Each organ's selective attraction of its needed moisture proves the attractive power is universal.

The final proof for the attractive power takes a broader view. The liver produces blood mixed with various substances, yet each organ receives exactly the moisture that suits it. This precise distribution can only be explained if each organ possesses an attractive power that pulls what it needs. Thus, the attractive power exists in all organs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the fifth proof. Notice the description of blood separation in the liver.
2
Understand the process
The liver contains blood mixed with phlegm, yellow bile, and black bile. These separate and each organ draws the moisture suitable for it.
This happens constantly or mostly.
3
Grasp the necessity
Without an attractive power in each organ, such precise distribution would be impossible. Therefore, the attractive power must exist in all organs.
This is a clear and general proof.

The Digestive Power and Its Distinction from the Nutritive Power
Defines the digestive power and distinguishes it from the nutritive power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the digestive power and how it differs from the nutritive power in Islamic philosophical medicine.

Core Idea
The digestive power prepares food; the nutritive power incorporates it.

This passage from the Sharh al-Mawaqif explains the second of the four servient powers: the digestive power. It is crucial to distinguish it from the nutritive power because they perform different functions. The text draws on the Canon and Imam al-Razi's commentary to clarify this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the digestive power
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the digestive power is defined as preparing food to become an actual part of the organ, and that it is explicitly stated to be other than the nutritive power.
The passage uses the phrase 'فهي غير الغاذية' which directly asserts difference.
2
Examine the quote from the Canon
The passage includes a definition from the Canon (Kulliyat al-Qanun). Compare this definition with the initial one: it adds details about transforming attracted and held matter into a consistency and temperament ready for further change.
This shows the digestive power's role as a preparatory stage.
3
Understand al-Razi's confirmation and the physiological process
Imam al-Razi confirms the distinction, and the passage explains the process: blood form is annulled and organic form arises through a 'cooking' that changes the disposition of the matter. Reflect on how this supports the distinction between digestive and nutritive powers.
The cooking (الطبخ) is key to the transformation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثانية ) من الأربع الخادمة ( الهاضمة وهي تعد الغذاء إلى أن يصير جزءاً بالفعل) من العضو (فهي غير الغاذية أعني صيرورتها ) أي أعني القوة التي تقتضي صيرورة الأغذية ( جزءا بالفعل ) من الأعضاء، ( وفي كليات القانون وأما الهاضمة فهي التي تحيل ما جذبته الجاذبة وأمسكته الماسكة إلى قوام مهيئاً لفعل القوة المغيرة فيه وإلى مزاج صالح للاستحالة إلى الغذائية بالفعل. قال الإمام الرازي: هذا الكلام نص في أن القوة الهاضمة غير القوة الغاذية ويؤيده أنه جعل الغاذية مخدومة للقوى الأربع التي منها الهاضمة فلنتكلم في الفرق . فنقول : إذا جذبت جاذبة عضو شيئاً من الدم وأمسكته ماسكته فللدم صورة نوعية وإذا صار شبيها بالعضو فقد بطلت عنه هذه الصورة وحدثت صورة أخرى عضوية، فهناك كون للصورة العضوية، وفساد للصورة الدموية وإنما يحصلان إذا كان من الطبخ ما لأجله ينتقص استعداد المادة للصورة الدموية ويشتد استعدادها
The second of the four servient powers: the digestive power, which prepares the food until it becomes actually a part of the organ. So it is other than the nutritive power—I mean its becoming—i.e., I mean the power that necessitates the becoming of foods as an actual part of the organs. And in the Summaries of the Canon: 'As for the digestive power, it is that which transforms what the attractive power attracted and the retentive power held into a consistency prepared for the action of the changing power upon it, and into a temperament suitable for transformation into actual nutriment.' Imam al-Razi said: This statement is clear that the digestive power is other than the nutritive power, and it is supported by the fact that he made the nutritive power served by the four powers, among which is the digestive power. So let us discuss the difference. We say: When the attractive power of an organ attracts something from the blood and the retentive power holds it, the blood has a specific form. When it becomes similar to the organ, this form is annulled and another organic form occurs. So there is a coming-to-be of the organic form and a corruption of the blood form, and they occur only if there is a cooking by which the disposition of the matter for the blood form decreases and its disposition for [the organic form] intensifies.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage defines the second servient power, the digestive power (الهاضمة), as preparing food to become an actual part of an organ. It explicitly distinguishes it from the nutritive power (الغاذية): the digestive power is not the same as the power that makes food become part of the organ (the nutritive). The passage then quotes the Canon (Qānūn) defining the digestive power as transforming attracted and retained material into a consistency and temperament ready for transformation into actual nourishment. Imam al-Razi confirms that this clearly shows the digestive power is distinct, because the nutritive power is served by the four powers including the digestive. The discussion then explains the difference: when blood is attracted and held by an organ, it has a 'blood form'; digestion ('cooking') weakens the blood form and strengthens the disposition for an organic form, thereby producing actual assimilation. The passage thus establishes the digestive power as a distinct preparatory faculty.

Key Terms
الهاضمة
الغاذية
الماسكة
القوة المغيرة
الطبخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The digestive power and nutritive power are the same thing.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the digestive power is other than the nutritive power. The digestive power prepares food by transforming it, while the nutritive power makes it become an actual part of the organ.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the digestive power do to the attracted and retained matter?
A) It directly turns it into an actual part of the organ.
B) It transforms it into a consistency and temperament suitable for further change into nutriment.
C) It attracts it from the stomach to the liver.
D) It expels it from the body.

Key Takeaway
The digestive power transforms food into a state ready for assimilation, while the nutritive power actually incorporates it into the organ.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your spiritual life, what 'digestive' process do you need to allow the Word of Allah to be prepared in your heart before it becomes a part of you? Are you rushing to incorporate lessons without proper reflection?
?
Just as the digestive power requires 'cooking' (trials) to transform raw matter, what ongoing difficulty is Allah using to prepare you for deeper growth? Are you resisting the process or trusting the Cook?
?
The passage shows that without the digestive power, the nutritive power cannot work. What preparatory steps in learning Islam (e.g., memorization, study of Arabic) have you neglected, expecting to absorb wisdom without the groundwork?

The Three Meanings of Food
Defines 'food' and distinguishes three meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of food (al-ghidha) and its three distinct meanings according to Imam al-Razi, and why it might be mistakenly thought to be four.

Core Idea
Food has three meanings: potential, near-actual, and actual.

This lesson examines a nuanced definition of food from the perspective of Islamic theology. It clarifies how the term 'food' can refer to different stages of transformation, from raw grain to fully incorporated nourishment. By studying Imam al-Razi's precise wording, we discover that what might appear to be four meanings are actually three.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and translation
Read the Arabic definition carefully and the literal translation. Notice the initial definition of food as 'that which replaces what dissolves by transformation into its kind.'
The definition is technical and relates to bodily nourishment.
2
Notice the potential confusion
The phrase 'by close and remote potential' might suggest four meanings. The author warns that this phrase 'gives the impression that food has four meanings.'
This sets up the need for clarification.
3
Analyze al-Razi's breakdown
Imam al-Razi's wording lists three meanings: (1) potential, like wheat; (2) food needing only adhesion to become firm; (3) food that has become part of the nourished substance. Identify each meaning in the Arabic.
The commentator explains that 'and it may be said' is a detailed explanation of what precedes, not an additional meaning.
4
Understand the commentator's point
The commentator notes that if al-Razi had used the letter 'fa' instead of 'wa', it would have been obvious there are three meanings. Reflect on how small particles can affect interpretation.
This highlights the precision required in scholarly texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التنبيه الثاني : الغذاء ما يقوم بدل ما يتحلل من الشيء بالاستحالة إلى نوعه. ويقال لما هو غذاء بالفعل وبالقوة القريبة والبعيدة. هذه العبارة توهم أن للغذاء معاني أربعة وعبارة الإمام الرازي في كتابيه هكذا : الغذاء هو الذي يقوم بدل ما يتحلل عن الشيء بالاستحالة إلى نوعه. وقد يقال : له غذاء وهو يعد بالقوة غذاء كالحنطة ويقال له غذاء إذا لم يحتج إلى غير الالتصاق في الانعقاد ويقال له غذاء عند ما صار جزءاً من المغتذى تشبيهاً به بالفعل. فقوله : وقد يقال له تفصيل لما قبله بلا شبهة فلو كان بالفاء لكان أظهر ولم يشتبه على أحد أن معانيه ثلاثة.
The second note: Food is that which replaces what dissolves from a thing by transforming into its kind. And it is said for what is food actually and by close and remote potential. This phrase gives the impression that food has four meanings. The statement of Imam al-Razi in his two books is as follows: Food is that which replaces what dissolves from a thing by transforming into its kind. And it may be said: it is food and it is considered by potential food like wheat, and it is said to be food when it does not need anything other than adhesion to become firm, and it is said to be food when it has become a part of the nourished substance, analogous to it actually. So his statement 'and it may be said' is a detailed explanation of what precedes, without doubt. If it were with the letter fa', it would be clearer, and no one would doubt that its meanings are three.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage defines 'food' (al-ghidha) and clarifies its meanings. It begins with the definition: food is what replaces what dissolves (from the body) by transforming into the kind of thing it is replacing. The phrase 'by close and remote potential' might suggest four meanings, but Imam al-Razi's statement shows that there are three: (1) potential food, like wheat which can become food; (2) food that only needs adhesion to be fully incorporated; (3) actual food that has become part of the body. The commentary explains that al-Razi's wording, if it had used the particle 'fa' instead of 'and', would have made it obvious that there are three meanings, not four.

بدل ما يتحلل
الاستحالة
نوع

بالقوة
الحنطة
الالتصاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think there are four meanings of food due to the phrase 'by close and remote potential'.
Correction: Actually, there are three meanings: potential, near-actual, and actual, as clarified by Imam al-Razi.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Razi, how many meanings does the term 'food' (al-ghidha) have?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Food, in the technical sense, has three meanings: potential (e.g., wheat), near-actual (needs only adhesion), and actual (part of the body).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After learning that food has three distinct meanings, consider how often you conflate potential with actual in your own understanding. Can you recall a recent instance where you treated something as already achieved when it was only potential?
?
The passage emphasizes that the word 'food' is used for all three stages analogously. How does this lesson challenge you to reconsider the precision of your own definitions and categories in matters of faith and practice?

Defending Common Sense Against Two Objections
Sanusi presents and refutes two objections against common sense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defends the existence of common sense by answering two key objections.

Core Idea
Common sense exists; objections based on size and brain function are answered.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses two direct challenges to the reality of common sense: one about physical size and another about brain involvement. By examining his responses, we see how rational proofs support inner faculties. This lesson unpacks his arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two objections
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the structure: the opponent gives two proofs (وجهين). Highlight each objection and its response.
The first objection involves a dream image; the second involves analogy with limbs.
2
Analyze the first objection and its response
The opponent says a large object (mountain of ruby) cannot exist in a small part of the body. Sanusi replies that the phantom, not the exact image, is imprinted.
This clarifies that imprinting is representational, not literal.
3
Analyze the second objection and its response
The opponent argues that just as we don't perceive with arbitrary limbs, we don't perceive through the brain. Sanusi denies the claim of brain non-intermediation and accepts that the brain is not a bodily instrument as the opponent meant.
Sanusi distinguishes between the brain's role in perception and the opponent's flawed proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - النوع الثاني : القوة المدركة الباطنة . حساً باطناً . ( ولقائل أن يقول : لعل المدرك لها النفس كما مر) من أنها تدرك الكلي والجزئي أيضا وامتناع ارتسام الصور التي لها مقدار فيها غير مسلم عندنا لما عرفت آنفاً . ( واحتج الخصم ) النافي للحس المشترك (بوجهين : الأول: أن حصول جبل من ياقوت وبحر من زئبق ) كما يرى في النوم ( في جزء من بدن النائم ضروري البطلان قلنا : قد ينطبع شبح الكبير في الصغير) إنما الممتنع أن يرتسم عين الكبير في الصغير ( كما مر الثاني : كما نعلم أنا لا نشم الروائح ( ولا نذوق ) الطعوم ( ولا نسمع الأصوات ) ولا نبصر الألوان ( بالأيدي والأرجل ) ، كذلك ( نعلم أنا لا نذوق ولا نلمس ولا نعقل شيئاً مما ذكرناه ( بالدماغ، ومنكره مكابر لإنكار ما يجده كل عاقل من نفسه . ( فنعم ) إذ لا نزاع لنا فيه .
The first observation - the second type: The inner perceptive power. An inner sense. (And one might say: perhaps the perceiver of it is the soul, as passed) because it perceives the universal and the particular also, and the impossibility of the imprinting of images that have magnitude in it is not conceded by us, as you have known above. (And the opponent) who denies common sense (argued with two proofs: First: that the occurrence of a mountain of ruby and a sea of mercury) as seen in sleep (in a part of the body of the sleeper is necessarily false. We said: the phantom of the large may be imprinted in the small) only the exact image of the large being imprinted in the small is impossible (as passed. Second: just as we know that we do not smell odors) (nor taste) tastes (nor hear sounds) nor see colors (with hands and feet), likewise (we know that we do not taste nor touch nor understand anything of what we mentioned) through the brain, and one who denies it is obstinate in denying what every rational person finds in himself. (We said: the non-intermediation of the brain in it) i.e., in sensory perception (is not conceded) and what you mentioned does not prove it. (As for it) i.e., the brain (not being a bodily instrument) i.e., its substance is not an instrument for the mentioned sensations as your proof required. (Yes) for there is no dispute from us about it.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage defines common sense as an inner sense and addresses two objections against its existence. The first objection argues that it is impossible for large images (like a mountain) to be imprinted in a small part of the body. The answer explains that it is not the exact image but a phantom that is imprinted. The second objection claims that just as we know we do not perceive through limbs arbitrarily, we know we do not perceive through the brain. The answer denies the claim of brain non-involvement and clarifies that the opponent's proof does not show the brain is not an instrument, which the author accepts. The passage follows an objection-response structure, defending the existence of common sense against these challenges.

The Definition and Function of Imagination
Sanusi defines imagination and explains its necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how imagination (al-khayāl) is defined in Islamic kalam as the power that preserves sensory forms when objects are absent, and why this power is necessary for recognition and order in life.

Core Idea
Imagination is the treasury of common sense, preserving forms when objects disappear.

Sanusi begins by introducing imagination as the second inner perceptive power. He defines it as the preserver of forms received by common sense, and explains that without it, we could not recognize previously seen things or distinguish beneficial from harmful. This passage lays the foundation for the following proofs of its existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice how Sanusi defines imagination and immediately gives its function: preserving forms from common sense when external objects are absent.
The phrase 'كالخزانة له' (like a treasury) is a vivid metaphor.
2
Identify the consequences of lacking imagination
The passage states that without this power, knowledge of previously seen things would be impossible and order would be disturbed. Reflect on how this affects daily life.
Sanusi mentions both worldly and religious matters ('المعاش والمعاد').
3
Connect to the broader framework
Recall that this is part of the inner perceptive powers. Ask yourself: Why is imagination classified separately from common sense?
This prepares you for the proofs that follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية: من القوى المدركة الباطنة ( الخيال وهو يحفظ الصور المرتسمة في الحس المشترك ) إذا غابت المحسوسات عن الحواس الظاهرة فهو ( كالخزانة له وبه يعرف من يرى ) في زمان ( ثم يغيب ثم يحضر ، ولولا هذه (القوة وحفظها لصور المحسوسات الغائبة ) لامتنع معرفته ) أي لامتنع أن يعرف من شيء أنه الذي رؤي فيما سبق من الزمان، ( واختل النظام) إذ يحتاج الإنسان حينئذ في كل ما يحس به أن يتعرف حاله في المرة الثانية وما بعدها كما في المرة الأولى فلا يتميز عنده الضار من النافع والصديق من العدو ويختل أمر المعاش والمعاد . ( وأثبت ) وجود الخيال ( بوجوه ثلاثة : الأول : قوة القبول غير قوة (الحفظ فمدرك الصور القابل لها أعني الحس المشترك غير حافظها الذي هو الخيال. (قلنا :) ما تمسكتم به ( هو فرع قولكم : الواحد لايصدر عنه إلا واحد ) وقد مر بطلانه . ( وإن سلم ) ذلك ( فالحفظ مشروط بالقبول ) بديهة فلا بد أن يجتمع القبول مع الحفظ ( فكيف تقول : القابل غير الحافظ ) البتة حتى يثبت أن مدرك المحسوسات يجب أن يكون مغايراً لما يحفظها ؟ (الثاني: الحس المشترك حاكم على المحسوسات كما سلف (دونها ) أي دون القوة الخالية لأن فعلها الحفظ ولا شك أن ما ليس بحاكم مغاير لما هو حاكم. (قلنا: ) يجوز أن يكون هناك قوة واحدة قد تحكم تارة ولا تحكم أخرى) فلا يلزم إلا التغاير بالاعتبار دون الذات . ( الثالث : الصور) المحسوسة (إذا كانت ( مرتسمة ( في الحس المشترك فهي مشاهدة كما في المحسوسات الحاضرة عندنا (بخلاف ما إذا كانت ) مرتسمة ( في الخيال) فإنها ليست كذلك. كما إذا غابت المحسوسات عنا فلا بد من تغير القوتين بحسب الذات (قلنا : قد يعود ما ذكرتم من الاختلاف بالمشاهدة وعدمها ) إلى ملاحظة النفس وعدمها بأن تكون الصورة مرتسمة في قوة واحدة فتارة تلتفت النفس إليها فتشاهدها وتارة تعرض عنها فلا تشاهدها.
The second of the inner perceptive powers is (imagination, and it preserves the forms imprinted in common sense) when sensible objects are absent from the external senses, so it is (like a treasury for it, and by it one knows a person seen) at one time (then absent, then present. Were it not for this) power and its preservation of images of absent sensibles, (knowledge of him would be impossible) i.e., it would be impossible to know of a thing that it is what was seen previously, (and order would be disturbed) because then a person would need in every sensation to reacquire knowledge of its state in the second time as in the first, so harmful and beneficial, friend and foe would not be distinguished, and the matters of livelihood and afterlife would be disturbed. (And they proved) the existence of imagination (by three ways: First: the power of reception is not the power of preservation, so the perceiver of forms receptive to them, i.e., common sense, is not their preserver, which is imagination. (We said:) what you have adhered to (is a branch of your statement: the one does not issue from it except one) and its falsehood has passed. (And if that is granted), (then preservation is conditioned by reception) by intuition, so reception and preservation must coexist. (So how can you say: the receptive is not the preserver) at all, so that it is proven that the perceiver of sensibles must be different from what preserves them? (Second: common sense judges sensibles as previously, (not it) i.e., not the imaginative power, because its act is preservation, and there is no doubt that what is not a judge is different from what is a judge. (We said:) it is possible that there is one power that sometimes judges and sometimes does not judge), so only difference in consideration is necessary, not in essence. (Third: sensible images) (if they are imprinted in common sense, they are observed as with present sensibles at us, (unlike if they are) imprinted (in imagination) for they are not so. As when sensibles are absent from us, so the two powers must differ in essence. (We said:) the difference you mentioned (regarding observation and its absence) may revert to the psyche's attention and its absence, such that the image is imprinted in one power, then sometimes the psyche turns to it and observes it, and sometimes turns away from it and does not observe it.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage defines imagination as the power that preserves forms from common sense when external objects are absent. It explains the necessity of this power for recognizing previously seen things and maintaining order in life. Three proofs are given for its existence as a distinct power: First, reception and preservation are different acts, but the response shows this relies on the rejected principle 'from one only one proceeds' and that preservation requires reception, so they can be in one power. Second, common sense judges while imagination preserves, but the response allows one power to have different acts at different times. Third, images in common sense are observed while in imagination they are not, but the response attributes this difference to the psyche's attention, not to a distinct power. Each proof is presented with a concise counter-argument.

Key Terms
الخيال
الحس المشترك
القوة الحافظة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think imagination is merely a weaker form of common sense.
Correction: The passage argues imagination is a distinct power with a separate function: preservation, not perception. Common sense receives forms, imagination stores them.

Checkpoint

A) It judges sensory data.
B) It preserves forms when external objects are absent.
C) It combines and separates forms.
D) It perceives abstract meanings.

Key Takeaway
Imagination is the inner power that stores sensory forms, enabling recognition and order in life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fail to recognize someone you've met before, do you consider the spiritual consequences of a weak imagination? Sanusi warns that without imagination, distinguishing friend from foe becomes impossible. How might your own lapses in recognizing what truly benefits or harms you be linked to not preserving the forms of knowledge you've gained? What practical steps can you take to strengthen your inner preservation of truths from this lesson?
?
The passage says that without imagination, 'the matters of livelihood and afterlife would be disturbed.' Think of one instance where a failure to remember a past experience (a form preserved by imagination) led you to a wrong decision. How could a more disciplined imagination have saved you from that error? Commit to one habit that will help you retain the forms of beneficial knowledge from your studies.
?
Sanusi uses the analogy of a treasury for imagination. What treasures are you storing in your imagination? Are you filling it with the forms of worldly distractions or with the truths that lead to recognition of the Divine? This lesson shows that imagination is not neutral—it directly impacts your ability to discern truth from falsehood in both this life and the next. What are you actively preserving in your imagination today?

First Proof: Reception vs. Preservation
Sanusi presents the first proof and its response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof for the existence of imagination as a distinct power based on the difference between reception and preservation, and the response that refutes it.

Core Idea
Reception and preservation can coexist in one power; they need not be separate.

Learners will now encounter the first of three proofs that imagination must be a distinct power. The proof argues that since reception and preservation are different acts, they must belong to different powers. The response challenges this by pointing out that preservation depends on reception, so they can be in one power, and the argument relies on a previously refuted principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and response
Read the Arabic passage for the first proof. Identify the claim and the reasoning given.
The proof starts with 'الأول : قوة القبول غير قوة الحفظ'.
2
Analyze the opponent's argument
The proof says: the power that receives forms (common sense) is not the same as the power that preserves them (imagination). Understand why they think these must be distinct.
Their assumption is reminiscent of the principle 'from one only one proceeds'.
3
Examine the response
The response has two parts: first, the proof relies on a false principle; second, even if granted, preservation requires reception, so they can coexist. Work through the logic.
The response uses 'فالحفظ مشروط بالقبول' (preservation is conditioned by reception).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
