( وأثبت ) وجود الخيال ( بوجوه ثلاثة : الأول : قوة القبول غير قوة (الحفظ فمدرك الصور القابل لها أعني الحس المشترك غير حافظها الذي هو الخيال. (قلنا :) ما تمسكتم به ( هو فرع قولكم : الواحد لايصدر عنه إلا واحد ) وقد مر بطلانه . ( وإن سلم ) ذلك ( فالحفظ مشروط بالقبول ) بديهة فلا بد أن يجتمع القبول مع الحفظ ( فكيف تقول : القابل غير الحافظ ) البتة حتى يثبت أن مدرك المحسوسات يجب أن يكون مغايراً لما يحفظها ؟ (الثاني: الحس المشترك حاكم على المحسوسات كما سلف (دونها ) أي دون القوة الخالية لأن فعلها الحفظ ولا شك أن ما ليس بحاكم مغاير لما هو حاكم. (قلنا: ) يجوز أن يكون هناك قوة واحدة قد تحكم تارة ولا تحكم أخرى) فلا يلزم إلا التغاير بالاعتبار دون الذات . ( الثالث : الصور) المحسوسة (إذا كانت ( مرتسمة ( في الحس المشترك فهي مشاهدة كما في المحسوسات الحاضرة عندنا (بخلاف ما إذا كانت ) مرتسمة ( في الخيال) فإنها ليست كذلك. كما إذا غابت المحسوسات عنا فلا بد من تغير القوتين بحسب الذات (قلنا : قد يعود ما ذكرتم من الاختلاف بالمشاهدة وعدمها ) إلى ملاحظة النفس وعدمها بأن تكون الصورة مرتسمة في قوة واحدة فتارة تلتفت النفس إليها فتشاهدها وتارة تعرض عنها فلا تشاهدها.
The second of the inner perceptive powers is (imagination, and it preserves the forms imprinted in common sense) when sensible objects are absent from the external senses, so it is (like a treasury for it, and by it one knows a person seen) at one time (then absent, then present. Were it not for this) power and its preservation of images of absent sensibles, (knowledge of him would be impossible) i.e., it would be impossible to know of a thing that it is what was seen previously, (and order would be disturbed) because then a person would need in every sensation to reacquire knowledge of its state in the second time as in the first, so harmful and beneficial, friend and foe would not be distinguished, and the matters of livelihood and afterlife would be disturbed. (And they proved) the existence of imagination (by three ways: First: the power of reception is not the power of preservation, so the perceiver of forms receptive to them, i.e., common sense, is not their preserver, which is imagination. (We said:) what you have adhered to (is a branch of your statement: the one does not issue from it except one) and its falsehood has passed. (And if that is granted), (then preservation is conditioned by reception) by intuition, so reception and preservation must coexist. (So how can you say: the receptive is not the preserver) at all, so that it is proven that the perceiver of sensibles must be different from what preserves them? (Second: common sense judges sensibles as previously, (not it) i.e., not the imaginative power, because its act is preservation, and there is no doubt that what is not a judge is different from what is a judge. (We said:) it is possible that there is one power that sometimes judges and sometimes does not judge), so only difference in consideration is necessary, not in essence. (Third: sensible images) (if they are imprinted in common sense, they are observed as with present sensibles at us, (unlike if they are) imprinted (in imagination) for they are not so. As when sensibles are absent from us, so the two powers must differ in essence. (We said:) the difference you mentioned (regarding observation and its absence) may revert to the psyche's attention and its absence, such that the image is imprinted in one power, then sometimes the psyche turns to it and observes it, and sometimes turns away from it and does not observe it.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage defines imagination as the power that preserves forms from common sense when external objects are absent. It explains the necessity of this power for recognizing previously seen things and maintaining order in life. Three proofs are given for its existence as a distinct power: First, reception and preservation are different acts, but the response shows this relies on the rejected principle 'from one only one proceeds' and that preservation requires reception, so they can be in one power. Second, common sense judges while imagination preserves, but the response allows one power to have different acts at different times. Third, images in common sense are observed while in imagination they are not, but the response attributes this difference to the psyche's attention, not to a distinct power. Each proof is presented with a concise counter-argument.

Key Terms
الخيال
الحس المشترك
القوة الحافظة
القبول
الحفظ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because two acts are different, they must belong to different powers.
Correction: The response shows that different acts can belong to one power if one act is a condition for the other, as preservation depends on reception.

Checkpoint
What is the main flaw the response identifies in the opponent's first proof?
A) It assumes imagination is unnecessary.
B) It relies on a previously refuted principle.
C) It confuses common sense with imagination.
D) It fails to define imagination properly.

Key Takeaway
The first proof that imagination is distinct fails because its reasoning depends on a false principle, and preservation actually depends on reception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a profound truth about Allah, do you quickly forget it because you treat it as mere information to be received rather than a treasure to be preserved? How can you change that today?
?
Sanusi argues that preservation depends on reception. In your spiritual life, have you prioritized receiving knowledge over preserving and acting upon it? What would it cost you to become a preserver of truth rather than just a receiver?
?
The proof assumes that different actions require different powers. But your heart can both receive and preserve the same truth. Why do you sometimes treat your faith as if it were fragmented into separate compartments? Is that a failure of understanding or a failure of tawheed?

Second Proof: Common Sense as Judge vs. Imagination as Preserver
Sanusi presents the second proof and its response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof for imagination's distinctness, which contrasts the judging role of common sense with the preserving role of imagination, and the response that allows one power to have multiple functions.

Core Idea
A single power can both judge and preserve at different times.

The second proof argues that since common sense judges sensibles and imagination only preserves them, they must be different powers. The response counters by suggesting that one power can perform different acts at different times, so the difference is merely conceptual, not essential.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof
Read the passage. Note the claim: common sense is a judge, imagination is not; therefore they are different.
The proof uses the fact that 'الحس المشترك حاكم' (common sense judges) while imagination only preserves.
2
Understand the response
The response says it is possible that one power sometimes judges and sometimes does not. This means the difference is in consideration, not in essence.
This is a common move in kalam: distinguishing between essential and accidental attributes.
3
Evaluate the argument
Think about whether the response is convincing. If one power can have multiple functions, then the proof does not establish two distinct powers.
This response avoids the need for a separate imaginative power.

Source Text

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This lesson teaches that one power can fulfill different functions at different times. How does that change the way you view your own consistency and character? Will you now trust that Allah, who designed this unity in you, can unite all perfections in Himself?
?
The proof in this lesson tries to separate common sense and imagination based on their different acts. But the response shows that difference in act does not necessitate difference in essence. In your own life, are you quick to judge others based on their actions alone, assuming that a single bad act reveals an evil essence? How can this lesson correct your tendency to make hasty judgments about people's character?
?
The response uses the concept of 'consideration' (iʿtibār) to resolve the proof. In your daily life, do you confuse conceptual distinctions with real separations? For example, do you treat your anger and patience as two different people inside you? How does this lesson call you to a more integrated view of yourself, where you accept that you can hold seemingly opposite qualities in one soul?

Third Proof: Observation vs. Non-Observation of Images
Sanusi presents the third proof and its response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof for imagination's distinctness based on whether images are observed or not, and the response that attributes the difference to the psyche's attention rather than separate powers.

Core Idea
Observation depends on the psyche's attention, not on a different power.

The third proof argues that images in common sense are observed (when sensibles are present) while images in imagination are not observed (when sensibles are absent), so the two powers must differ. The response replies that the difference in observation can be explained by the psyche either attending to or ignoring the image, which resides in a single power.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof
Read the passage. Note the claim: images in common sense are observed, but in imagination they are not, hence the powers are different.
This proof relies on direct experience: when you see something, the image is present and observed; when you imagine it, it is not observed in the same way.
2
Understand the response
The response suggests that observation depends on the psyche (al-nafs) paying attention. The image exists in one power, but the psyche may turn to it or away.
This is a psychological explanation: attention determines observation.
3
Evaluate the strength of the response
Consider whether this explanation is plausible. Does it satisfactorily eliminate the need for two distinct powers?
This response reduces the evidence to a subjective factor (attention) rather than objective difference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Will you commit to catching yourself when your attention drifts and deliberately turning your psyche toward what is before you?
?
The lesson teaches that the same image can be observed or not depending solely on whether the psyche attends to it. Consider the 'images' you carry in your mind — resentments, fears, desires. Are you aware that you can choose which ones to attend to? Which image have you been feeding without realizing it, and what would it mean to withdraw your attention from it today?

The Estimative Power: Definition and Examples
Sanusi defines the estimative power and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the estimative power is and the type of meanings it perceives.

Core Idea
The estimative power perceives particular meanings like enmity and love.

Sanusi defines a third internal power: the estimative power. Unlike the rational soul, it grasps particular meanings attached to sensory forms. Two vivid examples—a sheep's perception of enmity from a wolf and a lamb's love for its mother—illustrate this power's domain.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic definition and translation. Note that the estimative power perceives particular meanings (معاني جزئية).
2
Examine the examples
Notice the examples: the sheep perceives enmity from the wolf, the lamb perceives love from its mother. These are particular meanings, not universal concepts.
The wolf is a sensible form; the enmity is a meaning attached to it.
3
Understand the necessity
The passage argues that such meanings require a distinct power apart from the rational soul. Ask yourself why the rational soul cannot perceive them directly.
Rational soul deals with universals, not particulars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة : ( من تلك القوى هي (القوة الوهمية وهي التي تدرك المعاني الجزئية ) المتعلقة بالصور المحسوسة ( كالعداوة ) الجزئية ( التي تدركها الشاة من الذئب ) فتهرب منه ( والمحبة ) الجزئية ( التي تدركها السخلة من أمها ) فتميل إليها . فإن هذه المعاني لا بد لها من قوة مدركة سوى الناطقة . قالوا : ( وهي التي تحكم بأن هذا الأصغر) هو ( هذا الحلو ) ويتجه عليه أن النسبة التي بينهما وإن كانت معنى جزئياً مدركاً للقوة الوهمية إلا أن طرفيها محسوسان ومدركان بالحس المشترك والحكم لا بد أن يدرك الطرفين والنسبة حتى يتمكن من الحكم عليها فلا يجوز أن يكون الحكم المذكور للقوة الوهمية ولا للحس المشترك.
The third of those powers is (the estimative power, and it is what perceives particular meanings) related to sensible forms (like the particular enmity) (which the sheep perceives from the wolf) and so flees from it (and the particular love) (which the lamb perceives from its mother) and so inclines towards it. For these meanings necessarily require a perceptive power other than the rational. They said: (And it is that which judges that this smaller) is (this sweet). And there is an objection against it: that the relation between them, even if it is a particular meaning perceived by the estimative power, yet its two endpoints are sensible and perceived by common sense, and judgment necessarily perceives both endpoints and the relation in order to be able to judge it, so it is not permissible that the mentioned judgment be for the estimative power nor for common sense.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage defines the estimative power (al-wahmiyya) as the power that perceives particular meanings attached to sensible forms, such as the enmity a sheep perceives from a wolf or the love a lamb perceives from its mother. It argues that such meanings require a power distinct from the rational soul. Then it presents an objection: when this power judges that 'this small thing is this sweet thing,' the judgment involves a relation between two sensible endpoints. The objection says that judgment requires awareness of both endpoints and the relation, which cannot be done solely by the estimative power (since it perceives only meanings) nor by common sense (since it perceives only particulars). The objection is left without an explicit answer in this passage, suggesting the issue is open or will be addressed later.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: The text specifically states it perceives particular meanings (المعاني الجزئية), not universals.

B) Particular meanings attached to sensible forms

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

An Objection to Estimative Power's Judgments
Sanusi presents an objection regarding the estimative power's judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a key objection to the estimative power's ability to make relational judgments.

Core Idea
Judgments like 'this is that' require perceiving both endpoints and the relation.

After defining the estimative power, Sanusi presents an objection. The power is said to judge that 'this smaller is this sweet.' The objection argues that such a judgment involves a relation between two sensible endpoints, which neither the estimative power nor common sense can adequately grasp alone.

Guided Path
1
Identify the judgment
The objection concerns the judgment 'this smaller is this sweet.' Read the Arabic and translation for this sentence.
2
Analyze the objection
The objection states that the relation (النسبة) between the two endpoints is a particular meaning perceived by estimative power, but the endpoints themselves are sensible and perceived by common sense. The judgment requires awareness of both endpoints and the relation.
This raises a question: which faculty performs the judgment?
3
Consider the implications
The objection concludes that neither the estimative power nor common sense alone can account for the judgment, leaving the matter unresolved in this passage.
The author may provide an answer later.

Memory and Compositive Imagination
Sanusi describes memory, compositive imagination, and answers an objection about estimative use.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of memory and compositive imagination, how they relate to the estimative power and intellect, and the significance of subjugating the compositive imagination to the intellect.

Core Idea
The compositive imagination is a tool used by both intellect and estimative power—subjugating it to intellect is a great victory.

Sanusi continues his enumeration of the inner powers. He describes memory (al-ḥāfiẓa) as a treasury for the meanings perceived by the estimative power, and compositive imagination (al-mutakhayyila) as the power that combines and separates forms and meanings. An objection arises: how can the estimative power use compositive imagination for sensible forms if it doesn't perceive them? The answer reveals a mirror-like reflection among inner powers, with the estimative power as their sultan. The lesson concludes with a powerful exhortation: subjugating this power to the intellect leads to great victory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for definitions
Read the Arabic passage carefully and its translation. Identify the two powers: memory (al-ḥāfiẓa) and compositive imagination (al-mutakhayyila). Note how each is defined.
Memory is a treasury for meanings perceived by the estimative power; compositive imagination disposes of sensible forms and meanings.
2
Understand the relational analogy
Notice the analogy: memory's relation to the estimative power is like imagination's relation to common sense. This highlights memory's role as a storehouse.
Also note the dual naming of compositive imagination: it is called thinking power (mufakkirat) when used by intellect, and compositive imagination when used by estimative power.
3
Analyze the objection and answer
Read the objection: how can the estimative power use compositive imagination for sensible forms if it doesn't perceive them? Understand the answer: inner powers reflect like mirrors; the estimative power is the sultan and has authority.
The conclusion emphasizes the victory of subjugating this power to the rational soul.

The fourth of them is (the memory, and it is the preserver of the meanings that the estimative power perceives, like a treasury for it, and its relation to the estimative power is like the relation of imagination to common sense. So it was dispensed in proving it (by what we mentioned. Commentary
This passage introduces two powers: memory (al-ḥāfiẓa) which preserves the meanings perceived by the estimative power, and compositive imagination (al-mutakhayyila) which combines and separates forms and meanings. The compositive imagination is also called the thinking power (mufakkirat) when used by the intellect, and simply compositive imagination when used by the estimative power. An objection asks how the estimative power can use it for sensible forms when it does not perceive them directly. The answer explains that the inner powers reflect each other like mirrors; the estimative power has authority over them and can use them as instruments, and can even contend with the intellect. Subjugating this power to the intellect is considered a great victory.

The Five Inner Powers: Proof and Linguistic Note
Sanusi concludes with an argument for inner powers and a linguistic note.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the five inner powers are inferred from the multiplicity of their acts and the principle that from one only one proceeds, and you will learn a linguistic clarification of terms used in the discussion.

Core Idea
The five inner powers are proven through their distinct acts, relying on the principle that one cause produces only one effect.

In this concluding research, Sanusi brings together the argument for the five inner powers. He explains that these powers are recognized not through direct observation but through the multiplicity of five specific acts, and he notes the underlying principle that has been previously criticized. Finally, a marginal note clarifies the pronunciation and meaning of some words used earlier.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage includes both the main argument and a marginal note.
2
Identify the five acts
The passage lists the acts that prove the five inner powers. Find them and note how they correspond to different cognitive functions.
The acts are perception of sensibles, perception of particular meanings, preservation of both, and disposal in both – but preserve two distinct powers.
3
Understand the principle
The argument relies on the principle 'from one only one proceeds'. Understand why this principle is needed to infer multiple powers from multiple acts.
The author notes this principle has been criticized earlier.
4
Review the marginal note
The marginal note clarifies the pronunciation and meaning of the word 'muzarrad' and other terms. This is a linguistic aside.

Four Divisions of Bodies and Five Possibilities
Author enumerates four divisions of bodies and five possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical classification of bodies into four divisions and five possibilities regarding their createdness or eternity, and which position is affirmed as the truth.

Core Idea
Bodies are either originated or eternal in essence and attributes – four combinations exhaust all logical possibilities.

In this passage, the author sets the stage for proving that bodies are originated by first laying out all logical possibilities. He identifies four divisions based on whether the essence and attributes are originated or eternal, and then notes five epistemological possibilities: affirming any one of the four or remaining undecided. Understanding this classification is crucial for following the subsequent proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the symmetrical structure of the four divisions.
2
Identify the four divisions
Find the four combinations of originated/eternal for essence and attributes. Write them down in order.
The fourth division is 'originated in essence, eternal in attributes' – the reverse of the third.
3
Understand the five possibilities
From the four divisions, the author derives five possibilities: affirming any one of the four, or hesitating. Note which one is identified as the truth.
The first possibility (originated in both) is affirmed by all religious communities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني في عوارض الأجسام وأحوالها وفيه مقاصد ثمانية. المقصد الأول: الأجسام المحدثة أربعة أقسام ] في أن الأجسام محدثة وضبط الكلام في هذا المقام أن يقال : ( إنها إما أن تكون محدثة بذواتها وصفاتها أو قديمة بذواتها وصفاتها، أو قديمة بذواتها محدثة بصفاتها أو بالعكس، فهذه أربعة أقسام مقيسة إلى نفس الأمر ( ثم إما أن نقول : بواحد منها أولا نقول بل نتردد ونتوقف فهذه خمسة احتمالات، الأول: إنها محدثة بذاتها الجوهرية ( وصفاتها العرضية ( وهو الحق وبه قال المليون : ( كلهم ( من المسلمين واليهود والنصارى والمجوس
The Second Observatory on the accidents of bodies and their states. It contains eight aims. The First Aim: Bodies are originated, four divisions. Regarding that bodies are originated, the control of speech in this station is that it is said: (They are either originated in their essences and attributes, or eternal in their essences and attributes, or eternal in their essences and originated in their attributes, or the reverse. So these are four divisions measured against the thing itself. Then either we affirm one of them, or we do not affirm but hesitate and pause; so these are five possibilities. The first: They are originated in their substantial essence and their accidental attributes, and it is the truth, and the adherents of the religions (all of them) – Muslims, Jews, Christians, and Magians – said it.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage introduces the Second Observatory of the work, which deals with the accidents of bodies. The author states that this observatory contains eight aims (maqāṣid), and begins with the First Aim: that bodies are originated (muḥdatha). He then provides a precise formulation of the discussion by classifying the logical possibilities regarding the createdness or eternity of bodies with respect to their essences (dhāt) and attributes (ṣifāt). There are four divisions: (1) originated in both essence and attributes, (2) eternal in both, (3) eternal in essence but originated in attributes, (4) eternal in attributes but originated in essence. These are measured against reality (nafs al-amr). From these four, five possibilities arise: affirming any one of the four, or hesitating without affirmation. The first possibility – that bodies are originated in their substantial essence and accidental attributes – is identified as the truth, and it is the position of all religious communities (al-milliyyūn): Muslims, Jews, Christians, and Magians. The passage sets up the framework for the detailed discussion that follows.

الأجسام المحدثة
ذوات

أربعة أقسام
خمسة احتمالات
المليون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The four divisions represent actual existing categories of bodies in the world.
Correction: The four divisions are logical possibilities (maqīsah ilā nafs al-amr) that exhaust the logical space, not necessarily categories of actual bodies. The author is setting up a proof by elimination.

Checkpoint

A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The first possibility — originated in both essence and attributes — is the truth affirmed by all religious communities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the logical options about the nature of reality, do you accept the truth simply because it is the majority view, or have you personally examined each alternative? In what areas of your faith are you content to follow consensus rather than seeking certainty?
?
The author presents a rigorous logical framework. How often do you subject your own beliefs to such systematic analysis? Are you willing to list all possibilities about an issue before concluding? What prevents you from doing so?
?

What does your ability (or inability) to explain this say about the depth of your understanding? Commit today to learning one argument in such a way that you can teach it to someone else.

Thesis of Immaterial Souls and First Proof Begins
States the thesis of soul's immateriality and begins the first proof from intellection of simples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the thesis that human souls are immaterial and the beginning of the first proof that appeals to the soul's apprehension of simple realities.

Core Idea
The soul's ability to grasp simple realities shows it cannot be physical.

In this passage, the author introduces the second major topic: human souls. He states the core thesis that souls are immaterial — not bodies or powers in matter — and notes that this view has supporters and opponents. Then he begins the first of five proofs: the soul apprehends simple realities, which requires that it itself be simple and immaterial. The argument is incomplete here, but we can follow its structure so far.

Guided Path
1
Read the thesis and background
Read the opening lines where the author defines the nature of the soul as immaterial and names its supporters and opponents.
Notice that the author states the view of philosophers and the agreement of some Muslim thinkers.
2
Identify the first proof's starting point
Notice that the proof begins with the claim that the soul apprehends the simple. Distinguish the two sub-arguments: (1) the soul must apprehend simples if they are simple or composite, and (2) the locus of the simple must be immaterial, otherwise it would divide.
The argument is structured as 'first' and 'second' points.
3
Reflect on the incomplete ending
The text breaks off mid-argument. Ask yourself: Why would a physical locus cause division of the simple form? What is the intended conclusion?
This proof is continued in later sections.

Source Text

المقصد الثاني : في النفوس الإنسانية
( في أن النفوس الإنسانية مجردة) أي (ليست) قوة (جسمانية) حالة في المادة ) ولا جسماً) بل هي لامكانية لا تقبل إشارة حسية ( وإنما تعلقها بالبدن تعلق التدبير والتصرف من غير أن تكون داخلة فيه بالجزئية أو الحلول (هذا مذهب الفلاسفة) المشهورين من المتقدمين والمتأخرين ( ووافقهم على ذلك من المسلمين الغزالي والراغب ) وجمع من الصوفية المكاشفين ( وخالفهم فيه الجمهور بناء على ما مر من نفي المجردات على الإطلاق ) عقولاً كانت أو نفوساً (احتجوا) أي المثبتون لتجريدها (بوجوه ) خمسة : الأول : أنها تعقل البسيط الذي لا فتكون مجردة أما الأول فلانها تعقل حقيقة ما من الحقائق أي معنى المعاني ( فإن كانت تلك الحقيقة بسيطة فذاك ) أي ثبت المطلوب أعني تعقلها للبسيط ( وإلا كانت تلك الحقيقة ( مركبة من البسائط ) بالفعل لأن الكثرة متناهية كانت أو غير متناهية يجب فيها الواحد بالفعل لأنه مبدؤها ( وتعقل الكل بعد تعقل أجزائه ) بالضرورة لا يقال : هذا إذا كان الكل معقولاً بالكنه فإن تعقله يستلزم تعقل شيء من أجزائه لأنا نقول كلامنا في ذلك الوجه المعقول فإن كان بسيطاً فذاك وإن كان مركباً كان له بسائط كل واحد بالفعل. ( وأما الثاني ) وهو أنها إذا تعلقت بالبسيط كانت مجردة ( فلان محل البسيط لو كان جسماً أو جسمانياً ) أي لو كان ذا وضع أصالة أو تبعاً ( لكان منقسماً وانقسام المحل يوجب انقسام الحال بوجه ما لا
Second objective: On human souls.
(That human souls are immaterial) i.e., they are not a physical power existing in matter, nor a body, but rather are non-spatial (lā makāniyya) not admitting sensory indication. Their attachment to the body is the attachment of management and disposition, without being inside it as a part or by indwelling. This is the doctrine of the famous philosophers, ancient and modern. Al-Ghazālī and al-Rāghib and a group of unveiling Sufis agreed with them. The majority disagreed with them based on what was previously mentioned about the absolute denial of immaterial entities, whether intellects or souls. (They argued) i.e., those affirming their immateriality, (with five arguments): First: That it apprehends the simple (basīṭ) and then becomes immaterial. As for the first: because it apprehends the reality of realities, i.e., the meaning of meanings. If that reality is simple, then that is (the required: its apprehension of the simple). Otherwise, that reality is composed of simples actually, because multiplicity, whether finite or infinite, requires an actual one as its principle. And apprehension of the whole after apprehension of its parts is necessary. One should not say: this is when the whole is known in essence, for its apprehension entails apprehension of something of its parts; because we say: our discourse concerns that intelligible aspect: if it is simple, then that; if it is composite, it has simples each actually one. (As for the second) which is that when it is related to the simple, it is immaterial: because the locus of the simple, if it were a body or bodily (i.e., having position primarily or secondarily), it would be divisible, and division of the locus necessitates division of the accident in some way, not...
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage introduces the second objective (al-maqṣad al-thānī) of the third observation: on human souls. The author states the thesis that human souls are immaterial (mujarrada): they are neither physical powers inherent in matter nor bodies, but are non-spatial and manage the body without being spatially inside it. He notes that this is the position of the famous philosophers, supported by some Muslim thinkers (al-Ghazālī, al-Rāghib, and Sufis), while the majority of theologians denied it, rejecting all immaterial entities. The supporters of immateriality present five proofs (wujūh). The first proof is incomplete in this text but begins: the soul apprehends simple realities. The argument proceeds: if the apprehended reality is simple, that proves the soul apprehends the simple; if it is composite, it is composed of actual simples, and the soul must first apprehend those simples. The second part of the proof (still incomplete) argues that the locus of a simple form cannot be physical, because a physical locus would be divisible, and division of the locus would result in division of the form in some way. The passage ends abruptly mid-argument.

Key Takeaway
The soul's capacity to grasp simple, indivisible realities indicates that it itself is not divisible or corporeal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The soul can grasp simple, indivisible realities. When you reflect on Allah's oneness or your own mortality, do you push past the distractions of composite thoughts and emotions to reach that simple, direct awareness? What is one simple truth about your relationship with Allah that you have been avoiding facing?
?
This lesson says the soul is not inside the body as a part or by indwelling. Are you living as though your true self is this physical form, or as though you are an immaterial steward entrusted with a body? What would change today if you truly believed your soul is not of this world?

What Is Kalam?

Aristotle's Argument for Soul's Origination
Aristotle's argument for soul's origination and refutation of reincarnation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Aristotle's argument that the soul originates from the Active Intellect upon the body's occurrence, and how this refutes reincarnation.

Core Idea
The soul's origination requires a body, and reincarnation would cause two souls in one body.

Aristotle presents a causal argument: the soul flows from the Active Intellect when the body is ready, and this necessarily leads to one soul per body. This refutes reincarnation because if a body already had a transmigrating soul, a new soul would also attach, resulting in two souls.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for soul's origination
Read the first part of the passage to find what condition is necessary for the soul to originate from the Active Intellect.
The condition is the origination of the body, which serves as the receptive potential.
2
Understand the emanation process
Note that when the body occurs, a soul necessarily flows from the active principle due to the generality of emanation and the presence of the receptive potential.
3
Grasp the refutation of reincarnation
The passage argues that if reincarnation were sound, a body would have both a transmigrating soul and a newly emanated soul, which is impossible.
This is presented as a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

العدد في جوانب العالم لتتعلق بها تلك النفوس المفارقة عن أبدانها فلو كان تعلق النفوس على طريقة التناسخ لزم تعطل بعضها إلى أن يحدث بدن تتعلق به (وليس شيء منها والأظهر منهما أي من هذين الطريقين الآخرين (يصلح للتعويل) إذ لا نسلم لزوم التذكر لأحوالها في البدن السابق لجواز كونه مشروطا بالتعلق به على أنه قد نقل عن بعضهم أنه قال : إني لا تذكر كوني في صورة الجمل. ولا نسلم أن عدد أبدان الحيوانات الصغيرة والكبيرة في البحور والبراري لا يساوي عدد تلك النفوس المفارقة ) وعلى أصل الدليل الذي أبطل به التناسخ ( اعتراضات تعرفها إن كان ما مهدنا لك من الأصول على ذكر منك فلا نعيدها حذرا من الإطناب ) مثل أن يقال The need for a condition, so that the effect does not lag behind its complete cause. (For the origination of the soul) from the bestowing principle (a condition is the origination of the body) because it is the receptive potential for its management and disposal. (So when the body occurs, a soul flows upon it from the bestowing principle by necessity of the generality of emanation and the existence of the receptive potential. And with this he refuted reincarnation, where he said: If reincarnation is sound, (then when a body occurs, a transmigrating soul attaches to it and another soul flows upon it) that occurred now (due to what we mentioned of the attainment of the effective cause with its complete condition, so the single body would have two souls, and that is necessarily false, for everyone finds that his soul is one. And know that this statement of Aristotle about the origination of the soul and the refutation of reincarnation is (a clear circle, for he demonstrated the origination of the soul by the necessity of reincarnation) on the assumption of its pre-eternity (and its refutation, then he demonstrated the refutation of reincarnation by the origination of the soul. And that would be valid for him only if he demonstrated one of them by another way, such as what is said in refuting reincarnation: that remembrance of its states in the other body is necessary, or that the preparedness of bodies for souls and their becoming) i.e., the origination of souls (on one pattern) is uniform, for whenever a body becomes prepared, a soul occurs, unlike the separation of souls) with the origination of bodies (since a plague may occur) i.e., corruption of air (or a catastrophe) i.e., an eradicating event like the flood (or a general slaughter in which souls are destroyed) at once (that which is known by necessity that in that time the like of that amount of bodies did not occur contrary to habit), as it is reported that a war occurred in the land of Ionia, and in one day two hundred thousand from both sides were killed. And it is known that on that day bodies of this number did not occur in parts of the world for those separated souls to attach to their bodies. So if attachment of souls were by way of transmigration, some of them would necessarily remain idle until a body occurs for them to attach to (and there is nothing from them. And the more evident of the two i.e., of these two other ways (is fit for reliance) for we do not grant the necessity of remembrance of its states in the previous body, because it is possible that it is conditioned by attachment to it, and it is reported from some of them that he said: I do not remember being in the form of a camel. And we do not grant that the number of bodies of small and large animals in seas and wildernesses equals the number of those separated souls.) And upon the principle of the proof by which he refuted reincarnation (there are objections that you know if what we prepared for you of the principles is in your memory, so we do not repeat them to avoid prolixity) such as it is said: We do not grant that every originated thing must have an originated condition, for the free agent can specify originated things to their times without there being a motivator, and this is not entailed by the necessitating cause. We grant it, but we do not grant that the condition for the origination of the soul is the body; why may it not have another condition? We grant it, but we do not grant that when a body occurs, a soul must flow upon it; that would be necessary only if no transmigrating soul attaches to it. And it may be said: He meant by the principle of the proof what Aristotle mentioned on the origination of the soul, for it is the principle of his proof of the refutation of reincarnation.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents Aristotle's argument for the origination of the soul and the refutation of reincarnation, followed by criticisms and alternative considerations. It begins by stating that the origination of the soul from the Active Intellect requires a condition: the origination of the body, which is the receptive potential for the soul's management. When the body occurs, a soul necessarily flows upon it due to the generality of emanation and the presence of the receptive potential. This refutes reincarnation because if a body already had a transmigrating soul, a new soul would also flow upon it, resulting in two souls for one body, which is necessarily false. The passage then notes that this argument is circular, as Aristotle proves the soul's origination by the necessity of reincarnation (if souls were pre-eternal) and then refutes reincarnation by the origination of souls. Two alternative ways to refute reincarnation are suggested: (1) the transmigrating soul would remember past states, (2) the constant preparedness of bodies for souls versus sudden mass deaths disproves transmigration. Both are criticized: remembrance is not necessary, and the number of animal bodies is sufficient. Finally, further objections against the original proof are listed: the need for an originated condition is denied, the condition could be other than the body, and an occurring body does not necessitate a soul if a transmigrating soul is already attached.

المبدأ الفياض
التناسخ
القابل المستعد
العلة التامة

Key Takeaway
Aristotle's argument against reincarnation hinges on the principle that a single body cannot have two souls, which would result if a transmigrating soul were added to a newly emanated one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does that principle challenge the way you view others – especially those who seem different from you? The Circularity Objection to Aristotle's Argument
Criticism of Aristotle's argument as circular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will recognize how the author identifies a circularity in Aristotle's demonstration of the soul's origination and refutation of reincarnation.

Core Idea
Aristotle's proof is circular: he uses reincarnation to prove origination, then origination to refute reincarnation.

The passage points out that Aristotle's reasoning contains a logical circle. He tries to prove the soul's origination by arguing that otherwise reincarnation would be necessary, and then uses the soul's origination to refute reincarnation. This circularity weakens his argument unless he provides an independent proof for one of the claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two directions of the circle
Locate where the author states that Aristotle proves origination by reincarnation, and then refutes reincarnation by origination.
The circle is described as 'clear' (دور صريح).
2
Understand why this is problematic
A circular argument does not provide independent justification; each conclusion depends on the other.
3
Consider the condition for breaking the circle
The author says the circle would be avoided if Aristotle had demonstrated one of the two claims by a different method.
This sets up the next part where alternative arguments are considered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And we do not grant that the number of bodies of small and large animals in seas and wildernesses equals the number of those separated souls.) And upon the principle of the proof by which he refuted reincarnation (there are objections that you know if what we prepared for you of the principles is in your memory, so we do not repeat them to avoid prolixity) such as it is said: We do not grant that every originated thing must have an originated condition, for the free agent can specify originated things to their times without there being a motivator, and this is not entailed by the necessitating cause. We grant it, but we do not grant that the condition for the origination of the soul is the body; why may it not have another condition? We grant it, but we do not grant that when a body occurs, a soul must flow upon it; that would be necessary only if no transmigrating soul attaches to it. And it may be said: He meant by the principle of the proof what Aristotle mentioned on the origination of the soul, for it is the principle of his proof of the refutation of reincarnation.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents Aristotle's argument for the origination of the soul and the refutation of reincarnation, followed by criticisms and alternative considerations. It begins by stating that the origination of the soul from the Active Intellect requires a condition: the origination of the body, which is the receptive potential for the soul's management. When the body occurs, a soul necessarily flows upon it due to the generality of emanation and the presence of the receptive potential. This refutes reincarnation because if a body already had a transmigrating soul, a new soul would also flow upon it, resulting in two souls for one body, which is necessarily false. The passage then notes that this argument is circular, as Aristotle proves the soul's origination by the necessity of reincarnation (if souls were pre-eternal) and then refutes reincarnation by the origination of souls. Two alternative ways to refute reincarnation are suggested: (1) the transmigrating soul would remember past states, (2) the constant preparedness of bodies for souls versus sudden mass deaths disproves transmigration. Both are criticized: remembrance is not necessary, and the number of animal bodies is sufficient. Finally, further objections against the original proof are listed: the need for an originated condition is denied, the condition could be other than the body, and an occurring body does not necessitate a soul if a transmigrating soul is already attached.

Key Terms
الدور
التناسخ
حدوث النفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that a circular argument is automatically invalid, but in some contexts it can be part of a coherent system. However, here it is presented as a flaw because the argument is meant to be demonstrative.
Correction: The author's point is that Aristotle's specific argument is circular because it uses each conclusion as a premise for the other, which does not provide independent proof.

Checkpoint
Why is Aristotle's argument considered circular?
A) He proves the soul's origination by assuming reincarnation is false
B) He proves origination using reincarnation, then refutes reincarnation using origination
C) He uses the same term in two different senses
D) He relies on an unproven assumption

Key Takeaway
Aristotle's combined proof of soul's origination and refutation of reincarnation is circular, as each conclusion depends on the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider your certainty about the origin of your soul. Is your belief built on independent evidence, or does it rely on a chain of reasoning where one conclusion supports another in a circle? Trace your reasoning and see if any part depends on the very thing you are trying to prove.
?
When you defend the Islamic view of the soul's creation, are you using arguments that mutually depend on each other? How can you ensure each claim stands on its own, like a building with a solid foundation rather than a loop?
?
In your own life, have you ever accepted an argument simply because it confirmed what you already believed, without checking for logical pitfalls like circularity? How might this affect the strength of your faith and your ability to share it with others?

Alternative Arguments Against Reincarnation and Their Criticisms
Presentation and critique of two alternative refutations of reincarnation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two alternative ways to refute reincarnation (remembrance and preparedness) and the reasons they are considered unreliable.

Core Idea
Two alternative refutations of reincarnation are proposed but both are criticized as insufficient.

The passage suggests two independent ways to refute reincarnation: the necessity of remembering past lives, and the uniform preparedness of bodies versus sudden mass deaths. However, the author points out that these are not convincing, as remembrance may not occur and animal bodies could accommodate all transmigrating souls.

Guided Path
1
Learn the first alternative: remembrance argument
Understand the claim that a transmigrating soul should remember its past states in the previous body.
If the soul transmigrates, it should have memories of the previous life.
2
Learn the second alternative: preparedness argument
Understand the argument based on uniform preparedness of bodies versus sudden mass deaths.
Read the example of the Ionian war where many souls were released but no corresponding bodies were born.
3
Critique of the remembrance argument
The author objects that remembrance may be conditional on attachment to the body, so it is not necessary.
He also mentions a reported saying: 'I do not remember being in the form of a camel.'
4
Critique of the preparedness argument
The author denies that the number of animal bodies is insufficient to match the souls released in mass deaths.
Animal bodies in seas and wildernesses are countless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The memory argument against reincarnation fails because remembrance may depend on attachment to the new body. This shows how our intuitions can be misleading. Is there an area of your faith where you rely on a 'common sense' argument without deeper theological grounding? What will you do to strengthen that foundation?
?
The second argument fails because animal bodies are vast enough to accommodate all souls. This shows the complexity of refuting a false belief. How does this remind you that the truth of Islam is not always proven by simple, obvious arguments? What does that demand from you in your study of ʿaqīdah?

Further Objections to Aristotle's Original Proof
Listing objections to Aristotle's proof from other perspectives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be aware of three specific objections that can be raised against Aristotle's original argument for the soul's origination and refutation of reincarnation.

Core Idea
Aristotle's proof is challenged on the necessity of a condition, the identity of the condition, and the necessity of emanation.

After presenting his own criticisms, the author lists further objections against the original Aristotelian proof. These objections question key premises: whether every originated thing needs an originated condition, whether the body is the specific condition, and whether a body necessarily receives a new soul when it occurs.

Guided Path
1
First objection: Condition not necessarily originated
The objector denies that every originated thing requires an originated condition; the free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār) can specify times without a motivator.
This challenges the basis of the condition argument.
2
Second objection: Body may not be the condition
Even if a condition is needed, it might be something else, not the body.
3
Third objection: Body does not necessitate a new soul
If a transmigrating soul is already attached to the body, a new soul does not necessarily emanate.
This directly responds to the refutation of reincarnation.

Commentary
This passage presents Aristotle's argument for the origination of the soul and the refutation of reincarnation, followed by criticisms and alternative considerations. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that the objections are presented as definitive refutations, but they are listed as possible challenges that the reader should know from earlier principles.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the objections mentioned against Aristotle's proof?
A) That every originated thing requires an originated condition
B) That the condition might be something other than the body
C) That a body does not necessarily result in a new soul if a transmigrating soul is already attached
D) That the Active Intellect does not exist

Key Takeaway
The original Aristotelian proof is subject to at least three objections concerning the necessity, identity, and effect of the condition for the soul's origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if God is bound by your expectations of cause and effect, or as the absolutely free Agent who can act without any prior motivation? How does that affect your trust in His decree when things don't follow the patterns you expect?
?
If the soul's origin is not mechanically tied to the body, what does that mean for your understanding of your own existence? Are you just a product of physical conditions, or is there a deeper, freely willed divine act behind your soul?
?
The passage challenges the idea that a body necessarily receives a soul. Reflect: Have you ever assumed that certain 'conditions' automatically entitle you to something from God? What does this lesson teach you about emptying your heart of expectations and surrendering to His free will?

The Nature of the Soul's Attachment to the Body
Sanusi defines the soul's attachment to the body as a middle type, using analogies and explaining the soul's need for bodily instruments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the unique nature of the soul's attachment to the body as a middle type between weak and strong attachments, and how this attachment enables the soul's perfections.

Core Idea
The soul's attachment to the body is like a lover clinging to the beloved.

This passage from Sanusi defines the precise nature of the soul's relationship with the body. He rejects two extreme views—that it is merely accidental or that it is essential inherence—and presents instead a middle way: the soul uses the body as an instrument to acquire perfections. This sets the stage for understanding the soul's dependence on the body in this life.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of attachment
Read the passage and translation, noting how Sanusi distinguishes weak, strong, and middle attachments. Pay attention to the analogies he gives for each.
The weak attachment is like a body to its place; the strong like an accident to its substance.
2
Understand the middle attachment analogies
Focus on the analogy of the craftsman and his tools, and the lover's attachment. Why does Sanusi say the soul's attachment is like these?
The soul needs the body to act, just as a craftsman needs tools.
3
Trace the soul's need for the body
Follow the reasoning that the soul begins devoid of perfections and needs diverse bodily tools to acquire knowledge and virtues. This justifies the attachment.
Sensations lead to universal perceptions.
4
Examine the first attachment: the cardiac spirit
Sanusi explains that the soul first attaches to a subtle vapor in the heart. Consider the medical and philosophical basis for this claim.
This is the 'spirit' that carries power to the rest of the body.
5
Connect to the theological conclusion
The passage ends by attributing all powers to God directly. Reflect on how this fits into Kalam theology's emphasis on God's direct action.
No need to prove intermediate powers.

[ المقصد الرابع : تعلق النفس بالبدن ] تعلق النفس بالبدن) ليس تعلقاً ضعيفاً يسهل زواله بأدنى سبب مع بقاء المتعلق بحاله كتعلق الجسم بمكانه وإلا تمكنت النفس من مفارقة البدن بمجرد المشيئة من غير حاجة إلى أمر آخر وليس أيضاً تعلقاً في غاية القوة بحيث إذا زال التعلق بطل المتعلق مثل تعلق الأعراض والصور المادية بمحالها لما عرفت من أنها متجردة بذاتها غنية عما تحل فيه بل هو تعلق متوسط بين كتعلق الصانع بالآلات التي يحتاج إليها في أفعاله المختلفة، ومن ثمة قيل : هو ( تعلق العاشق بالمعشوق ) عشقاً جبلياً إلهامياً فلا ينقطع ما دام البدن صالحاً لأن تتعلق به النفس. الا يرى أنها تحبه ولا تمله مع طول الصحبة ولا تكره مفارقته وذلك لتوقف كمالاتها ولذاتها ) العقلية والحسية ( عليه ) فإنها في مبدأ خلقتها خالية عن الصفات الفاضلة كلها، فاحتاجت إلى آلات تعينها على اكتساب تلك الكمالات وإلى أن تكون تلك الآلات مختلفة، فيكون لها بحسب كل آلة فعل خاص حتى إذا حاولت فعلاً خاصاً كالإبصار مثلاً التفت إلى العين فتقوى على الإبصار التام، وكذا الحال في سائر الأفعال ولو اتحدت الآلة لاختلطت الأفعال ولم يحصل لها شيء منها على الكمال، وإذا حصلت لها الإحساسات توصلت منها إلى الإدراكات الكلية ونالت حظها من العلوم والأخلاق المرضية وترقت إلى لذاتها العقلية بعد احتظائها باللذات الحسية فتعلقها بالبدن على وجه التصرف والتدبير كتعلق العاشق في القوة بل أقوى منه بكثير، (و) إنما تتعلق من البدن ( أولاً بالروح القلبي المتكون في جوفه الأيسر من بخار الغذاء ولطيفه ) فإن القلب له تجويف في جانبه الأيسر ينجذب إليه لطيف الدم فيبخره بحرارته المفرطة، فذلك البخار هو المسمى بالروح عند الأطباء، وعرف كونه أول متعلق للنفس بأن شد الأعصاب يبطل قوى الحس والحركة مما وراء موضع الشد ولا يبطلها مما يلي جهة الدماغ وأيضا التجارب الطبية تشهد بذلك ( وتفيده ) أي تفيد النفس الروح بواسطة التعلق ( قوة بها تسري ( الروح ( إلى جميع البدن فتفيد ) الروح الحامل لتلك القوة ( كل عضو قوة بها نفعه من القوى التي فصلناها فيما قبل وهذا كله عندنا للقادر المختار ابتداء ولا حاجة إلى إثبات القوى ( كما مر مراراً . يتم
[ The Fourth Section: The Attachment of the Soul to the Body ] The attachment of the soul to the body is not a weak attachment easily removed by the slightest cause while the attached remains as it is, like the attachment of a body to its place, otherwise the soul would be able to separate from the body by mere will without need of anything else. Nor is it an extremely strong attachment such that when the attachment is removed the attached is annihilated, like the attachment of accidents and material forms to their loci, because you have known that they (souls) are abstract in themselves, independent of that in which they inhere. Rather it is a middle attachment between that of a craftsman to the tools he needs for his various actions. Hence it is said: it is (the attachment of the lover to the beloved) a natural, inspired love, so it does not cease as long as the body is suitable for the soul to attach to it. Do you not see that it loves it and does not tire of it despite long companionship, and does not dislike separating from it? That is because its perfections and pleasures (intellectual and sensory) depend on it. For at the beginning of its creation it is devoid of all virtuous qualities, so it needs tools to help it acquire those perfections, and these tools must be different, so that for each tool it has a specific act. When it attempts a specific act like sight, for example, it turns to the eye and becomes capable of perfect vision; likewise with other acts. If the tool were unified, the acts would be mixed and it would not attain any of them perfectly. When it acquires sensations, it proceeds from them to universal perceptions and obtains its share of sciences and praiseworthy morals, and ascends to its intellectual pleasures after being nourished by sensory pleasures. So its attachment to the body is by way of disposal and management, like the attachment of a lover in power, indeed much stronger. And it attaches to the body (first to the cardiac spirit formed in its left cavity from the vapor of food and its subtle part). For the heart has a cavity on its left side to which the subtle part of blood is attracted, and it vaporizes it with its excessive heat; that vapor is called the spirit by physicians. And it is known that it is the first attachment of the soul because tying the nerves annuls the powers of sense and motion beyond the place of tying but does not annul them on the side toward the brain; also medical experiments testify to that. And it (the soul) benefits it (the spirit) by means of the attachment (a power by which the spirit permeates the entire body, and the spirit carrying that power gives each organ a power by which it performs its function from the powers we detailed before. And all this, in our view, is from the Powerful, the Free Agent, in the beginning, and there is no need to prove the powers, as has been repeated many times. End.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of the soul's attachment to the body. It first distinguishes it from two extremes: it is not weak like a body's attachment to its place (otherwise the soul could leave at will), nor is it extremely strong like the inherence of accidents in matter (which would destroy the accident upon separation). Instead, it is a middle type, analogous to a craftsman's attachment to his tools. It is described as the attachment of a lover to the beloved—a natural, inspired love that persists as long as the body is suitable. The reason for this attachment is that the soul begins devoid of perfections and needs different bodily tools (senses and organs) to acquire knowledge and moral virtues. Through sensations it rises to universal intelligibles. The soul first attaches to the 'cardiac spirit' (the subtle vapor in the left heart cavity), which is the primary substrate, and from there the spirit carries a power to the whole body, enabling each organ's function. The passage concludes that this entire process is from the Free Agent (God) and does not require proving intermediate powers.

الروح القلبي
العشق الجبلي
المتعلق
الصور المادية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul's attachment to the body is like an accident inhering in a substance, so separation would annihilate it.
Correction: No, the soul's attachment is a middle type, not essential inherence; the soul is abstract and survives separation from the body.

Checkpoint

B) Weak, like a body in its place.
C) A middle attachment like that of a craftsman to his tools or a lover to the beloved.
D) Non-existent, as the soul is completely independent.

Key Takeaway
The soul's attachment to the body is an instrumental, lover-like relationship that allows the soul to acquire perfections, and it is sustained directly by God's power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Are you using your body as a tool to acquire spiritual perfections, or are you indulging it as an end in itself? ?

What would it mean for you to dedicate one of your bodily faculties purely to seeking knowledge of Allah this week?

Definition of the Intellect
The author defines the intellect and announces three objectives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the intellect as a simple substance independent from bodily instruments and why it is not a body or accident.

Core Idea
The intellect is an immaterial substance, not a body or accident, self-sufficient in action.

In this passage, the author begins the Fourth Observatory on the Intellect. He provides a precise definition that distinguishes the intellect from physical objects and accidents. This foundation is essential for later discussions on the intellect's role in knowledge and action.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key terms: possible existent, not a body, not an accident, simple substance.
Pay attention to the negations and the positive description.
2
Understand each negation
The intellect is not a body (jism), not an accident (hal), not a part. Why? Because it is a simple substance.
These negations clarify what the intellect is not.
3
Grasp the positive description
It is a simple substance (jawhar mujarrad) and independent in its activity (mustaghni fi fa'iliyyati 'ani al-alaat al-jismaniyya).
This positive description establishes its immaterial nature.
4
Note the structure
The author says this observatory contains three objectives (maqasid). This sets up the forthcoming sections.
This prepares us for the detailed discussions ahead.

المرصد الرابع في العقل ] والمراد به كما مر موجود ممكن ليس جسماً ولا حالاً فيه ولا جزءاً منه بل هو ذاته جوهر مجرد مستغن في فاعليته عن الآلات الجسمانية ( وفيه مقاصد ) ثلاثة .
The Fourth Observatory on the Intellect. By it is meant, as previously, an existent possible [entity] that is not a body, nor an accident inhering in it, nor a part of it; rather it is in its essence a simple substance, independent in its activity from bodily instruments. And it contains three objectives.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification. The author begins a new section, the Fourth Observatory (al-marsad al-rabi'), which treats the intellect (al-'aql). The definition states that the intellect is a possible existent (mawjud mumkin) — meaning it is not necessary of existence. It is not a body (jism), nor an accident (hal) inhering in a body, nor a part of a body. Instead, it is by itself a simple substance (jawhar mujarrad), meaning it is immaterial. It is self-sufficient in its activity, not needing bodily instruments. The author then informs that this observatory contains three objectives (maqasid), which will be explained in the following text.

First Three Rulings on the Intellects
Enumeration of first three rulings on intellects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first three rulings concerning the intellects: their lack of origination, their incorruptibility, and their singular species-individual relationship.

Core Idea
Intellects are neither originated nor corruptible, and each is its own species.

Sanusi begins the third objective by enumerating the rulings of the intellects. The first three establish their metaphysical status as simple, immaterial, and unique. They are not subject to generation or corruption, and each intellect is individuated by its essence alone.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style of each ruling.
2
Identify the three rulings
Locate each of the first three rulings in the text. Note how each is introduced with 'الأول', 'الثاني', 'الثالث'.
The rulings are clearly numbered.
3
Examine the reasoning for each
For each ruling, identify the reason given. For the first, the reason is that origination requires matter. For the second, corruption involves composition; intellects are simple. For the third, individuation is by essence.
The reasoning is based on the nature of matter and composition.
4
Reflect on implications
Think about why these rulings matter. They establish that intellects are beyond the physical world, not subject to time or change.
This connects to the broader kalam understanding of the celestial realm.

المقصد الثالث : في أحكام العقول ( في أحكام العقول وهي سبعة: الأول : إنها ليست حادثة لما تقدم أن الحدوث يستدعي مادة الثاني : ليست كائنة ولا فاسدة إذ ذاك عبارة عن ترك المادة صورة ولبسها صورة أخرى فلا يتصور إلا في المركب المشتمل على جهتي قبول وفعل ( وأما البسيط فلا يكون فيه جهتا قبول وفعل ) فلا تكون العقول لبساطتها فاسدة بل أبدية ( الثالث : نوع كل عقل منحصر في شخصه إذ تشخصه بماهيته وإلا لكان بالمادة وما يكتنفها كما تقدم الرابع: ذاتها جامعة لكمالاتها أي ما يمكن لها فهو حاصل ) بالفعل دائما ( وما ليس حاصلاً لها فهو غير ممكن لما علمت أن الحدوث يستدعي مادة يتجدد استعدادها بحركة دورية سرمدية فلا يتصور إلا في مادي هو تحت (الزمان ) والعقول مجردة غير زمانية . The Third Objective: On the Rulings of the Intellects. (On the rulings of the intellects, and they are seven: First: They are not originated, because it has preceded that origination requires matter. Second: They are neither generated nor corrupted, since that is the leaving of a form of matter and the donning of another form, so it can only be conceived in a composite that has two aspects of receptivity and act. As for the simple, there are no two aspects of receptivity and act in it; thus the intellects, due to their simplicity, are not corruptible but eternal. Third: The species of each intellect is confined to its individual, since its individuation is by its essence; otherwise it would be by matter and its accompaniments, as has preceded. Fourth: Their essence encompasses their perfections; i.e., whatever is possible for it is actually present, and whatever is not present for it is impossible, because you know that origination requires matter whose preparedness is renewed by perpetual circular motion, so it can only be conceived in a material thing that is under time, while the intellects are immaterial and not temporal. Fifth: They are intellective of themselves, since intellection is the presence of an essence stripped of foreign veils to a thing that is immaterial and subsistent by itself, and there is no doubt that their essence is present to themselves, for the presence of the essence is more general than the presence of the other and the non-other, and conceptual distinction is sufficient for the realization of presence. There is a consideration in this, because it is possible that the condition for intellection is the presence of an other essence, as in the senses; for sensation occurs only by obtaining an other form at the sense, not by obtaining a form absolutely, otherwise the senses would perceive their own external forms, which is false. Sixth: They know universals, and likewise every immaterial thing that subsists by itself knows universals, because every such immaterial thing can know, since its essence is free from attachments foreign to its quiddity and material impurities that prevent intellection; so its quiddity does not need an operation to make it intelligible. For if it does not know, it would be a faculty of the knower. So every immaterial thing in itself can know. And everything that can know can know together with another, because we know by necessity that there is no opposition in acts of intellection. So every intelligible can be known together with every other intelligible. Also, everything that is known is not free from the correctness of judging it by universal attributes like unity, possibility, etc., and judgment between two things requires their togetherness, so every intelligible can be known with another in general. Then it is possible for it, i.e., the immaterial thing, to be accompanied by the abstract essence, i.e., the essence of another in the mind, because intellection is the obtaining of the essence of the intelligible in the knower. So if the immaterial thing knows together with the essence of another, both are present in the mind, so each accompanies the other therein. So if it is possible for it to accompany the abstract essence of another in the mind, then it is also possible for it to accompany it absolutely, i.e., whether the immaterial thing is existent in the mind or in the external world, since its being, i.e., the obtaining of the essence of the immaterial thing in the mind, is not a condition for absolute accompaniment and its validity, because if it were a condition for absolute accompaniment and its validity, then its accompaniment to the mind, which is more specific than absolute accompaniment, would also be conditional upon its being in the mind, because the more specific must be conditional upon what the more general is conditional upon. Then a circularity would result, because the being of the essence of the immaterial thing in the mind is the very accompaniment to it which is conditional upon it. And if the being of the immaterial thing in the mind is not a condition for the accompaniment between it and the essence of another, then accompaniment between them is permissible when the immaterial thing is existent in the external world. And if accompaniment of the universal abstract essence of the other to it, i.e., the essence of the immaterial thing, is permissible, meaning the quiddity...
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of seven rulings (ḥukm) concerning the intellects (al-ʿuqūl). The author lists each ruling followed by a brief explanation. The first ruling states that the intellects are not originated (ḥāditha) because origination requires matter, which they lack. The second ruling states they are not subject to generation and corruption, as those processes involve composition of matter and form, but intellects are simple. The third ruling asserts that each intellect's species is confined to its individual; individuation is by essence, not matter. The fourth ruling explains that all potential perfections are actual in the intellects because they are immaterial and not subject to time. The fifth ruling argues that intellects are self-aware, but the author notes an objection: perhaps intellection requires an other essence, as in sensation. The sixth ruling begins by stating that intellects know universals. A long argument follows to show that all immaterial substances can know, and that they can know multiple things together without incompatibility. The argument considers conditions for accompaniment of essences in the mind. The passage is cut off mid-explanation of the sixth ruling.

Key Terms
الحدوث
الفساد
البساطة
التشخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking 'eternal' (abadiyya) implies co-eternity with God.
Correction: It actually refers to their lack of composition and temporal origination, not uncreatedness.

Checkpoint
According to the third ruling, how is each intellect individuated?
A) By its form and matter
B) By its essence (māhiyya)
C) By its temporal origination
D) By its relationship to the spheres

Key Takeaway
The first three rulings establish that intellects are immaterial, simple, and individuated by essence, free from the conditions of matter and time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your true self (intellect) is simple and not composed of matter, what does that say about your attachment to physical possessions and bodily desires? How can you live today as though your essence is not defined by what you own or how you look?
?
The intellects are individuated by their essence alone—not by matter or external circumstances. What does that imply about your own identity? Are you relying on worldly markers (job, status, relationships) to define who you are, or do you root your identity in something deeper and unchanging?
?
Since intellects are not subject to corruption and are eternal, what is the state of your soul right now? Are you investing in what is eternal within you, or are you consumed by what will perish? What one concrete step can you take today to align your focus with what is permanent?

Fourth and Fifth Rulings: Perfection and Self-Intellection
Explanation of fourth and fifth rulings on intellects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that intellects possess all possible perfections actually and that they are aware of themselves, along with an objection regarding the condition of intellection.

Core Idea
Intellects are fully actual and self-intellecting, with no potentiality or temporal change.

The fourth and fifth rulings further characterize the intellects as perfect and self-aware. The fourth ruling explains that all potential perfections are actual because they are immaterial. The fifth ruling asserts that they know themselves, but an objection is raised: perhaps intellection requires an object distinct from the knower, as in sensory perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the fourth and fifth rulings. Note the structure: each ruling is presented with its justification.
The fifth ruling includes an objection (nazar).
2
Understand the fourth ruling
The fourth ruling says the essence of each intellect encompasses all its perfections. The reason given is that any possibility not actual would require matter and time, which intellects lack.
This shows intellects are fully actual (bi'l-fiʿl dā'iman).
3
Understand the fifth ruling and its objection
The fifth ruling says intellects are intellective of themselves. Intellection is defined as presence of essence to an immaterial self-subsistent entity. The objection questions whether the presence of an other essence is required, as in sensation.
The author presents the objection without resolving it.
4
Reflect on the objection
Consider why the objection is important. If intellection always requires an other, then self-intellection would be impossible. The author notes that conceptual distinction suffices.
This is a key point in debates about divine self-knowledge.
