⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثالث : في أحكام العقول ( في أحكام العقول وهي سبعة: الأول : إنها ليست حادثة لما تقدم أن الحدوث يستدعي مادة الثاني : ليست كائنة ولا فاسدة إذ ذاك عبارة عن ترك المادة صورة ولبسها صورة أخرى فلا يتصور إلا في المركب المشتمل على جهتي قبول وفعل ( وأما البسيط فلا يكون فيه جهتا قبول وفعل ) فلا تكون العقول لبساطتها فاسدة بل أبدية ( الثالث : نوع كل عقل منحصر في شخصه إذ تشخصه بماهيته وإلا لكان بالمادة وما يكتنفها كما تقدم الرابع: ذاتها جامعة لكمالاتها أي ما يمكن لها فهو حاصل ) بالفعل دائما ( وما ليس حاصلاً لها فهو غير ممكن لما علمت أن الحدوث يستدعي مادة يتجدد استعدادها بحركة دورية سرمدية فلا يتصور إلا في مادي هو تحت (الزمان ) والعقول مجردة غير زمانية . ( الخامس : إنها عاقلة لذواتها إذ التعقل حضور الماهية المجردة عن الغواشي الغريبة ( عند الشيء ) المجرد القائم بذاته ولا شك أن ماهيتها حاضرة لذواتها فإن حضور الماهية أعم من حضور الماهية المغايرة وغير المغايرة ) والتغاير الاعتباري كاف في تحقق الحضور . ( وفيه نظر لجواز أن يكون شرط التعقل حضور الماهية المغايرة كما في الحواس ) فإن الإحساس إنما يكون بحصول صورة مغايرة عند الحاسة لا بحصول صورة مطلقاً وإلا كانت الحواس مدركة لصورها الخارجية وهو باطل السادس: إنها تعقل الكليات وكذا كل مجرد ) من المجردات القائمة بذواتها فإنه يعقل الكليات ( إذ كل (مجرد) كذلك ( يمكن أن يعقل ) لأن ذاته منزه عن العلائق الغريبة عن ماهيته والشوائب المادية المانعة عن التعقل فماهتيه لا تحتاج إلى عمل يعمل بها حتى تصير معقولة فإن لم تعقل كان جهة العاقل فكل مجرد فهو في حد نفسه يمكن أن يعقل ( وكل ما يمكن أن يعقل فيمكن أن يعقل مع غيره إذ ) نعلم بالضرورة أنه ( لا تضاد في التعقلات ) فكل معقول يمكن أن يعقل مع كل واحد من سائر المعقولات، وأيضا كل ما يعقل فإنه لا ينفك عن صحة الحكم عليه بالأمور العامة كالوحدة والإمكان وغيرهما، والحكم بين شيئين يستدعي تعلقهما معاً فكل معقول يمكن أن يعقل مع غيره في الجملة، وحينئذ (فيمكن أن يقارنه ) أي المجرد ( الماهية المجردة ) أي الماهية الكائنة التي ( للغير في العقل لأن التعقل عبارة عن حصول ماهية المعقول في العاقل، فإذا تعقل المجرد مع ماهية غيره كانا معا حاصلين في العقل فيكون كل منهما مقارنا للآخر فيه، فإذا أمكن أن يقارن ماهية الغير المجرد في العقل (فيمكن أيضاً أن يقارنها ) أي يقارن ماهية الغير ماهية المجرد ( مطلقاً ) أي سواء كان المجرد موجوداً في العقل أو في الخارج (إذ كونها ) أي حصول ماهية المجرد( في العقل ليس شرطاً للمقارنة) المطلقة وصحتها ( لأنه لو كان شرطاً للمقارنة على الإطلاق وصحتها ( لكان مقارنته ) أي مقارنة المجرد (للعقل) التي هي أخص من مطلق المقارنة مشروطة) أيضاً ( بكونها ) أي بكون ماهية المجرد ( في العقل) لأن الأخص لا بد أن يكون مشروطاً بما شرط به الأعم (و) حينئذ ( يلزم الدور) لأن كون ماهية المجرد في العقل هو عين مقارنته له المشروط به وإذا لم يكن كون المجرد في العقل شرطاً للمقارنة بينه وبين ماهية الغير جازت المقارنة بينهما إذا كان المجرد موجوداً في الخارج ( وإذا جاز مقارنة ) الماهية الكلية ( المجردة ) التي للغير ( إياها ) يعني ماهية
The Third Objective: On the Rulings of the Intellects. (On the rulings of the intellects, and they are seven: First: They are not originated, because it has preceded that origination requires matter. Second: They are neither generated nor corrupted, since that is the leaving of a form of matter and the donning of another form, so it can only be conceived in a composite that has two aspects of receptivity and act. As for the simple, there are no two aspects of receptivity and act in it; thus the intellects, due to their simplicity, are not corruptible but eternal. Third: The species of each intellect is confined to its individual, since its individuation is by its essence; otherwise it would be by matter and its accompaniments, as has preceded. Fourth: Their essence encompasses their perfections; i.e., whatever is possible for it is actually present, and whatever is not present for it is impossible, because you know that origination requires matter whose preparedness is renewed by perpetual circular motion, so it can only be conceived in a material thing that is under time, while the intellects are immaterial and not temporal. Fifth: They are intellective of themselves, since intellection is the presence of an essence stripped of foreign veils to a thing that is immaterial and subsistent by itself, and there is no doubt that their essence is present to themselves, for the presence of the essence is more general than the presence of the other and the non-other, and conceptual distinction is sufficient for the realization of presence. There is a consideration in this, because it is possible that the condition for intellection is the presence of an other essence, as in the senses; for sensation occurs only by obtaining an other form at the sense, not by obtaining a form absolutely, otherwise the senses would perceive their own external forms, which is false. Sixth: They know universals, and likewise every immaterial thing that subsists by itself knows universals, because every such immaterial thing can know, since its essence is free from attachments foreign to its quiddity and material impurities that prevent intellection; so its quiddity does not need an operation to make it intelligible. For if it does not know, it would be a faculty of the knower. So every immaterial thing in itself can know. And everything that can know can know together with another, because we know by necessity that there is no opposition in acts of intellection. So every intelligible can be known together with every other intelligible. Also, everything that is known is not free from the correctness of judging it by universal attributes like unity, possibility, etc., and judgment between two things requires their togetherness, so every intelligible can be known with another in general. Then it is possible for it, i.e., the immaterial thing, to be accompanied by the abstract essence, i.e., the essence of another in the mind, because intellection is the obtaining of the essence of the intelligible in the knower. So if the immaterial thing knows together with the essence of another, both are present in the mind, so each accompanies the other therein. So if it is possible for it to accompany the abstract essence of another in the mind, then it is also possible for it to accompany it absolutely, i.e., whether the immaterial thing is existent in the mind or in the external world, since its being, i.e., the obtaining of the essence of the immaterial thing in the mind, is not a condition for absolute accompaniment and its validity, because if it were a condition for absolute accompaniment and its validity, then its accompaniment to the mind, which is more specific than absolute accompaniment, would also be conditional upon its being in the mind, because the more specific must be conditional upon what the more general is conditional upon. Then a circularity would result, because the being of the essence of the immaterial thing in the mind is the very accompaniment to it which is conditional upon it. And if the being of the immaterial thing in the mind is not a condition for the accompaniment between it and the essence of another, then accompaniment between them is permissible when the immaterial thing is existent in the external world. And if accompaniment of the universal abstract essence of the other to it, i.e., the essence of the immaterial thing, is permissible, meaning the quiddity...
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of seven rulings (ḥukm) concerning the intellects (al-ʿuqūl). The author lists each ruling followed by a brief explanation. The first ruling states that the intellects are not originated (ḥāditha) because origination requires matter, which they lack. The second ruling states they are not subject to generation and corruption, as those processes involve composition of matter and form, but intellects are simple. The third ruling asserts that each intellect's species is confined to its individual; individuation is by essence, not matter. The fourth ruling explains that all potential perfections are actual in the intellects because they are immaterial and not subject to time. The fifth ruling argues that intellects are self-aware, but the author notes an objection: perhaps intellection requires an other essence, as in sensation. The sixth ruling begins by stating that intellects know universals. A long argument follows to show that all immaterial substances can know, and that they can know multiple things together without incompatibility. The argument considers conditions for accompaniment of essences in the mind. The passage is cut off mid-explanation of the sixth ruling.

التعقل
الغواشي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking self-intellection is impossible because knowledge requires a distinct object.
Correction: Conceptual distinction (taghāyur iʿtibārī) is sufficient for presence, as the author argues.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of intellection (taʿaqqul) according to the fifth ruling?
A) Acquisition of a new form
B) Presence of an essence stripped of foreign veils to an immaterial thing
C) Comparison of concepts
D) Sensory perception

Key Takeaway
Intellects are fully actual and self-knowing, but the objection about the necessity of an other object of intellection remains a point of consideration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The intellect knows itself without needing an external object. Do you know yourself honestly, or do you rely on others' opinions to define who you are? What foreign veils (ghawāshī) are you allowing to obscure your own essence? Commit today to one concrete action that strips away a veil of self-deception.
?
If intellects are fully actual with no potentiality, they have no excuses for not knowing or being perfect. You, however, are a temporal being with potential. What potential are you leaving unactualized out of laziness or fear? Identify one specific potential you have neglected and a step you will take this week to actualize it.
?
The objection in the passage suggests that knowledge might require an other. Do you find it easier to know others than to know yourself? Why? What would it take for you to turn your gaze inward and seek self-knowledge with the same urgency you seek external validation? Resolve to spend 10 minutes today in silent self-reflection, asking: 'Who am I apart from my roles and relationships?'

Sixth Ruling: Intellects Know Universals
Argument for knowledge of universals and simultaneous intellection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth ruling that intellects know universals, and the argument that immaterial substances can know multiple things together without opposition.

Core Idea
Immaterial beings can know universals and can know them together with others.

The sixth ruling states that intellects know universals (kulliyyāt). The author explains that any immaterial self-subsistent being can know because its essence is free from material impurities. Furthermore, it can know multiple things together because there is no opposition in acts of intellection. The argument extends to show that if an intellect knows another essence in the mind, it can accompany it absolutely, avoiding circularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the sixth ruling. Note the argument structure is more complex than previous rulings.
The passage is cut off at the end; the argument continues.
2
Understand the initial claim
The sixth ruling claims that intellects know universals, and similarly every immaterial substance does. The reason is that immateriality frees from material impediments to intellection.
The text says 'كل مجرد يمكن أن يعقل'.
3
Follow the argument for simultaneous knowledge
The author argues that if something can know, it can know with another because there is no opposition in intellections. This is supported by the fact that intelligibles can be judged together.
This is a key step: no tadād in taʿaqqulāt.
4
Examine the accompaniment argument
The author then considers whether the intellect must be in the mind to accompany another essence. He shows that if that were a condition, a circularity would result. Thus, accompaniment is possible whether the intellect is in the mind or not.
This argument uses conditional reasoning to avoid infinite regress.

Source Text

Second: They are neither generated nor corrupted, since that is the leaving of a form of matter and the donning of another form, so it can only be conceived in a composite that has two aspects of receptivity and act. The passage teaches that intellects can know together without conflict. Are you holding onto any belief that you fear contradicts another truth? How can you trust that all truth is harmonious and seek understanding without opposition?
?
If your immaterial soul can know universals, then you have a capacity for profound knowledge. Are you using that capacity to know the Creator, or are you satisfied with trivial, material knowledge? What will you change today to prioritize the knowledge that truly matters?
